Wurry M.—
About “ coloured hearing.” Are there any R.P. readers
who have their colour sense excited by sound ? do any
two find the same sound produce similar effects ? These
are some of ray experiences (but I can only get good
results in darkness and silence). Gon a piano is always
red, the red of venous blood. A-fiat is to me a rich
orange, like an Emperor mandarin among the dark -green
foliage of the tree. A is absolutely colourless —looks or
sounds like pure cold water ; but B-flat has to me no
colour at all. B-na;tural is the bluish colour of a knife-
blade ; -C-natural the yellow of Chartreuse jaune ; and
F-natural the bluish-gray of a sea-gull’s back. The inter-
vening notes have no colour, except E-flat, which at
times suggests some colour that I cannot define. Tome,
most men, when angry, speak in some shade of red,
though I know one (very passionate) with a dark-
grey voice. I have tried to reason that the relation-
ship of these sounds and colours is due to association, but
it won’t work out. As a youngster I had never seen
Chartreuse, but the first time I did 1 recognised the note
C. Bid anyone ever notice that nearly all barmaids have
dull brick-red laughs? Can anyone pick out a Sydney
parson with a voice the colour of the scum on dirty,
stagnant water—a sort of grey -green ?

THE BED PAGE.
The Australian in the Tropics.
Condensed from a paper read by Dr. Josbph Ahearnb, of
Townsville, N.Q., to the R.C. Congress at Sydney.
WHILE journeying in America some three
years ago I became acquainted with F.
Marion Crawford, and had the pleasure
of hearing him lecture on “Mr. Isaacs.”
His introduction was a rehearsal of the circum-
stances that led to his career as a novelist. The
first essential incident in the series was the picking
up of a book from a dust-covered heap, to place in
his knapsack as he was preparing to start on a
walking tour through the hills behind Rome. In
the guest-room of a country inn he found it
was an old Sanskrit Grammar that he had brought
for his holiday reading. There was no help for it,
and he became interested ; pursuing the study of
the language at the University later, he developed
into a Sanskrit scholar. One day a man was
wanted to go as a newspaper editor to Allahabad;
Marion Crawford was sought out, chosen, and,
despite his protest that he knew nothing of
journalism, was despatched there. Business took
him to Simla, where, at the hotel, he met the
fascinating person he has thinly disguised under
the name of “ Mr. Isaacs.” On leaving India, he
dined -with an uncle in New York, and told him,
after dinner, of his attractive acquaintance—the
Eastern gentleman. His uncle made him promise,
before their late-at-night parting, that he would
write the story—so his first novel was written
under the pressure of this undertaking, and the
rest followed.
Was it by accident that Sanskrit book was
packed in the valise? Mr. Crawford most cer-
tainly believes it to be otherwise. At one time
we both would have attributed the agency to
intangible Fate —now, thank God, we are both by
our Faith enabled to regard such an episode, and
those of a kindred type, as due to the direction of
an all-wise Personal Providence. Thus, in my
own case, through a succession of occurrences,
from the most unlikely beginning, I find myself,
in obedience to His Will, addressing the Austra-
lian Catholic Congress of the year 1900. This is
my justification.
* « * •
No one will spend time profitably here who does
not accept without faltering the truism that God
rules the universe. “And God said let there be
lights in the firmament, to divide the days from
the night, and let these be for signs, and for
seasons, and for 1 days and years.”
“And let them be for lights in the firmament
of the Heaven to give light upon the Earth ; and
it was so.”
And it is so—because, fortunately, Man is
powerless to amend the movements of the sun and
moon.
He made Man, and “sent him forth from the
Garden of Eden to till the ground from whence
he was taken.”
What was His intention ? Saint Paul tells us
in his Athenian speech : “He hath made of one
blood all nations of men for to dwell upon all the
face of the earth, and hath determined the times
before appointed and the bounds of their habita-
tion.
Being an intelligent God, He equipped Man so
that he should be suitable to His environment.
Accordingly, He designed that men should vary
in color and shades of temperament to fitly
occupy the multiple regions of the globe.
• * • *
Look at a map of the earth’s surface between
the tropical lines Cancer and Capricorn, the belt
known to everyone as the Tropics. It includes
Siam, Borneo, Java, Hindostan, Mid Africa,
West Indies, the Panama Isthmus, and the Can-
nibal Islands. It almost needs an apology to tell
you that the inhabitants of these countries are
black. We have inside this area, also, the
Northern part of Queensland, jutting into the
Arafura Sea; close neighbor to New Guinea, on
the level of Fiji. The aboriginal owners are
black —very black—but a white race is meantime
in occupation : it is of this people I would speak.
Are they in the “ bounds of their habitation ” ?
If not, what penalties are they paying for stepping
over Nature’s boundary ?
I propose to deal with the inexorable exactions
under three heads. This confidence of mine is
meant as assurance of brief infliction, to avoid an
experience a bishop once had in. the House of
Lords. He rose and began by saying he intended
to divide his speech into twelve heads. . . Lord
Durham thereupon got up and begged leave to
interpose for a few minutes to tell the House an
anecdote. He was returning home, he said, a few
nights before; and passed St. Paul’s Cathedral
just before midnight. As he did so there was a
drunken man trying to see the time. Just then
the clock began to strike the hour and slowly
tolled out twelve. The drunken man listened,
looked hard at the clock, and said, “ Damn you !
why couldn’t you have said all that at once ? ”
At this relation the bishop condensed his re-
marks.
• » » *
Well, then, the White Race in North Queens-
land is undergoing modification physically, men-
tally, and morally. Physically —in a decided
narrowing of chest capacity and of the trunk
generally. In less decided though definite ten-
dency to disproportionate elongation.
I may be permitted to use an extract from a
paper I read in 1896 before the Australasian Asso-
ciation for the Advancement of Science. It is a
comparison of measurements between boys on the
North Queensland coast and boys of similar ages
in England.
Average Ohest-girth of
Average Ohest-girth of Boys in North Queens-
English Boys land (data supplied
(Maclaren’s Tables). by Head-Teachers
of Schools).
At 10 years is 25-1 inches At 10 years is 24 inches.
At 11 „ 26$ „ At 11 „ 24$ „
At 12 „ 27$ „ At 12 „ 25$

At 14 „ 29$ „ At 14 „ 27 „
At 15 „ 30| „ At 15 „ 28 „
The usual exception turned up in a healthy place called
Bowen, where a boy of 14 measured 31$ inches around
the chest.
So much for the width —now for the height:
When the arms, are placed horizontally during
moderate expiration, the circumference imme-
diately under the nipples and the angles of the
shoulder blades should be equal to half the length
of the body. However, in North Queensland I
find that the mean length of the bodies of 58
boys exceeds the mean circumference of their
chests by 1.15-16 in.: that is to say, in North
Queensland a boy is two inches taller than he
ought to be. What of that? Ah ! In these
days of scientific economy, it is hydraulic waste :
it is pumping a column of fluid two inches higher
than was normally intended, 70 times per minute
for every 24 hours year after year, until the pump
is prematurely worn out, if accident does not
throw it violently out of gear. '
And what is the effect of a narrow chest ? A
narrow ohest means limited lung space. It is
easy to conceive this, if one remembers that
Nature does not exert herself unnecessarily in any
direction. A large share in the elimination of
bumt-up material from the body is performed
through the skin and the lungs. In a cold climate
the skin is obviously very moderately used as com-
pared with its use in the tropics ; hence in a cold
climate the lungs are called upon to be ventilators
and energetic eliminators of noxious gases. The
continued automatic action enlarges the chest
space of the growing child —which, of course, re-
mains capacious when the adult frame-work is
completed, provided disease does not interfere.
On the other hand, when the skin acts perspir-
ingly, and to all extents continuously, such lung
space is not needed and the chest proportion must
essentially be moderate.
This must affect an individual’s suitability for
cosmopolitan residence and occupation. While
the Englishman is furnished to undergo the trials
of the tropics, the tropical white native is not
framed for residence in the harsher countries.
Indeed, in the latitude he has made his own, the
tropical resident of some years standing possesses
less endurance than his fellow workman imported
from a more bracing locality.
I remember making an official visit to report on
the sanitary state of a camp in the neighborhood
of a big engineering work which was being carried
out by Southern contractors. One of the firm told
me he had employed local men at the beginning of
the operations, and for a time, a short time only,
matters progressed with satisfaction. The work
was hard, and gradually, one after another, the
hands retired, admitting they needed a spell.
Substitutes from the Southern colonies were
brought to fill their places. This was not a ques-
tion of wages—it was simply a case of getting
work done in a certain time; and local men, I
was. assured, failed to support the necessary
strain.
This experience can be reasonably believed from
what I have said. When the lungs are freely
used, quick oxygenation is proceeding, tissue
change is accomplished rapidly, and good red
blood courses speedily through the channels of the
system.
The elongation, the length without propor-
tionate width, weakens the back; the spine is
indifferently buttressed ;it gives; curves. This is,
perhaps, more the case amongst girls, who have
not the same amount of athletic exercise as boys.
So, with weaker body muscles to sustain them,
girls relieve the vertebral weight imposed on its
pelvic base by instinctively stooping, crouching
forward, making a concavity of the chest, which
of itself is predisposed to become hollow. Of
course, this injury is fostered by the senseless
parents who put their children’s feet in shoes;
and later, the girl exaggerates her deformity when
she struts the pavement in the high-heeled shoe
of the French coquette. Unattractive, ugly, this
gait which silly woman voluntarily assumes. The
poor Chinese female has to submit to severe
distortion of her feet, although the infant voice
pityingly protests with rending cries against the
cruelty. After all, it is only the feet that are
squeezed out of shape in China; but your girls,
your sisters, your wives throw the body out of its
natural axis and so dispose it to a train of bad
consequences. Thus it is quite plain to understand
how and why the Registrar-General of Queens-
land, in his vital statistics for 1898, has the
melancholy statement to report—“By far the
most fatal (cause of death) to child life was
enteritis; the next was premature birth.” He
remarks —“This is more properly speaking a
cause of death rather than disease.”
Give me the carriage of the aboriginal woman,
who, shoeless in every land, fulfils the familiar
words of the Nature-loving poet, “ Vera incessu
pxtuit dea.” This amendment of the framework,
with its reduction in space, is of more serious im-
port to the female than to the stronger sex. The
vital redress of the blood depends primarily on
the supply of oxygen, so that diminished lung
area, which is air surface, means impairment of
blood —in popular language, thinness of blood.
Our women therefore are pale, and suffer exhaus-
tion from the demands upon their vital energy.
And it requires no argument—surely statement is
enough to gain your adhesion to the proposition—
that a woman to be a mother should not be
anaemic, listless, jaded—offering, as somebody has
neatly said before me, the silent plea of a spirit-
less non-possumus. People who, in the South,
complain of the enervation induced by the op-
pressiveness of the two or three days that precede
a southerly buster, may try to imagine weeks,
months of such weather. Sick women lie swelter-
ing in hot beds, pale children make ghastly
attempts at play, men expose their perspiring
chests to feel the air they make by their own
motion, for often no movement is in the atmo-
sphere itself. Dense with moisture that one may
almost collect between the palms, this atmosphere
rests provokingly, while the great sun shines
drowsily through the haze, with a smile as of con-
scious power, directed to those helpless against
his mood. For seven months in the year, on the
coast, this is the true condition every day until
the sea breeze reaches the shore during the south-
east monsoons; and it is true of all day and all
night, when the winds come from the north and
the rainy season is established.
Is this a fair field for motherhood ?—for most of
our mothers are good natural women, who nourish
their babies to their own injury—risking, I might
almost say, forfeiting, thereby any attractive
freshness they might have had ; an attractiveness
which it is the lot of their more favoured dusky
sisters in the same latitudes to possess and retain
for a span.
The children, however, who live thrive in their
first years until the influence of climate overcomes
that of race.
To minimise the glare, and with instinct to pro-
tect the eyes from the fierce rays of light, we get
face and forehead wrinkles, making the young
look old and knowing.
We get freckles which are merely pigmentary
changes; some more, some less, according to the
degrees of exposure and individual susceptibility.
But individual susceptibility may be considered a
minor factor only, for all skins change to the expo-
sure, and all parts—the bared arms and chest of
the bushman, the uncovered legs of the beach-
comber, stain under the influence of the tropical
sun. I firmly believe that the coastal inhabitants,
if fenced off from benefiting by the introduction
of fresh material, would darken in color, or die out.
This is a suggestion which I do not present as a
sensational random opinion, and I offer you in
support a few comprehensive sentences that tell
very muoh in its favor.
Sir Joseph Fayrer, M.D., F.R.S., has spent the
greater part of his honourable life in distinguished
service in India. On the 30th May of this present
year he delivered to the Baleneological and Cli-
matological Society an address in which he states,
“ Past history shows, however, that colonisation
by the unmixed European race in the plains of
India is impracticable. In the very rare instances
in which the third generation has been reached it
had evidently attained its utmost desirable limits;
whilst to the Portuguese who preceded us, no
descendant unalloyed by native blood can be said
to exist.”
India lies between the Bth and 35th parallels of
North latitude ; North Queensland lies between
10 degrees and 21 degrees South. There may be
a special intervention of Providence to favor the
white North Queenslander. I doubt it.
Of course, we have trouble with our teeth, but
that is not altogether a consequence of
geographical distribution, nor indeed is the pro-
fession of dentistry a modern art. We, know that
in the luxurious period of the Roman Empire
teeth were stopped and bridging was done;
whereas the hardy Goths, living on simple food,
ignorant of refined methods in artificial feeding,
were not afflicted with “that hell o’ a’ diseases.”
• • • •
To advance to the mental effects. I deal with
these as they display themselves in temperament;
by temperament, I mean an inherent disposition
fostered by local influences such as led to the
making of cold tea in Boston Harbor, such as
created the bobbery over Clause 74 of the Com-
monwealth Bill a trifle over a century afterwards.
It is temperament that Cardinal Logue referred to
in Bradford the other day, when of Catholic
University claims he said: “There is stubborn
resistance on the part of the Orangemen ; in fact
they never reason on these questions in Ireland —
they fight them.” By the way, this valuable
characteristic to ours explains why English
generals, real generals, are always Irish ;—pug-
nacious, brilliant Irishman; stubborn, supercilious
Englishman, each has a temperament affirmative,
definite.
The Atlantic mists coming from the wide West,
driven by strong gales, do not linger tediously on
the Emerald Hills, they speed quickly through
the green valleys; the companionable sun peeps
out now and then with a quarter of an hour’s
smile on almost the worst kind of day. This rapid
movement, familiar to the native eye, impressing
the retina, may be causa causans of our volatile
methods. On the other hand the dense fog of the
Channels and the North Sea, whose scarcely
penetrable gloom invades Westminster and Down-
ing-street and the adjacent country, lasting for
days together, sets the mind stolid, is accountable
for introspection, has planted the habit of strug-
gling with dark complications, and patiently
striving with difficulties to overcome them.
The everlasting sun’s heat makes people selfish :
les faineants aiment leur aise. In the tropics
man, to be a nice man, should sit down under a
shady tree all day and eat fruit. Contrast the
pleasant, good-tempered Hawaiian, lover of
flowers, dilettante consumer of vegetables, with
theDyakof Borneo, who nervously rushes through
life hunting for heads ; or the equable Kanaka of
the New Hebrides with the restless Solomon
islander, who raids his neighbor to devour his
flesh. It is apparent—the stern, rapacious eager-
ness in these men-eaters, these tropical beef-eaters.
Good fighters. Yes ! cruel, quick, full of nerves.
So if we want endurance we must have beef. I
dare to say the pale clerk who eats meat twice a
day will out-fight, and out-last, and out-starve the
burly agricultural laborer, whose big thews and
sinews are mostly compounded of potatoes, corn,
and water. And as oUr climate resents activity,
we eat much beef to yield energy and defy climate.
We eat beef very young, and we get energy—
nerve energy ; and so we go to school early, very
early—voluntarily—our children love school with
an unnatural liking—it is morbid. Thus we enter
on the anxieties of life prematurely. What for ?
Education.
But is it good education her daughter has had,
when the best of English mothers tells one “ The
girls won’t do anything at home —I have to cook
and clean the brass-work, or it would not be done
well, if at all ” ? When an exemplary Irish widow
declares of her girls, “The natives are too lazy to
work, ” On the other hand, it is too much trouble
for the relaxed parents to exact obedience. ' With
the school anxiety and the sacrifice of mind and
muscle, we tire young.
The shopman is slow to serve, does not think of
suggesting alternative purchases, or recommending
what you don’t want —he wishes you hadn’t come
in, he longs for you to be gone. In fact, acute-
ness of early wear and tear weakens the nerve-
cells. Mind impressions are not distinctly
stamped—-receptivity is not assured. To draw a
parallel—-when one touches an electric button, if
the battery is well charged, and the connections
be well insulated, the click of the record is sharp,
and the indications definite. If the battery-fluid
be weak, or the insulation imperfect, or the disc
hinge slack, you do not wonder to have a blurred
register.
* * * *
But let us pass on from dwelling on this phase
to the consideration of our moral atmosphere.
Moral changes suggest to me the 42nd chapter
of Horrebow’s “Natural History of Iceland.”
The heading is “ Concerning Owls,” and the entire
contents of the chapter consist of this simple sen-
tence—“ There are no owls of any kind in , the
whole island.”
Now I do not wish to say we have no morals of
any kind. No! I mean to take a stand and de-
clare “North Queensland’s moral life is better
than prevails in N.S. Wales.”
That is my chapter, in which T imitate Horre-
bow’s briefness. However, I ask permission to
append a note, as it were—one often comes across
nearly a whole page of notes to a few lines of text.
Note: The femme detraquec is not encouraged
amongst us—the morally degenerate female does
not bloom in our society.
A school Sister enquiring after absentees, ask-
ing the children the reason of their absence, was
invariably answered that they were detained at
home to mind the babies. One day, being tired
of this repeated excuse, the Sister said, half in
fun, to a little girl, “Bother the babies!” The
child evidently brought the story home, for next
day the Sister had a long letter from the mother
in which, amongst other good things, she wrote :
“ I would have you know, Sister, that I am not
like some of our new-fashioned women who have
no babies, and dislike them, too. lam an old-
fashioned woman, and I believe in babies.’’
But, while admitting we are “ not too good ” (in
the present dreadful idiomatic vogue) all Australia,
in fact, might be a lot worse, notwithstanding the
critical chastisement of a candid visitor and the
admonitions of a prophet in our own country. No
need to be hysterical; for I read from the Regis-
trar-General of Queensland that in N.S.W. the
rate of illegitimacy was above that in Queensland ;
on the other hand the ratio for South Australia
was remarkably low. The figures are: —Queens-
land, 6'04, illegitimate births per cent. ; N.S.
Wales, 6'93 ;S. Australia, 3‘<>2. And, further, I
think it is as well we should know from the same
source that “Although the present rate of
illegitimacy is in excess of what formerly ob-
tained. it still compares well with most European
countries. According to Mnlhall, some of them
are as follows : England, 5'4 illegitimate births
per cent. ; Germany, B'7; France, 7'4; Sweden,
10 '2; Austria, 13’5.
I have been speaking particularly about the
coast country. On the N.Q. tableland, however,
the conditions are better. Although we have a
few exceptional elevations, they are not used as
in India for hill stations. These Fayrer describes
as “Resorts in which the European may preserve
his health and avoid the deterioration which in-
evitably results from protracted residence in the
plains—where indeed it seems, even possible that
lie may take root, thrive, and propagate his race,
and for which there are grounds for belief that
the prospects of success are not altogether un-
favorable, though so far no conclusive proof is
forthcoming.” Our Herberton district is high,
about 3000 feet ; there we have broad-chested,
rosy-cheeked children, and the languor of women
in a great measure disappears. The interior
averages perhaps only 1000 feet above sea-level.
It is even now obvious to the open-eyed observer
that the stalwart pioneers who drove the first
cattle and sheep on to the unsettled north country
are being succeeded by slimmer types of men.
And now, if J have made plain my own belief that
white people in the tropics are beyond “the
bounds of their habitations,” I trust I have in a
measure succeeded in justifying that belief.
It is well if it can be established in the minds of
dwellers in temperate Australia that the con-
ditions of life and climate in tropical Queensland
are special. It will be well if those facts, those
local conditions, be recognised and remembered by
the legislator of the Commonwealth, who will
serve the best interests of his country by preserv-;
ing the humility becoming to ignorance in matters
affecting purely tropical questions if he be par-
donably egotistical on all others. Perhaps I can
best illustrate my immediate purpose by telling a
ridiculous story probably altogether untrue. It is
one of the many told of President Lincoln in the
difficult days of the American War. He wanted
a Comlnander-in-Chief, and happening to be in
conversation with a friend on the subject of the
struggle, asked him if he had ever commanded an
army. “No! Mr. President,” was the reply.
“Do you think you could command an army?”
“I presume so, Mr. President; 1 know nothing to
the contrary.” He was appointed Commander-in-
Chief at once.
It should be the prayer of all of us that the gen-
tlemen soon to be commissioned by the Southern
constituencies will be temperate in their meddling
with tropical affairs. May they indulge the self-
confidence of Mr. Lincoln’s Commander-in-Chief
in fields where experiment and* crude theory can
be practised without endangering a national
industry by ill-timed radical disturbance. I do
not wish to touch on anything political. It is an
economic question of deep universal conoern to
the whole Australian nation—the question of how
work is to be done in the tropical plains and jun-
gles ; and that question will no doubt be raised in
the Federal Parliament. It merely occurred, to
me to make use of my. little effort in attempting
to draw a moral, as it were—a commonsense
moral.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is a hardship upon N.S.W. road-main-
tenance-men (writes a correspondent) that they
don’t sometimes get their pay until long after the
proper time, because the wages are paid through
the district superintendents, who are often away
from their centres when payment is due.
Twain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Which is truest and most sure:
Maiden of the fair allure,
Or the maid with tresses deep
Dyed in darkness and in sleep ?
* * * *
I kissed her rosy mouth at morning-light—
For she was sweet as love, and life, and sin—
I heard a soft voice crying in the night;
And, oh, I let the dark-veiled woman in.
* * * *
The woman I love is fair
As a flower at sunrise,
With heart of gold in her hair,
And soul of blue in her eyes.
Her breasts are as white as foam
Of the sea at morning is,
And sweet as a honeycomb
Her passionate scarlet kiss.
When Sorrow with face morose
Comes along with his comrade Care,
She hides me, and keeps me close,
Beneath the veil of her hair.
And 0, but that veil is fine,
And as fine as fine gold spun
By Dawn in her dream divine
When Earth is Bride of the Sun.
We are as bridegroom and bride,
Pure without stain or flaw,
A sword lies us beside—■
And who will that sword withdraw ?
She sweeps the stars away,
The bright stars of the North and South,
And turns years into a day
With kisses of her red mouth.
She is tender and mystical
As a lily beneath the moon,
And red as a rose withal,
And sings a magical rune.
Her beauty will never fade,
’Tis sweet as a rose is sweet;
I know she will see me laid
Under the Winding Sheet.
For ever, when she draws near,
And her arms around me are,
I see, in a vision clear,
A woman who stands afar.
She stands on a shore of calm
Where no wild waves ever beat •
Above her is a palm,
A stone is at her feet.
That woman is dark and pale,
And a strange dark tune she plays;
She wears on her head the veil
That no man living may raise.
I know that strange tune hath charms
To awake me and lead me away,
And lure me out of the arms
Of her whom I love some day.
Some day, under other skies,
Or these same—on land or foam—■
That tune I shall hear and rise
And follow, follow it—Home.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Loving-Cup.
Australia :
Drink hael, 0 mother ! Let lips be red
With a draught new-drawn from the veins of the
dead !
England*:
Waes hael, O daughter ! I pledge you a bowl
From the vintage we pressed in the land that we
stole!
Australia :
Drink hael, 0 mother ! I laugh in scorn
At the men we slew and the wives that mourn !.
England:
Waes hael, O daughter ! From me you drew
The spirit that mocks at the men we slew.
Australia :
Drink hael, 0 mother ! I’m drunk with blood ;
Let me bathe to the neck in the crimson flood.
England:
Waes hael, 0 daughter ! The world is wide ;
From the throats of the free shall flow the tide.
V.
ISHMAEL.
Backward.
We hoped our century of calm increase,
With all the aid of Industry and Art,
Had weaned us from the Old World ways apart,
And set for aye our feet in paths of peace !
That we, inheriting the Golden Fleece,
Uncursed by war, should on our journey start,
With name unsullied and with guiltless heart,
To bring the day when nation-strife shall cease !
But ’cross the waters from our kindred came
The noise of men fast arming for the fray—
Grand seemed the Gun, god-bright the Sabre-
gleam !
See in our heart the blood-lust leap and flame,
The slumbering savage wake again to slay !
And shattered lies our glowing, radiant
Dream.
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.

THE GOVERNOR AND* HIGH MASS.
“We are eon 3 of sturdy fathers, who wrung
the great charter from the craven John!'.’ declared
the mover of the resolution condemning the ap-
pearance of the Governor at High Mase in St.
Mary's Cathedral. And the five or six thousand
people In the hall cheered themselves 'hoarse.
Englishmen Question the acta of their Sovereign
or her representative slowly and reluctantly, but
when they do speak their words have greater
weight, contended the speaker. He denied that
the protest was an act of disloyalty, and as be
called attention to the battles of religious factions
fought by our fathers, when the faith of Protes-
tants —their faith —emerged triumphant, so thati
the Kings and Queens of England swore to defend
it at their Coronation, wave after wave of ap-
plause swept up from the thousands of people
below him, and his worde, “We are profoundly
and intensely loyal to her Majesty, and profoundly
and Intelligently respectful to any gentleman who
represents her, but we claim the right to protest
when he misrepresents his royal mistress and our
beloved and honored sovereign." called forth the
enthusiastic approval of his hearers.
The meeting decided to send the resolution com-
plaining of the Governor’s action to Downing-
street.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will caref ully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraph •,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1900.
A Policy for the Commonwealth.—The
Federal Tariff.
Next year it will be necessary to make a
Customs tariff for all Australia, and there-
fore the whole tariff question will come up
for review as a matter of necessity. Conse-
quently, the subject will be argued all over
again, and in view of this opportunity a
great rally of the Foreign-trade party is
already being organised, and the old brass
god is being specially scoured and burnished
for the occasion. A new weekly paper,
decorated with most lamentable illustra-
tions, most of them showing a partly-dressed
female with large feet (understood to be
Britannia), has been started to meet the
emergency, and much irrelevant and bare
assertion is published therein. The Foreign-
trade party is once more bellowing its two
old war-cries, which are : “ Look at Eng-
land, and see the wondrous prosperity
that arises through Freetrade,” and
“Look at Victoria, and see the
awful disasters that arise through
Protection.” And these two war-whoops
certainly do a great deal of service. Very
many people don’t look at all ; they simply
take things for granted. A few do look,
and note the fact that the United Kingdom
of Great Britain and Ireland is the richest
country in Europe, has the most commerce,
the most manufactures, and the most of
some other things, and are convinced
thereby that Freetrade must be best. And
yet these matters, in themselves, have no-
thing at all to do with the subject in dispute.
It is a primitive kind of baby logic that only
looks at one end of a case and takes no heed
of the other end. Britain is admittedly
ahead of many Protectionist countries in
many respects. And if Britain is farther
nhead of them than she was when she aban-
doned Protection then it may be fairly claimed
that Freetrade has a good case. But if
Britain, though still ahead, is not so
far in front as she was when she left
off Protection then Freetrade has no
case worth mentioning. If, on the whole,
she is only the same distance ahead,
then it is a dead-heat. Britain, previous to
about 1840 or 1850, was one of the most
intensely Protectionist countries in the
world ; and Britain then was far in advance
of almost every other country in almost
everything. The energy, intelligence,
aggressiveness, and fighting capacity of her
people, and the fact that she was more Pro-
tectionist than most of her rivals, had given
her a lead that seems almost incredible
when the facts are viewed in cold print.
Then Britain took to Freetrade, while her
leading rivals either remained Protectionist
or became more Protectionist. And then,
for the first time, the relative merits of the
two policies had a chance of fair comparison.
* * * #
The heat and burden of the following
statistics are mostly taken from the latest
edition of the great “Dictionary of Statis-
tics ” of Mulhall, the best-known statisti-
cian of the day, and himself an Englishman
and a Freetrader. Where the “ Dictionary
of Statistics ” doesn’t contain the required
information Mulhall’s “Industries and
Wealth of Nations ” has been used instead,
but the eminent Freetrade statistician is the
authority used all through. Britain is
quoted as the typical representative of Free-
trade ; France, Germany, and the United
States as the leading representatives of Pro-
tection. It was impossible to set against the
three great Protectionist States three great
Freetrade ones, for there ajen’t three nota-
ble examples of Cobden’s hazy policy. So
far as possible, the statistics deal with a
comparison of the years 1840 and 1896 ; the
first date goes far enough back to take in the
beginning of the Freetrade period in Bri-
tain : and the second one is adopted because
the statistics go no further. Where different
years are used it is only because Mulhall
doesn’t furnish figures for these two exact
dates. Unlike the Sydney Freetrade organ,
whose main argument consists in unsup-
ported assertion and the villainous woodcut
of the woman with big feet, The Bulletin
gives chapter and verse for all its statements,
and it quotes from a Freetrade authority in
every case.
* * * *
The comprehensive test of progress is the
wealth of each State, in proportion to its
population. That includes everything—
shipping, railways, canals, buildings, tele-
graphs, mines, factories, agricultural and
pastoral products, forests—everything. The
ordinary Freetrade authority, as a rule,
sticks closely to Britain’s superiority in com-
merce and shipping, and if shipping was the
only form of wealth, he would be quite
justified. But shipping has no more right
to be considered to the exclusion of other
forms of wealth than have wheat or brown
soap or new-laid eggs. If Britain rules the
world in commerce it is good for Britain,
but if, where Britain makes a million out of
commerce, another country makes a million
out of peanuts, one fully balances the other.
The total wealth per inhabitant is the test
which includes all assets, and therefore the
following figures are a solemn essay on the
futility of that logic which consists in waving
the arms windmill-fashion and crying wildly,
“ Look at England ! ” The figures in brackets
refer to the page in the 1899 edition of Free-
trader Mulhall’s “ Dictionary of Statistics ”
where the information may be obtained.
The figures for 1840 are worked out from
Mulhall’s estimates of wealth and popula-
tion, and anybody can check the result for
himself. The figure's for 1896 are taken
bodily from a table on page 818, the result
being worked out by Mulhall :

Mulhall supplies no wealth statistics for
Germany, except as regards a very few
recent years, so it is reluctantly omitted.
Also he gives no British population statistics
for 1840 (only for 1831 and' 1841). The 1840
population figures are arrived at by deduct-
ing one year's increase from the figures for
1841. But the eminent Freetrade authority
shows that in both France and the United
States the individual citizen is groiving rich
faster under Protection than is the individual
Briton under Freetrade. (The increase in
the total wealth of France would have been
much greater but for the loss of two rich
provinces in 1871.) This result is not what
one might have expected from so ardent a
Cobdenite, and evidently Mulhall should be
suppressed by Parliamentary enactment.
Mulhall (this time in the “Industries
and Wealth of Nations ”j gives some rather
ragged information about the total earnings
of various nations. The results are difficult
of comparison ; still they are interesting.
(Figures in brackets refer to the page of
“Industries and Wealth of Nations,” where
the matter quoted can be found) :

(Increase over £74,000,000 per annum.)
The fact that the returns do not relate to
exactly similar years is a drawback. Another
drawback is that there are no population
returns for many of these years, so the exact
increase per head can’t be shown. Still, it
appears that the earnings of the people com-
posing what is now the German Empire
increased more in 54 years than the earnings
of the British people did in 59 years. And
the earnings of the United States increased
over twice as much in 24 years as those of
Britain did in 59 years. In the case of the
States the comparison starts at 1870 in order
to avoid the boom period of the Californian
gold discoveries, and the civil war, and the
Mexican and Texan annexations, and to deal
with a period of steady-going industry only.
* * * *
Also on page 129 of his “Industries and
Wealth of Nations ” Freetrader Mulhall
gives a curious set of figures as regards the
average earnings of every inhabitant of
Britain and every inhabitant of France in
1816, 1860, and 1894 respectively. By this
he elaborates the fact that in 1816 the Briton
averaged £6 a year more earnings than the
Frenchman. In 1860, when the Protectionist
era in Britain had not long ended, the Briton
earned £8 a year more than the Frenchman,
thus showing that while Britain was, in the
main, more intensely Protectionist than
France, the Britonkept getting further ahead.
And in 1894, when Britain had enjoyed 34
years of Freetrade, and France had had another
long spell of Protection, the Briton’s average
earnings were only £5 per annum more than
the Frenchman’s. The Protectionist French-
man kept dropping further and further
behind the' Protectionist Englishman, but he
seems to be fast overtaking the Freetrade
Englishman. No similar tables regarding
Germany and the United States appear to be
published.

* * • * ’
Then finally we come on this mass of in-
formation regarding trade. The commerce
of Freetrade Britain is the sheet-anchor of
the Freetrade logician, and in this matter
the mode of presenting British statis-
tics has given him a great advantage
over all comers. The average trade statis-
tics deal with external trade only, and
Britain reckons as external trade ' what
most of its rival states regard as internal.
Britain may be said to have started its
Empire with the three kingdoms of England,
Scotland, and Ireland. It, added to them
many other possessions, and the trade of the
three kingdoms with these other possessions
is called external trade, and therefore it
appears written large in the statistics. The
present German Empire extended from the
Kingdom of Prussia, and the U.S. extended
from the original 13 States, and the Russian
Empire from the petty Grand Duchy of
Muscovy; but old Muscovy’s trade with
Poland and Siberia, or New York’s with
Chicago, or Prussia’s with Bavaria are not
mentioned in the ordinary trade statistics at
all. On page 388 of Muuir all’s “ Industries
and Wealth, of Nations” can be found the
total trade, internal and external, of the
four great States, in 1896, as follows—and
by this method they are all put on the one
footing :
United States. .£3,311,000,000 Germany ..£1,501,000,000
Gt. Britain and France .. 1,324,000,000
Ireland .. 1,884,000,000
No statistics of previous years are given, so
comparison of relative progress is impossible.
Still, the fact that Britain is so far behind
the United States in the gross, and barely
keeps pace with it in the amount of trade per
inhabitant is hardly what Freetrade litera-
ture would lead one to expect.
* * * *
Britain is undoubtedly far ahead as the
great ship-owning country of the world, and
this fact is the great capital of the Freetrade
logician—or illogician. But the value of
shipping is included in the figures relating
to wealth, and the profits of it are included
among earnings, so in the above tables
Britain has received full credit for this item.
The general result seems to be that Britain
under Freetrade barely keeps pace with the
decadent Frenchman, while as regards the
two great Protectionist States of its own
branch of the human family—the two that
are largely of the same descent, and gifted
with the same energy, grit, and staying
power—it is being rapidly overhauled in
some respects and has been already over-
hauled in some others. The crucial
test is that which deals with the in-
crease of total wealth per inhabitant,
for that item covers everything ; and this
test shows that the enormous lead which
Britain gained in its Protectionist days is
being shortened every year under its later
Freetrade policy. The mere spreading of
Britain’s empire has nothing to do with the
case, though there is a vague, unreasoning
idea abroad that the Anglo-Saxon shoots
straight through the helpful influences of a
low tariff; the so-called Anglo-Saxon was
the same successful and enterprising fighter
and annexer long before he heard of Free-
trade that he is now. It is for the reasons
above shown that the war-cry of the Aus-
tralian Calico- Jimmy party, “ Look at Eng-
land and see the Glorious Results of Free-
trade,” does not appeal much to The Bulle-
tin. It has looked at England, while most
of those who use that war-whoop, and most
of those who are impressed by it, evidently
have not, or else have only looked with one
eye. And now that the cry of “Look at
England ” is evidently going to do service
once more in the Federal tariff cam-
paign, this paper asks every Australian
voter to look, to take a good, long,
intelligent look, and judge for him-
self, instead of taking things for granted.
Looking at England has done more than
anything else to make this paper Protec-
tionist. A Protectionist policy will indirectly
yield much revenue, which this country
requires badly. And the balance of evidence
appears to be in favour of the theory that it
will yield a great increase of prosperity at
the same time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The 20 Municipalities of Adelaide.
Adelaide’s Town Clerk has issued a weighty
document re the urgent need for consolidating the
many municipalities of that Holy City. Adelaide,
in the matter of city government, is in the same
difficulty as Melbourne, Sydney, Brisbane, Dune-
din, &c., only it has the disease in a rather worse
form than most of its neighbors. It has twenty
municipal governments, 20 mayors, sets of aider-
men, town-clerks, town-halls, and complete sets of
officials, and about as many separate debts—all
for 150,000 people. The results are just what they
are in the other Australian capitals. The munici-
pal bodies can’t put up 20 destructors—one each —-
for the incineration of their refuse, and they can’t
agree to combine and have one or two big ones,
and no one would let another use its destructor;
so there is none. The tramways are in private
hands, because the 20 councils can’t agree about
joint ownership. They can’t even have a joint
garbage-tip. A cheap, comprehensive system
of electric - lighting is impossible, for they
can’t agree to combine, and they can’t
buy 20 electric-lighting plants. They don’t
feel called upon, in fact, to agree about any-
thing, so nothing decent in the way of city
government is ever done in Adelaide —nor in any
of the other capitals. The municipal control of
an Australian city is a chronic interview of 20 or
more strange cats on a roof. Worst of all, the
only remedy that any Government seems to
suggest is that nothing shall be done until the 10
or 20, or 30 or 40 municipal cats, which have never
managed to really concur on the smallest subject,
shall come together and arrive at a workable plan
for combining under one city administration —
shall amicably abolish themselves, in fact. This
is just as promising as the idea that a burglar
should give himself a fair trial and then imprison
himself. And just here The Bulletin again
wastes a little good space in putting its own
scheme before the community, which is that some
intelligent Government in each province should
draft a measure to this effect:—
(1) That all the municipalities in and around its own
capital shall be embodied in one.
(2) Aldermen to 1 e elected every three years, all on one
day, by one-adult-one-vote. Aldermen to be paid—ex-
perience has shown that in Australia good men can no
more be got to manage a city gratis than to manage a
bank gratis. Mayor to be elected bv the people. State
audit of accounts. Auditor to have power to compel the
levying of a sufficient rate to cover the year’s estimated
expenditure, contribution to sinking fund, and previous
year’s deficit, if any. Loans to be raised for permanent
works only. Every loan to have sinking fund attached.
Council to have power to compulsorily resume property
for widening and straightening streets, opening new
streets, and other improvements. Also to have power to
establish or compulsorily purchase, own, and control
sewerage system, gas works, water works, electric-light-
ing works, tramways, baths, public laundries, parks,
recreation grounds, markets, abattoirs, charitable institu-
tions, savings banks, houses and shops, libraries, and
many other things.


(3) That the new city be divided at first into wards,
each ward being one of the existing municipalities.
Representation to go according to population. The
reason for this method of division appears hereafter.
(4) The new municipal government to take over, at a
price to be fixed by independent valuators, all the saleable
or profit-earning assets of the existing municipalities, and
pay for them by assuming a corresponding proportion of
the debt. Where there is no debt, or the value of the
asjets taken over is more than the debt, the ward (or
former municipality) to be paid the difference by a
reduction in its taxes, spread over, say, ten years.
(5) Each of the other wards to pay off the balance of
its debt (the part not covered by asssets handed over) by
an increase of taxes spread over ten years. The amount of
increase would vary according to the amount of the debt.
Thus in ten years everything would be placed on a
uniform footing ; the debts of the most burdened muni-
cipalities would be paid off ; the ones which had entered
into the union with more assets than debts would have
been paid the balance due to them for their assets ; none
of the solvent existing municipalities would have been
taxed for the benefit of their insolvent neighbours;
thereafter taxation would be uniform and there being no
need to retain the old boundaries (only required for pur-
poses of taxation) the city could be re-diviiied into wards
according to population only.
(6) To prevent the city politics being wholly of the local
pump order, the Council to consist of two sets of
aldermen equal in number—one class elected by the
separate wards, the other by the city voting as one con-
stituency.
And having drafted some such scheme as this, the
intelligent Government should submit it for
acceptance or rejection to the municipalities on
the one-adult-one-vote basis, and leave the exist-
ing Councils out of the question altogether. Their
consent isn’t to be expected in any case. The
whole scheme is based on the conviction that the
existing Councils are so many ignorant and stupid
excrescences and require to be abolished, and the
idea of waiting for reform till 10 or 20 or 30 or 40
Councils agree on the subject of their own in-
capacity is too slow. This paper can’t remember
when even two men—let alone 200 aldermen—
agreed on the subject of their incapacity unless
one was much larger than the other. Even then
it was, as a rule, only a case of the big man agree-
ing about the small one’s incapacity, and often he
only agreed till the small man found a policeman.
♦
No Exceeding’.
The Vic. wine-export trade is being steadily
wrecked by the local grower, who insists on
adulterating with salicylic acid—a foolish and
nefarious ingredient which most wine-growing
countries have prohibited. After much inex-
plicable hesitation, the M'Shiels Government
has at last introduced a Bill prohibiting this
adulteration under a penalty not exceeding £lO for
the first offence, £5O for further offences, or in
default not more than six months’ gaol. It is
tolerably safe to assume that that good old “ not
exceeding ” will make this law. a mere
joke, like most other such laws in Australia,
for the wine-growing J.P. will make the fine
Is. the first time and 2s. 6d., or less, afterwards.
In like manner, Australia tries to put down larri-
kinism by authorising the inferior courts to award
anything not exceeding six, or perhaps twelve,
months “hard,” and then the “beak” fines the
most murderous cases 40s. and merely brutal ones
ss. ; and Australia wonders why a law authorising
such heavy punishments (which are never enforced)
doesn’t suppress the evil. A minimum punish-
ment is quite as badly wanted as a maximum one.
If the law was only half as careful, to decree that
the adulterator should be fined not less than £5,
and the brutal rowdy get not less than three
months, as it is to make sure that they
will be fined not more than so much, or im-
prisoned not longer than so long, it might “get
there” occasionally. But it never gets there—
not with the adulterator and the larrikin, anyhow.
When it has tried hard for a long time to put
down some offence by a penalty not exceeding £lO
(which means 2s. 6d. in practice, unless the
J.P. happens to be a personal enemy), then it
is generally decided that further legal terrors are
wanted, and the penalty is raised to £2O (and it
still means 2s. 6d. in practice, unless the
J.P. is a personal enemy). Good old “not exceed-
ing ” is one great reason why so many laws have
all the backbone of a piece of soaked blotting-
paper.
The Coming’ Crawl.
England, as it has been well said, is a country
which goes on all-fours. The poor man cringes to
the rich ; shirt-sleeves bow down to sillc-hat; the
“ lower classes ” crawl before the “ upper ” —who
crawl to the titled—who crawl to the monarch.
Naturally enough, it is sought to graft this atti-
tude upon Australia ; and the foundation of the
Commonwealth is to be celebrated by a visit from
the Duke and Duchess of York—and a prime
crawl. The taxpayers’ money will be spent in
triumphal arches; the Fitzgibbons of the con-
tinent will surpass themselves in grovelling adula-
tion ; the political nonentities, tongue in cheek
and hat in hand, will make speeches of enthusias-
tic welcome ; and the crowd, which wishes to be
fooled, will be fooled to the bottom of its shallow
instincts. And why ? The Duke and Duchess of
York are two commonplace young people, abso-
lutely undistinguishable from the herd in looks,
or brains, or stature. They do nothing,
they know nothing, they are nothing. But
because the Duke of York is the grandson of the
reigning English monarch, it is the duty of a
“ loyal ” people to be dust beneath his ordinary
feet. Was there ever anything so insane as this
brand of loyalty ! When England was a compara-
tively savage State the monarch had his justifica-
tion. Harold and William of Normandy, who
fought for the English crown, were the best fighters
of their epoch. They ruled in virtue of their strong
brains and strong hands; and the respect
paid to them had some warrant of logical
necessity. But that a man should be respected
because he is the son of his father, who was
respected for being the son of his father, and
so on, there is no logical warrant whatever. The
Duke of York is a physical weakling, and intel-
lectual pigmy—that is to say, his natural endow-
ments are on a par with those of the ruck of
his “subjects”; and he looks every inch a
nonentity. He does not make his claim to
respect valid. In every ordinary walk of life
a man has to show cause why he should
be honoured before public honour befalls
him; but a monarch rests on his ancestry,
and gets his wages without earning them. Isn’t
it time the foolish superstition which transfers
credit from the high office to the mean occupant
was abandoned by people who call themselves
reasonable? Or even if it be admitted that
monarchy has its uses in England as a constitu-
tional stop-gap—a makeshift device till English-
men are able to govern themselves without an
ornamental Panjandrum at the top of the social
edifice; of what use is the monarch in Aus-
tralia? What do we gain from revering the
ancient fetish ? It does not help us —not even as a
stop-gap or makeshift. We gain nothing at all
from the Duke of York, and can gain nothing.
But when he visits Australia, foolish Australians
will crawl officially and privately—because their
fathers crawled, and their grandfathers ; and the
crawl-instinct is engrained in them. The Bulls-
tin can only hope to some day see this country
erect upon two legs in face of any monarch or
god or devil whatsoever.

The Battered Miner and His Wife.
“ Miner ” to The Bulletin on the above text: —
Mines Minister Fesan’s Miners’ Accident Relief Bill
now before N.S.W. Parliament is for one or two reasons
an improvement on the one it displaced. One reason is
that it refers to all miners and not only to coal-miners ;
another is that the fund, which is to be created, is to be
available for those temporarily as well as for those per-
manently disabled. Of course since to pay out of a fund
means first paying into it the Bill has had numerous
kicks aimed at it; but if it had been a little better
drafted a lot of these kicks would have been saved up for
something else. For instance, Mr. Fegan proposes that
each working miner shall pay £1 a year (9d. a fortnight—
it amounts to about the even sovereign), the owners 10s.
for each workman they employ, and the State as much as
owners. But wanting to say that payments shall be made
by the owners in quarterly instalments the Bill con-
trived to say that they shall pay 10s. quarterly
—£2 a year. And as" on the local mine com-
mittees and on the Central Administrative Board
owners and miners are to be about equally represented,
this tax of £2 as against the miners’ £1 naturally set the
owners kicking. Yet from the Minister’s explanations it
is clear that the owners, while paying 10s. a year, are to
have representation almost equal to the miners who pay
£1; so that if any kicking is to be done on that score the
miners should do it. The gist of the proposal is that 9d.
a fortnight shall be stopped from each man’s pay and
handed to a committee formed at each mine employing
over 20 men. There doesn’t seem to be any provision for
men working at smaller mines ; but seeing that such men
are occasionally thoughtless enough to have wives and
children and mothers, and as stones drop down little
shafts as well as big ones, there doesn’t appear to be any
good reason why, if one little mine can’t form a local com-
mittee, several little mines together should not do so.
These local committees are to consist of the manager, the
sub-manager, the Govt, inspector, and three miners’ repre-
sentatives. It would be more convenient if the owners were
permitted to nominate what officers they please ; in the
case of a big mine the manager will rarely give the time
and the accountant will always take his place. Also, the
Govt, nominee might be left open—it will not always be
convenient for the inspector to act. There is not a
mining inspector within a 60 hours’ journey of White
Cliffs, for example, though five or six hundred men are
usually employed in the claims there. The owners are
not to pay into these local funds, but into a central fund,
which is to be managed by a board representing the three
sets of contributors—a very good idea, because this cen-
tral body will not mind bringing a long-winded co. to see
the error of its delays. The local committees are to pay
out of the local funds as far as the funds will go ; for the
rest they are to draw on the central fund. The payments
are, roughly, Bs. a week to a widow while unmarried or
to the guardian of a victim’s motherless children,
and 2s. 6d. to each child under 14; or if the
victim was unmarried Bs. to the mother “ if
she was at the time of his death dependent on
the deceased for her support ” ; or in certain circum-
stances the victim’s sisters or their children may get the
allowances. Also £l2 is allowed for the funeral. If a
man is disabled only, he is to get 15s. a week. The pay-
ments are well enough as far as they go ; but if a man
pays into the fund should he not be absolutely entitled to
benefits from it ? If an unmarried miner is killed and it is
shown that he was not supporting his mother or sisters,
nobody receives any allowances. Even if he ought to
have been supporting somebody, even if he has been
grossly neglecting his duty, the payments cannot be
made. He may have a crippled father aged 89 ; but if he
has not been doing the old man justice the fund won’t do
him justice either. The schedule wants overhauling.
With this done and the bill further licked into saying
what the Minister says he meant to say, it will be a first-
rate start on a big and intensely practical social reform.

A Decorated Gentleman on Reciprocity.
The Australian public will note with some in-
terest that William Patrick Manning, knight,
of N.S.W., has been giving away their property
in London in a rather lavish manner, and arrang-
ing one-sided reciprocity treaties, _ and otherwise
fixing things up. The following is from an in-
terview which Mr. Manning poured out on the
bosom of St. James’ Gazette :
I have always felt that, apart from sentiment, in return
for what the colonies received from the home country in
the way of financial assistance, protection from outside
interference, and in many other ways, they should at
least give a quid pro quo —say in the direction of a lesser
tariff than that which is imposed on the imports from
foreign countries. The matter has not been publicly re-
ferred to in Australia, but I imagine that the proposition
has only to be stated to find acceptance.
Now, if Mr. Manning feels that he owes Britain
a debt of gratitude for anything (his knighthood,
possibly), it is only honest that he should pay it—
at his own expense. Biit as regards Australia in
general the case isn’t clear. To start with the
alleged “financial assistance,” this country has
paid interest, very high interest in many cases,
for all the assistance it has received. It is still pay-
ing 5 and even 6 per cent, on some of its old public
loans, and Britain has a great deal more cause to
be grateful for the interest than Australia has for
the principal. As regards private borrowings, this
country, despite some recent losses, has
been a mine of gold to the British investor. In
the grand old early days he often got over 30 per
cent, for the coin he sent out here ; the case of the
shareholders of the Union Bank is one in point.
In the matter of defence, Britain has never yet
had to spend anything for the protection of Aus-
tralia for it has never been attacked. This country
has already spent a little money helping in purely
British wars, but Britain hasn’t spent a copper
on Australian wars for there have been none; up
to recently we have been sensible enough to stick
to our own business and keep a civil tongue in our
head. And so far as sentiment goes, that holds
good either way. If the Anglo-Saxon in Australia
should have a sentimental attachment for the ditto
in England, then the latter should have an equally
strong sentimental attachment for the Anglo-
Saxon in Australia. They are both descended
from the same old tribe of German marauders. So
this paper holds that reciprocity treaties are a
matter of pure business, and should be treated as
such. At present Australia is mostly Protection-
ist, for purely selfish reasons; it reckons that
Protection pays best. Britain is mostly Freetrade
for purely selfish reasons; it reckons that Free-
trade pays best. Australia admits British and
foreign goods on the same basis, and Britain ad-
mits Australian and foreign goodson thesame basis.
Each has to struggle for a foothold in the other’s
markets on exactly the same terms as the French-
man, German, American, Russian, Chinaman,
African, Kanaka, and other outsiders, and no
favour is shown on either side. There is no reason
that this paper is aware of why Australia should
not give a preference to Britain, provided Britain
will respond—that also would be pure business. If
this country admits British goods at, say, 5 per
cent, less duty than those of other countries, then
the reasonable course is for Britain to return the
compliment. Where Britain (with a view purely
to what she regards as her own interests) charges
no duty at present, then the obvious solution of the
difficulty is for that country to put 5 per cent, duty
on foreign goods. Whether it is a case of charging
5 per cent, less on Australian goods or 5 per
cent, more on non-Australian goods as a matter
of indifference; the result is the same. But for
any gentleman, in a burst of gratitude for his
knightly decoration, to assure Britain that Aus-
tralia is quite ready to make a reciprocity treaty
wherein all the reciprocity will be on one side is
absurd. It may be that Mr. Manning ought to
go out of his way to help British trade, out of
gratitude for his decoration, but there is no
evidence that Australia should do so out of grati-
tude for Mr. Manning’s decoration. If all Aus-
tralia had been knighted the situation would have
been different. The size of the difference would
depend on the value which this country put on
the gewgaw.
♦ 7
The long delay in the presentation of N.S.W.’s
financial statement suggests that Premier Lyne
is in a desperately tight place. And so he is.
Last year, so far as is known, ends barely met,
even without the war expenditure ; it would have
been a wonder if they had, for the Plague was a
big extra, and ends haven’t really met in N.S.W.
for many a day with no Plague. This financial
year the war expenditure is still going on ;
the Federal expenditure begins in January,
which is another extra, though not a large
one; the finance committee has recom-
mended increasing the annual payment towards
the deficit from £150,000 to £400,000 (£250,000
extra), and Lyne promised to carry out its recom-
mendations ; he has also promised to establish an
Old Age Pension system; he is disinclined
to save any part of the Federal expenditure
by cutting down the local Assembly one-half
—it would take a strong man to do it, and
Lyne is only a long man; any large in-
crease of direct taxation would bring down a yell
of execration, and a big increase of customs
duties would do likewise. And even if new duties
were imposed now they could only be collected for
nine months of the year at the outside. A
financial genius would revel in such a situation;
it is only by having difficulties to surmount that a
big man can gain credit for surmounting them.
But Lyne doesn’t revel worth a cent. He never
revelled in all his life. Probably he will meet the
difficulty better than Reid, but he can’t say the
funny things about it that Reid would. Reid, so
to speak, drew attention from his deficits by turn-
ing two somersaults and shouting “ Here we are
again ! ” Lyne’s solemnity makes 2d. surplus
look like a deficiency of a shilling.

Bishop Webber and sundry other Brisbane
notables are very anxious that Chief-Justice
Griffith should be allowed to enter the arena of
Federal politics—with intent to become the Fed-
eral Premier. Of course, it is assumed, that the
other States can have no possible objection. But,
inasmuch as that worthy Judge is not ripe for a
pension for the next six years, a little scheme is
hatching to bring in a bill to shorten_ that
period and let Griffith have his pension a
little earlier. This scheme has the support,
naturally, of the Brisbane Courier, which
has its own reasons for wishing to see
Attorney-General Rutledge step into the Chief -
Justiceship. Without wishing to deprecate Grif-
fith’s undoubted services to the Federal Cause, it
is to be hoped, in the interests of Democracy, that
he remains outside the Federal Parliament. At
present he is a reactionary of the most dangerous
type —inspired, apparently, by a bitter hatred of
the progressive movement. In any case, the his-
tory of Queensland in particular has shown
the inadvisability of confounding the legisla-
tive and judicial functions. It is not to the
interests of any country that men should be
hopping from the Leg. Assembly to the judicial
bench, and from the judicial bench back into Par-
liament again. The essence of Parliamentary life
is partisanship; the essence of the judicial func-
tion should he impartiality. How a man is going
to be a pure partisan one year and a perfectly pure
and impartial judge the next year, is a problem
that will pass most poor wits to fathom. They
may manage it in Bananaland for in Bananaland
politics all things are possible.

♦The Albert River (Q.) Tramway Bill presents
some delightful features. Aforesaid Bill provides
for the construction of 120 miles of line from the
Albert River to Lilydale, via Burketown. The
co. gets the freehold of the land necessary for the
line and the buildings, and obtains, moreover, a
concession of 2000 acres of mineral lands free from
all labor conditions for the next 50 years.
Half of, this area may be selected at any
time during the next half-century, either
in block or in scattered sections anywhere
within 40 miles of the railway. Any minerals
raised are to be exempt from all such
preposterous impositions as royalties and so forth.
Furthermore, so that the co. may have some poor
recompense for its toil and trouble in grabbing the
above-mentioned 2000 acres of mineral lands, it is
to be allowed to charge 50 per cent, more than
the present rates ruling on N.Q. Govt, lines.
This, as The Bulletin has already pointed out,
is the same burden on the country as if the State
built the line with money borrowed at per cent.
In the next 20 years there will probably be enor-
mous reductions in the rates of Govt, railways
in the North, but the Albert River Tramway
Co. is to go on its joyous way unaffected
by little trifles of that kind. In case
there be any disputes with settlers concerning
railway rates and so forth, the Bill kindly provides
that there shall be no remedy save in the Supreme
Court —a provision which will admirably suit such
of the Albert River settlers as may be millionaires.
The surface rights of the country taken up by the
syndicate are also the property of that philan-
thropic body, wherefore any miners settling
thereon will be simply tenants of the co. There
is, to crown all, a sweet little provision, by which
the co. does not need to put a pick in the ground
for the next six years. And it is alleged that this
measure was brought in to promote a speedy
settlement of the wilds of North Queensland !!
N.S.W.’s ex-Premier Reid went over to Mel
bourne recently and delivered a soul-stirring
address on the blessings and advantages of Free-
trade. But he never explained why he crawled
down so abjectly when it came to applying his
own policy to his own province. Reid had a
glorious chance and threw it away. He preached
Freetrade, and he retained protective duties on
sugar, glucose, molasses, treacle, biscuits, confec-
tionery, fruits and jams. He kept up the old Pro-
tection of 3d. and 6d. per gallon on beer, which is
why beer has always flourished in N.S.W. (There
is no Protection on spirits, so there isn’t a single dis-
tillery in the province.) Alsohe retained the protec-
tive duty on tobacco, cigars, and cigarettes (cigars
and cigarettes are protected in N.S.W. to the
enormous extent of 3s. Gd. per lb., the duty on the
imported article being 6s. and the local one only
half-a-crown). He left very heavy Protection on the
wine industry. He protected the agricultural
industry to an enormous extent by charging higher
railway rates on the imported article. George
Reid says he believes with all his soul that Free-
trade is a policy which enriches the country.
That leaves two alternatives; either he isn’t tell-
ing the truth, or while he was in power he was a
rascal who tried, by leaving a certain num-
ber of injurious Protective duties in force, to
injure the country which trusted him and paid
him his salary.
A South Australian writes
When the labor-unions broke down so badly during
their first struggle with organised employers, 10 years
ago, and when “ Socialism in our time ” seemed to thou-
sands the only cure for the laborers’ wrongs, South Aus-
tralia established 13 village settlements, all more or less
communistic—mostly more. Now a Royal Commission
which has been inquiring into them finds six of them
dead, and the other seven (containing 95 adult males)
have cost the country, in advances in excess of repay-
ments, the tidy sum of £76,100. The unpaid advances to
the abandoned settlements totalled £19,500. Nor have
the settlements left off sucking yet, though the “ sub-
sistance ” allowances are now very small; yet, if they
are withdrawn altogether, it seems likely that there will
be some more collapses. The Commission —it included
two Labor-members and Premier Holder— recommends,
in accordance with the general wish of the settlers, that
the communistic or co-operative basis should be further
amended ; “ the general admission is that ‘ human nature
is not good enough ’ to work on such a plan, and the de-
sire is for a larger measure of individualism.” The ques-
tion, “ Who is to wash-up ? ” was fatal, as usual.
A couple of Carlton (Melb.) honorariums
reached such a giddy height of out-
rageousness last week that even Victoria
noticed something unusual about their be-
haviour. Catherine Hughes was to be charged
before them with assault, and had been allegedly
summoned, but when the case was called there
was no Catherine. It transpired that the lady
was at that moment serving a sentence of seven
days for another offence, and so couldn’t appear ;
but the awful justices weren’t going to let a little
thing like that stand in their way. The accused
wasn’t on view, nor was she represented by a
lawyer or other agent, but the pair of
J.P.’s heard the prosecution’s evidence
and promptly gave the absent Kate, another
two months’ hard. The Melb. dailies re-
mark gently that the Solicitor-General should
take steps to have the wholly-illegal proceedings
annulled, but the necessity for having the two
incredible J’sP. annulled is even more urgent.
Also, is Carlton so hard-pushed for magistrates
that it is necessary to keep a couple of specimens
like these on the roll? Truly the Carlton J.P.
is the champion prize exhibit of Victoria, and for
outrageous absurdity and shameless injustice he
overlies every other created thing.

The Queensland Full Court has come to a decision
which, if fully acted upon, will be a ruinous knock
to some branches of business. The plaintiff in the
case sold certain mining shares and gave notice to '
the company to accept no more calls in his name.
The object of this intimation was, of course, to
make it certain that the new buyer must register '
himself and make himself liable for calls, and that
if he failed to do so, the shares would be forfeited
and sold. The co. didn’t act on the instructions,
and the buyer didn’t register himself. The buyer
(who may be called Smith to save pronouns) left
the shares standing in the name of the former
holder (who may be alluded to as Jones to save
circumlocution). Of course if the co. had begun -
to declare dividends at any time it is safe to as-
sume that Smith would have registered himself
Eromptly and taken the dividends, but till then
mith lay low. He paid calls in J ones’s name
till he became convinced that there were never
going to be any dividends. Then he stopped
pacing, and left Jones liable for calls on shares
which he didn’t own. And while Jones was
liable for calls hq still had the pleasing knowledge
that, should the mine take a change for the better,
Smith would turn up and take the dividends.
The Full Court has decided that this state of
things is quite legal, and that Jones must pay
Smith’s calls until Smith chooses to relieve him
of further liability by registering himself as the
owner. There is apparently no means by which
Jones can compel Smith to register, and the co.
is not bound to take any notice of Jones’s
intimation that he sold the shares to Smith.
Under these conditions it seems that when a man
sells shares in Queensland he may be afterwards
ruined by an unlimited liability for calls on the
other man’s property, and the only remedy
appears to be never to own shares in Queensland
under any conditions whatever.

This epistle gets into print mainly because of its
ingenuous thankfulness that Wesleyans don’t
drink :
Editor Bulletin.— Nobody feels really “insulted” by
Archbishop Redwood’s assertion that “ the founders of
their particular brand of belief were mostly hogs.” Pro-
testantism, I take it, believes in Protestantism only, and
not in the man who founded it. You speak of Protes-
tantism as “ a particular brand of belief,” but considering
that a majority of the “ believers” scoff at the saints of
the sects, such as Wesley and Calvin, it is hardly fair to
brand them all alike. The one point of sympathy among
Protestants is their protest against what they consider
the obvious absurdities of R.C. bigotry and super-
stition. On all other points of kerristian faith the
sects have a little contempt—mostly good-humoured
contempt—for one another. They can’t insult one
another as regards their respective points of difference
and as for what any R.C. may say about Martin Luther
and Co.—why, they refuse to take it seriously. A Catho-
lic can’t insult a Protestant’s religion. It is too strong in
the belief in its own comparative reasonableness to resent
the shrieks of angry bigotry. But a Catholic’s, an Irish
Catholic’s, religion is eternally looking for insults, it
seems to me. The R.C. trails his precious Faith along the
ground and waits for a dhirty Protestant to tread on the
tail of it. I always think of a devout Catholic as a man
who starts to hate all Protestants when he has got half-a-
dozen whiskies in him. His religion then becomes a
public nuisance. Probably the religion of the bigoted
Wesleyan would be even more of a nuisance, if he inflamed
it with liquor. But he doesn’t, thank Heaven! I’ve
never heard a person proclaiming his belief in the holiness
of Wesley, say, at an hotel bar ; but I’ve heard man3 r
drunken Catholics defying the company in general to
speak disrespectfully of the Pope.

“ Layman ” on death-under-chloroform:
More than 50 years have elapsed since chloroform first
came into general use, and doctors have not yet learned
to administer it to patients with safety. The number of
deaths under the anaesthetic has greatly increased during
the last few years in Australia. The other day an appar-
ently healthy young lady succumbed to chloroform in a
dentist’s chair in Melbourne, making the third local misad-
venture of the kind during the last 12 months, without
counting the too frequent occurrence of deaths from the
drug in the hospitals and at the hands of general practi-
tioners. The fatal results do not often arise from heart-
failure, or any diseased condition of the individuals
operated upon, but from suffocation pure and
simple. If a gallon of water is poured steadily
down a man’s throat in a gentle stream, he
dies because he does not get time to breathe. With
chloroform, if the physician administering it does not
constantly watch the pulse and respiration, and see that
there is plenty of time for the alternate inhalation of air
and chloroform, suffocation must speedily follow. In the
hospitals it often happens that the person entrusted with
the administration of the drug is a young medico who is
too much interested in watching the operation to pro-
perly attend to his duty, and lives are thus sacrificed. It
is imperative that a trained anesthetist shall be em-
ployed on all occasions, the utmost care and watchfulness
being observed ; and there is an added danger in giving
the anesthetic to persons sitting in a chair, a position in
which respiration is less easily performed than in the
recumbent one. It will be nothing short of a calamity’ if
the most beneficent discovery of the century is dis-
credited, and sufferers are thrown back on the horrors of
the pre-anesthetic days. Pepys of the Diary celebrated
by an annual banquet to the end of his long life what he
justly considered his marvellous survival of an operation
for stone with nothing but a few doses of brandy to alle-
viate the pain. And Nelson never went into action after
his arm was cut off without ordering the ship’s carpenter
to boil the surgeon’s instruments, so great was his terror
of the coldness of the steel.

tures.

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
