Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Sydney rolled up very considerably to the
picturesque little Centenary Hall last Friday
night, when the dark, saturnine face of its “Banjo”
gazed down from an unusually high altitude of
platform. That returned pen-warrior described
the Transvaal scrap from its every-day bread-
and-sausage aspect, and he really succeeds in
getting the every-day aspect of the war, while
some of the others merely make a heroic
attempt to show its poetry and glory. “ Banjo ”
manages to bring us into close sympathy with the
common daily camp life, and the men who go
into battle, not shouting the battle-cry of their
fathers, but with hens and ducks slung over
their saddles, so that they may be sure of a good
feed when the fight is over. He likewise gives
a hit at the excited Australian who couldn't be
bothered with the routine, and had to be gently
reasoned out of his delusion that going to war
meant merely fighting. “ Banjo ”is not as good
a lecturer as writer, and he suffers from nervous-
ness most of the time, but he does not seem to
suffer from swelled head. He makes a strong im-
pression that whatever he says is absolute fact,
and that he could only tell a lie by trying very
hard indeed. He is likewise under no delusion
regarding the glory of war. He looks on this
prosaic business as brightened, not by sublime
thoughts of Queen and country, but by the joke
you have with the Tommy next you when another
Tommy conveniently finds that his horse is
lame, so that he may fall back and become a
“ waggon warrior ” at the rear, where the bullets
are thinnest and the Boer cumbereth not the
kopje. The limelight pictures are rather faint, but
genuine. As the
lecturer himself
pointed out, the
people repre-
sented are not
gazing at the
camera, but
doing their
work. Apropos
of which, “Ban-
jo” gave a jibe
or two at several
lying cinemato-
graphe shows we
all know of, and
explained that
modern battle
is not one man
standing in one
comer of the
field waving a
flag, and another
man running
about on a horse
making a target
of himself, with
onearm upraised
to give the bul-
lets still another
chance. It is
merely a num-
ber of people lying flat on the ground, and
continuing to lie flat on the ground for a good
time. In fact, if the cinematographe is to describe
a Transvaal battle correctly all it needs to do is to
bring on the screen a piece of veldt and let it re-
main there for half an hour or so, while a helmet
bobbing up now and then will show that the fight
is still raging !
** * *
The crazy-quilt drama of “ New Babylon ’
unfolded 12 tableaux at Sydney Royal on Saturday
night, and did it in three hours and a decimal.
It isn’t easy to remember any drama of the size
with less consecutive story in it. There are shreds
and scraps of tale, kept apart by cattle and horse-
races and irrelevant auction sales of small ponies
with long tails, and the casual herb of narrative is
found growing sparsely in spots with miles of
picture and panorama in between. There is a
masterly collision in mid-Atlantic, and a large,
very gorgeous and brilliantly-lighted ship heaving
on a green and worried-looking sea. There are
two girls, one good and the other base and horrid,
but so much alike that the audience takes them
for the same girl—especially as Miss Frances Ross
plays both of them —and becomes tangled in its
mind like the legs of the fretful octopus. Both
girls are incomprehensible. One of them is
the adopted daughter of the old man who
comes from nowhere in particular, and either
she or the other one is also the daughter
of the old woman who demands gin, and
sleeps on doorsteps, and whose bonnet looks
as if the cat dwelt in it. And the old woman
either was or wasn’t married to the man who came
from nowhere ; and if she was, then his second
marriage was illegal and his other daughter is
illegitimate, and if she wasn’t, what is all the row
about ? And the second girl, if she really is the
second girl and not the first girl over again, is the
siren who has ruined Baker on the turf, and
Baker is engaged both to her and to the girl who
is illegitimate if the old woman who sleeps on
doorsteps has told the truth ; and the second girl
seems to be also in some way or other involved
with Norman, who is really married to the first
girl; at least, it being quite impossible to sort out
the girls, tilings look like this, but may be other-
wise. Anyhow, the £12,000 isn’t in the old man’s
safe when Norman and his two friends break into
it. And honest John Randall has just called the
old man bad names and left by the back door, re
some misunderstanding about the girls, when dis-
honest John Ramirez comes in by the other
back door and welts the ancient from behind,
and takes the £12,000 out of his insensible and
prostrate pocket. So he naturally thinks it was
John Randall who fractured him from behind, and
puts the accusation down on paper before he falls
down partly dead, but nothing Being legible after
the “Ra” of Randall the law, by an unusually
correct mistake, takes Ramirez instead. This is
the one coherent fact in the 16 acts. After that,
the drama gives up trying to explain who is who,
and its latter end is like unto an illustrated maniac
with ancient Chaldaean inscriptions in his hair.
* * • *
Some foolish and misguided person has
been trying to bring “New Babylon” up to date,
with sad and boshy dialogue about the Chinese
war, the brotherhood of the U.S. and England,
and the like. A very large number of people
are washed back and forward in this drama, like
old bell-toppers on a rising tide, but there is so
much horse-race and cattle-show and ship and
floral fete that no one has a chance to be much
more than a lay figure. A very magnificent ballet
is a most commendable member of the co. Misses
Frances Ross and Harrie Ireland are two fine pic-
torial effects. Baker emerges from the ruck of
horse, cow and dance for about 15 minutes in all.
Norman, Bland Holt and Arthur Styan appear
for a trifle longer, and Charles Brown erupts
occasionally. The effect is satisfactory, so far as
it goes, but scrappy. Max Maxwell's Chinaman
leaves a more definite recollection behind him than
anyone else.

** * *
“ Jones ” at Sydney Palace is going
successfully in his fifth or sixth or seventh week,
and as business is still robust enough to pass The
A.M.P. Society’s medical examination without
difficulty, he will probably last some time longer.
*# * *
Lizzie, the elephant, still enacts the
luncheon-eating alderman at Fitzgerald’s Circus,
and also wears for a short time the monkey beard
of a blameful Kruger. If this beard were kept on
when the British Lion takes his famous ride on
her back supported by the Empire’s Flag, the
item would then have a beautiful symbolical
interest. This idea is presented to the proprietors
absolutely gratis. Rosie Aquinaldo is contorting
even more wonderfully than at first, and suggests
another idea —that she and Ferry of the Tivoli
should have a contorting match together, to prove
beyond doubt which stands highest for absolute
bonelessness. The items which take place near
the ceiling happen as before.
** * #
Changes are not very numerous at Sydney
Tivoli this week. Albert Whelan, the new per-
former, gives clever imitations of several actors
that the average theatre-goer knows and several
that he doesn’t. Miss Billie Barlow is in her last
nights. When Billie is gone the audience will be
left still wondering what she would do if the fat
man she is always asking for were really passed
over to her one evening. The Haytors are giving
the “Original Burlesque Cinematograph.” It is
rather a good burlesque in its way.
*# * *
“Constance Clyde” gives “Drink” a
send-off: —
“ Drink ” has been bowdlerised so completely at Sydney
Criterion that there now isn't adequate explanation for
the things that happen. Those are principally the hus-
bands that happen to Lily Dainpier. That lady’s melan-
choly tones are for once heard to advantage in a part
wherein she seems to spend the most of her life in
going through a relay of tight husbands. “ Drink ”is the
sad story of a nice girl who tried to get a sober partner,
and the play ends without any dead certainty that she
has got one at last. Her. first is a gentlemanly journey-
man hatter, whose work consists in looking for work at
12 o’clock at night when it can’t be found, and then retir-
ing to an apartment where the chest of drawers, which
won’t open, is realistic, and the bed, which is oniy wide
enough for one, isn’t. When Husband No. 1 dies
outside the scenes in the vague fashion which,
in the stage, means an early resurrection, Lily Dainpier
mmediately espouses a working man who is not gentle-
manly and who has signed the pledge, and she hopes
thereby to gain peace. The fact that husband No. 1
turns up on her No. 2 wedding day and wants her back
that he may stay away from her as before does not get on
Miss Dampier’s nerves at all, for the next act finds her
still married to No. 2, and an offspring on the scene, and
no wail about her lost honor at all. The only wail she
wails is re the fact that husband No. 2 got drunk on the
wedding-day owing to the fact that, having kept away
from drink for some time, what he does take now is all
the more inclined to go to his head. All the other men
who haven’t taken the pledge remain quite sober, though
they have presumably taken just as much as he did. This
goes on throughout the play. Hubby No. 2is the only per-
son who sees rats or wanders among fruit-laden branches
that afterwards turn into slimy snakes with horned heads,
all because he signed the pledge. Having taken to drink
once more through the effects of falling off a scaffold,
husband No. 2 dies in a chair in the same old blue blouse
which he has worn throughout the eight years or so of
the play, and Lily, after falling in the snow and saying
she wants to be with the angels, promptly gets up again
when another fellow comes along and requests to be No. 3.
As this man is different from husband No 2, and likewise
different from husband No. 1, there is some possibility
that he may pull through the ordeal of being married to
Lily without succumbing to the Curse; but the fact that
he is the Champion Abstinence Man of the district is a
good deal against it.
* * * *
To which it may be added, by way of P.S.,
that “Macbeth” will be played at the Cri. on
Friday, and “The Penalty of Crime” starts on
Saturday. The latter is “for the first time in
Australia.” Pity is that the drama which appears
for the first time in Australia is often so like the
one which ought to have appeared there for the last
time that its newness makes no difference.

The man who runs the exhibition of our
best girl’s skeleton and other human organisms,
vitals and jim-jams, at the Queen’s Hall (Sydney),
reports that his show will move on in a few weeks
to illuminate the intellect of other parts. Mean-
while, admission is reduced to Is., and the model
of our best girl is unscrewed and lectured on every
night.
** * *
Madame Adelina De Lara, whose portrait
appears in this issue, is a pianiste of much dis-
tinction. Of Spanish extraction, the lady was,
however, bom in England, and received her musi-
cal education in Germany, Italy, and various
Continental centres. At the age of six the artiste
appeared as a prodigy on the concert-platform,
and the promise exhibited as a child nas been
thoroughly fulfilled. Madame De Lara is the
wife of Mr. Thomas Kingston, the well-known
English actor, now touring Australia with the
Nance O’Neil co., and has taken advantage of her
husband’s sojourn in Australia to pay us a visit.
Her first concert will be given at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, on Tuesday next, Oct. 2.
*# * *
The invincible brogue of J. F. Sheridan
has scored again at Melbourne Royal, and “A
Trip to Chicago ” is proceeding until further
notice. Nine long years, it seems, have passed
over the Widow’s auburn wig since she first
tripped to the place where the Exhibition was in
the character of Bridget O’Brien Esq., with
Gracie Whiteford as Norah O’Bre-on, her darter.
Her present alias is Johanna Murphy, and her
spinster offspring is known as Lulu. The inci-
dental mining boodler, and also the military person
whose leg went wrong at Balaclava, are chasing
the relict of O’Brien under their original names;
otherwise the new bill differs freely from the
document of May ’9l, when Williamson, Garner
and Co. figured at the head of it. A number of
small details have been added to the narrative,
and Johanna Murphy’s millinery is not what
it used to be. The Bulletin makes this
assertion with a profound forgetfulness of
Bridget’s previous splendor, but it looks a safe
thing to say, because her garments change their
fashion and grow old, whilst Sheridan simply
renews himself at intervals, like Parliament. At
10 o’clock “A Trip to Chicago” takes the form
of a garden-party by moonlight, and then all the
members of the co. who have been running a
farcical comedy give a mild series of “turns,”
assisted by some frivolous lady visitors, who dance
a hornpipe. The garden and the Heavens above
are the work of the young man (Reg. Robbins)
who fled from his awful mother-in-law and got
drunk in the first act, and for the sake of his
clever stage picture an uncomplimentary notice
of that young man’s acting is cancelled herewith.
** * *
The Brotherhood of Gethsemane were to
break up their monkery at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
on Tuesday of this week, from which it appears
that the holy man who went to Glory by night
was not quite so popular as he promised to be.
An interesting revival of “ Tess ” has possession
of the floor to-night (Thursday) and to-morrow, if
the advt. kept his word. “Tess,” with a new,
well-balanced co., and Cecil Ward as the truant
Angel, ought to be fully equal to business require-
ments. “The Only Way” makes a first Melb.
appearance on Saturday (29th), but the unselfish
bachelor of last century will lay down his tired
life for six nights only. After that a “Royal
Divorce” revival, and then a brief closing of the
theatre for furt her alterations.
** » *
At the Bijou things are much the same as
before, plus the announcement that Billie Barlow
will add her engaging smile to the programme on
Saturday. Meanwhile, Emmonds and Emerson
and the other Emmonds are the newest arrivals,
and public opinion seems favorably disposed to-
wards those frivolous strangers. Also the eye of the
populace rivets itself every evening upon the bio-
graph picture of the fond young mother who
wears a pair of nice clean stockings under her
nightgown, and gets saved from a bogus conflagra-
tion by a gallant impostor in a helmet. The pic-
ture, apart from its nightgown interest, is gene-
rally admired for its artless stage management
and simple faith in the credulity of man.
** * *
Particulars are to hand of George Mus-
grove’s new opera co., which makes a first appear-
ance at Melb. Princess’s (Oct. 13th) in “II Trova-
tore ”or “ Faust.” The 12 principal singers in-
clude the famous Salvi and another tenor of
“star” rank, three young sopranos, each with
prima-donna successes to her name, two first-class
contraltos—one of whom, Miss Agnes Jansen, has
appeared with Melba and the De Reskes —and
baritones and bassos of a quality said to be fine all
round, and undoubtedly very expensive in several
cases. George Musgrove, in fact, has risked the
loss of much money in grand-opera speculation,
believing that Australia will appreciate the show
at its full worth, and give him financial encourage-
ment to do it again next year, and years after.
The so far ill-starred Overflow mine does
not seem to agree with the German doctors asso-
ciated with it. Some time ago Dr. Lilie died,
greatly regretted, and the other day Dr. Colpe, of
Nymagee, a practitioner much esteemed by the
medical profession, was found dead in the neigh-
borhood of the mine.
The Fahey fiasco in Melb. (where a young
man was wrongfully charged with assault on a
girl in a railway-carriage) was simply due to the
old jealousy between the detectives and the uni-
form police, which is o.ily one degree less bitter
than the jealousy between the individual detec-
tives themselves. In this case the detectives were
too high and mighty to consult the police at Box-
hill (where Fahey lived) who could have proved
defendant’s innocence, while the police, on the
other hand, resented the d.s’ neglect and pre-
ferred to see the Grown made a fool of by an
abortive prosecution rather than open their
mouths. The Vic. Detective Office is sadly in
want of a head.
The great joke of the period is the decision
of the Mayor of Sydney to prosecute that violent
cleaner-up, Premier Lyne, and his colleague
O’Sullivan, for keeping dirty and infectious pre-
mises in the resumed area. The shining act of
Bill Lyne’s administration was his great cleaning-
up, when he took the reins from the hands of a
dirty and effete municipal council, and scrubbed
the awful city which it should have scrubbed, but
didn’t. If the council had accused Lyne of drink,
embezzlement or bigamy it would have been bad,
but not unbearable; it would at least not have
been plugging him on his brightest virtue, so to
speak. But when Mayor Harris rises up as a
clean mayor and the head of a clean and sanitary
council, and says publicly that the filthy character
of the great cleaning-up Government has driven
him to take legal proceedings—well, insult isn’t a
name for it.

The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table in-
stalled at their South Melbourne works of a parcel of
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating Mill at the Conrad Mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mine
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without re*crushing. The result of the Wilfley
test is a high-grade concentrate, and tailing waste show-
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*
■* * * *
The quantity of sulphides now treated per week
at the “ Barrier” compares favorably with the output of
any silver-lead mining field in the world. The difficulty
in treatment of sulphides is being successfully grappled
with mainly owing to improved systems of mechanical
“dressing.” In this the Luhrig Vanner plays a very
prominent part. A large number of these machines are
now in use there, and fresh orders are still being given to
the agents, Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne. The
South Mine people are remodelling their mill on up-to-
date lines, and will install twelve or more Luhrig Vanners
of the 1900 pattern. The British Co. is also erecting more
of these superior machines.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts,forgings, andallsortsofiron-work.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
To the Amending Municipalities-Bill, now before
N.S.W. Parliament, a clause should be added providing
that no alderman be allowed to sit whose rates are over a
month in arrear. Municipalities are mostly bossed by
landlords, who often endeavor, and successfully, to dodge
the rate-collector. Result is disinclination to enforce
payment of arrears, for fear of exposing themselves.
Consequently rates are allowed to accumulate until dead
loss often occurs. Meanwhile the inevitable overdraft is
piling up interest, and the community suffers both ways.

« » * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph moans again about
the opposition to the A.M.P. extension :
“ There are still sections of the opposition striving to
discredit the directors by statements which may be
noticed as showing the extent to which misrepresentation
can be carried. It is, for instance, asserted that the
directors are seeking to extend their business to all the
hot-beds of fever in the Empire—Hongkong, Burmah,
Singapore, Demerara, and so on, to say nothing of the
Niger River territories, British Honduras, and the Fly
River. It would, of course, be distressing to picture the
votes of the niggers on the Gold Coast some day out-
weighing those of the Australian native, if it were not all
such an effort of pure imagination. ... If the A.M.P.
Society, by amextension of its field of operations, were
merely seeking to find outlets for the dissipation of the
society’s funds, why take all this trouble about it, when
it is in the power of the directors to squander them here
quite as effectually? They here have ample powers to
extend their business to consumptives and inebriates.
They can vote every officer of the society a salary of
£lO,OOO a year. They can deposit all the society’s funds
in the Post-office Savings Bank for safety, and only draw
interest on £2OO out of the fifteen millions. But the fact
remains that the directors do none of these things, though
it would be equally as much to the purpose to get up a
scare about them as about a tropical river swamp outside
Australia.”
* * • •
The Bulletin is certainly one of the papers
which drew attention to the fact that the A.M.P. directors
are fighting with desperate insistence to persuade policy-
holders to give them full power to extend theA.M.P.’s busi-
ness to the West Coast of Africa, Hongkong, India, Ceylon,
Burmah, Honduras, Demerara, the Fly River and other
dreadful and ruinous places, though it certainly never
said that they intended to extend there. What they
intend doing is only known to themselves. But the fact
remains that it is unspeakable foolishness to entrust any-
body with power to extend to such places. They may have
got power, by accident, to put the A.M.P.’s £15,000,000 of
funds in the N.S.W. Savings Bank, where the whole pile
would only bring in 16 a year interest in all; but that
has nothing to do with the case. The matter at issue is :
supposing they hadn’t that power, and supposing they
were moving heaven and earth to persuade the members
to specifically give them full authority to put all the
£15,000,000 in the Savings Bank, where it would only
earn £6 a year interest, it would be highly inadvisable to
grant them the authority asked for. Likewise if they
were found harrying the policy-holders to put a clause in
the articles of association specifically authorising them to
pay all the society’s employes, office-boy included, £lO,OOO
a year each, it would be highly desirable to vote “ No ” in
that case also. The situation is just this : The directorate
of the A.M.P. Society is making tremendous efforts to
induce the policy-holders to give full power to them, and
to all their successors, whoever the latter may be, to
extend the society’s business to every unhealthy spot in
the British empire. They don’t ask merely for authority
to extend to the temperate regions of the empire outside
Australasia, but they are also touting with intense energy
for votes to give them authority to do business in all the
unwholesome spots mentioned above, and many more
besides. And the Daily Telegraph’s argument is that
they should get this authority because they can be relied
upon not to use it. They don’t even promise themselves
that they won’t use it; the Telegraph promises for them.
And The Bulletin’s argument is that they shouldn’t get
it if they intend using it, because in that case the per-
mission will be harmful; and they shouldn’t get it if they
don’t intend using it, for in that case the permission will
be useless and not ornamental. To get down to business,
if the Telegraph will act up to its own doctrine this
paper will withdraw its opposition to the A.M.P.
extension—will withdraw it absolutely. The terms are
these : The Telegraph is hereby requested to give The
Bulletin its certified cheque for £lOOO (just as the A.M.P.
policy-holders are requested to give authority for extend-
ing the A.M.P. business to India, Ceylon, West Coast of
Africa, British East Africa, the Fly River, Hongkong, &c.)
The Bulletin doesn’t assure the Telegraph that it won’t
use the cheque, but it will get the Barrier Miner to give
the assurance on its behalf, without assuming any
responsibility (just as the A.M.P. directors don’t per-
sonally assure the members that they won’t use the
authority they are clamouring for, but the Telegraph,
without guaranteeing to make good the damage if it is
wrong, assures for them.) If the Telegraph desires to
know why on earth The Bulletin wants a cheque that it
isn’t going to use the answer is simple ; it wants that
cheque for the same reason that the A.M.P. directors
want an authority which, we are assured, they don’t in-
tend to use. And if the Telegraph says that, under these
circumstances, it would be quite as satisfactory not to give
the cheque, then The Bulletin’s reply is that it is quite
as satisfactory not to give the authority. If the D. T.
departs on the side issue that the right of extension to
India, Sierra Leone, &c., is only part of the question, and
that it is tacked on to the question of the right of exten-
sion to England (a power which the management does
intend to use), The Bulletin is quite willing to tack its
question of the £lOOO cheque on to some other question
also. But it asks the Telegraph to be logical, and hand
over that £lOOO cheque.
* * * *
And the Daily Telegraph winds up by saying
that the public may rely that the directors advocate the
extension of the society’s operations (though it is wholly
to countries where the death-rate is higher than in Aus-
tralia, and largely to countries where the interest rate is
lower), “ with a clear knowledge that by so doing this
(the A.M.P.’s) high reputation will surely be increased,
and the existing members benefited.” The Bulletin also
asks for that £lOOO cheque with the clear knowledge that
by parting with it the Telegraph’s high reputation will
be increased and the owners benefited. It gives no convinc-
ing reasons for this knowledge, but then the A.M.P.
directors don’t either.
* * • *
The People’s Prudential Assurance Co. (it has
now swelled to this dignity, but it was formerly known as
the People’s Prudential Benefit Society) Sydney, com-
pleted its fourth year on 31st August, 1900. Results so
far:
Total
Income Expenses Claims Outgoings Balance
1897 £8,611 £3,796 £3,966 £7,762 £849
1898 11,670 4,646 6,173 10,819 851
1899 11,782 4,674 6,935 10,609 1,173
1900 11,544 4,737 5,164 9,901 1,643
£4,516
The business of the People’s Prudential Assurance Co. is
mostly in the sick and accident benefit line, though it
also does life assurance of the humbler kind. It is appar-
ently another development of the system under which
doctors contract to attend a large number of people in
the lump at very little each per annum—the great
medical sweating system, as it may be called. It was
originally founded by one Mulligan, who was, for a time,
the whole institution —the laws of N.S.W. allow Mulligans
to do such things. Then it was floated into a company
(present paid-up capital, £4110) and the co. bought Mulli-
gan’s business for £3500. Therefore one of the largest
assets is goodwill, £3500. Affairs stand as follows :
Liabilities. Assets.
Paid-up capital ..£4llO Deposits, &c. ..£3515
Assurance Fund .. 4516 Mortgages, Furniture,
Small debts.. .. 279 &c. .. .. 1890
Goodwill (Mulligan) .. 3500
£8905 £8905
Income is smaller than it was last year or the year before,
and expenses are higher than they have ever yet been.
Still, the business is apparently prospering from a share-
holder’s point of view. It is accumulating money reason-
ably fast, and the expenses (41 per cent.) are really low,
as things go in the melancholy industrial business. Next
year there is to be an actuarial investigation, and then it
should be possible to say how the assets compare with the
liabilities. Next year also, the management proposes to
begin writing off the paper (goodwill) item of £3500, which
is a highly commendable idea. No dividends have yet
been paid, but they will probably commence before very
long.

Melbourne Permanent Building Society shows
a material improvement for the year ended 31st July,
1900. This concern reconstructed in 1893, agreeing to pay
the impossible rate of 5 per cent, on its debentures, and
re-reconstructed in 1894 with debenture interest at 2£ per
cent. Results have been as follows :

Net result is a loss of £49,504 in eight years, reducing the
reserves from £56,548 to £7044. The actual losses on
property sold and for depreciation written-off have been
rather larger than appears above, but the Society has set
against them certain profits on the repurchase of its
debentures at a discount, and only the net loss is shown.
On the other hand the liabilities (apart from liabilities to
shareholders) have been reduced from £463,725 in 1893 to
£246,353, which is an enormous load lifted ; the trading
profits are increasing rapidly, and though they still only
equal £1 9s. 6d. per cent perannum on the nominal £177,164
capital, there seems no reason why they should not
presently soar to the dazzling rate of 2 per cent, or even
more. The Melbourne Permanent Building Society, in
the days of the great slump, had nearly the whole of its
mortgaged properties left on its hands; its principal
assets are £43,202 of loans on mortgage, £54,147 worth
(nominal) of vacant land, and £326,583 worth (purely
nominal) of house property. It is one of the largest and
most comprehensive city landlords in Australia. The
house property at present yields £2 Bs. 2d. per cent, net
on its nominal worth, so on real present values there is
probably a loss on this and the vacant land of £lOO,OOO or
more. ’ Still, while the institution can show increasing
profits and keep reducing its liabilities there seems no
reason why it should not hang on to its properties till
they become worth full paper value again, and with
sufficient patience it should not only save its capital in-
tact but become once more a good dividend-paying
institution.

The City and Suburban Permanent Build-
ing and Investment Society completed its 22nd year in
1900. It was the only Brisbane institution of its kind
which did not suspend in 1893, a result owing to the fact
that it owns practically no land except what its office
stands on, takes no money repayable at call, and does
pure building-society business only. The last four years
have shown these results :

Reserves have risen from £6804 in 1895 to £12,025, an in-
crease of £5221. The City and Suburban, of Brisbane,
isn’t the ordinary kind of building society as the term is
understood in Australia now. The societies which pay
dividends regularly and keep increasing their reserves
could almost be counted on the thumb of one hand at
present.

The belated report of the Perpetual Trustees,
Estate and Agency Co. (Dunedin, M.L.) for the year
ended 30th April, 1.900, has just come to hand. It is a
material improvement on some of its predecessors, as
shown below:
Profit. Dividend.
1897 £1,476 12£ per cent. = £1,016
1898 1,722 121 „ = 1,016
1899 1,308 121 „ = 1,016
1900 1,943 12j „ = 1,016
£6,449 £4,064
In 1898 the Perpetual Trustees, Estate and Agency Co.
Erofessed to have an undivided profit balance of £2519,
ut as its accounts included the purely paper item of
“ Goodwill £2831,” there was really a shortage in its
visible assets, as against liabilities to shareholders
and the public, of £312. In the four years since then it
has accumulated £2385 over and above its regular 12J per
cent, div., and after writing off the objectionable paper
item of goodwill there is a real balance of £2073. V\ hy
the directorate does not put £2OOO of this to reserve (a
highly necessary item which the Perpetual Trustees,
Estate and Agency has never yet possessed), and then set
itself to systematically build up that reserve to solid pro-
portions, it is difficult to understand. Not only should
every trustee co. have a solid reserve, but both it and the
capital not actually used in the business should be in-
vested in State securities only; there is no institution
which requires to be more completely above suspicion
than a trustee co. The Perpetual, of Dunedin, earns
larger profits in proportion to capital, and pays larger
dividends, than any trustee co. in Australia or Tasmania,
but the smallness of the capital (£8126 paid-up) makes a
large reserve all the more essential. It is to be hoped
that the management won’t raise the dividend above 12J,
which is large enough in all conscience, but will set itself
to strengthen the institution instead.

The usual 10 per cent, dividend, and bonus of 10 per cent,
to shareholders on purchases, absorb £4839. The balance,
with £Bl4 brought forward, leaves £1029 at credit of pro-
fit and loss. Of this, £5OO is placed to reserve-fund (the
first addition made for a very long while), raising it to
£12,000, and the other £529 is carried forward. The total
reserves are thus £12,529, or nearly double the paid-up
capital of £6993. Also, it is satisfactory to note that the
bank overdraft, which was £3822 six months ago, is down
to £986.

The Argus Permanent Building and Investment
Society (Melbourne) is one of the very rare institutions
which never suspended, reconstructed, asked its de-
positors for reduced interest or extended time, or wrote
off capital. It even claims to have never yet sold-up a
client for unpaid interest. The Argus has stopped paying
dividends for a considerable time, but otherwise, for a
small concern vvith only £16,825 capital, it has made a
remarkably good showing all through the lean years :
Loss through de-
Trading loss. preciation of pro-
perty, &c.

The net loss in six years is thus £270, reducing the re-
serves from £lB5O to £l5BO. Even this, however, leaves
a reasonably solid reserve. Liabilities to depositors,
bank, and mortgagee were £14,443 in 1896, and are now
reduced to £13,512. The investments, after deducting
allowance made for doubtful debts, yield over £4 3s. per
cent., so the assets seem to be worth somewhere near the
amount now set against them, and there should be not
very much more writing down to provide for. The trading
profit is now about £1 Bs. per cent, on capital—not much,
yet more than the building society average just now.

A benevolent Melbourne lady, described as
“advanced in years and suffering from the effects of a
carriage accident,” recently offered the Alfred Hospital a
gift of £5OO provided the hospital paid her an annuity of
£4O a year for the rest of her life. Offer declined. Now,
how “ advanced in years” was that lady ? If a lady of 60,
even though she may have suffered from 24 carriage acci-
dents, puts down £5OO on the counter of the A.M.P.
Society, she will only get £4l 16s. Bd. per annum for the
rest of her life. And then she is supposed to be getting
full value for her money—not making a charitable gift

Sydney Harbor Colliery (Balmain) has now got
its shaft down 2000 feet, and hopes, in another year, to
have it down 3000 feet, and to be blackening the glorious
harbor with coal-dust at a great and profitable rate.
» • • «
The ancient conflict between squatter and pros-
pector has broken out at Wertago copperfield (Far West
N.S.W.), where the erection of a smelting furnace had
lately to be stopped because it was found that no timber,
either live or dead, could be cut in the whole of the
country except by the pastoral tenants. But the miner
is having his innings in the West now (five out of the
seven Western members are miners’ men), and the
obnoxious reservation has been cancelled in favor of one
a little less obnoxious.
• * • *
B.H. Consols, the English-owned mystery,
lying on a flat three-quarters of a mile from the Broken
Hill lode, is again yielding some lumps of almost pure
silver. There Is an “ indicator ” in the property ; they
follow this, and every hundred feet or so get into a bunch
of metal, paying a year’s expenses (it is economically
worked), and perhaps a div. or two. Practically no
shares are held in Australia ; but it finds in the Consols
a never-failing source of speculation for geologists and
mineralogists.
* * * *
What the just-floated South Blocks are to the
south end of the B.H. line, the Victoria B.H. is to the
north end. Both were worked years ago; both were
abandoned when the silver-slump came, not that either
ever marketed any silver, but because calls were not forth-
coming. In those days the Victoria B.H. was the Vic-
toria Cross. It has £lO,OOO to go on with now, and recent
developments in the neighboring North ought to show
the management which way to prospect. But the B.H.
lode must end somewhere ; and if it doesn’t end just
before it reaches the Cross, the chances are that it does
end for all practical purposes before it gets out of it.
To prove it one way or the other is a fair speculation.
• •* * »
Dear Bulletin.—Your correspondent’s infor-
mation that you “ had forgotten ” and which appears in
your Wild Cat of Bth inst., as put is misleading. This co.
(Soldiers’ Hill Diamond Co.) has paid in its first year’s
existence £lOOO in two divs. from profits in working the
mine, not as stated “ £6OO mostly from a 2s. 6d. call on
contributing shares.” The total sum from allotment of
shares was £750 —£600 of which was paid for engine and
plant, leaving for working expenses a balance of only
£l5O ; yet no call has ever been made. The Company
paid its way from the start from the proceeds of
diamonds. The mine has been run for shareholders, not
for the market. Additional mines have been purchased
and developed by a tunnel 517 ft. through hard granite,
costing up to £5 per foot, which has been paid for out of
profits. This co. is the first and only diamond co. in
Australia that has ever paid a dividend, due principally
to the fact that it has the only perfect diamond-saving
machinery yet erected in Australia. Other co.’s on the
field whose mines are five times richer in diamonds, and
that have spent many thousands of pounds in unsuitable
machinery, are to-day closed down or in liquidation. The
diamonds are here, and the field will yet come promin-
ently to the front when the mines get into proper hands
with suitable machinery and proper management.—
Yours, G. B. B. Elliott, Managing Director.

Cobar Chesney, N.S.W. is being examined and
reported on this week by a man representing London
people who are negotiating on a basis of £200,000 casn
which would mean over £1 a share. If they do not come
up to scratch another representative of London capital is
(so he declares) ready to take the mine over on a big cash
basis with a large paid-up share interest and a solid work-
ing capital. Shares 7s.—indicating that the market has
no faith in these deals eventuating or none in the mine or
perhaps none in either. That the mine can be made a
big one with big money and good management there is
not a doubt.
* * * *
North Nymagee, N.S.W. Writer’s old copper
friend proudly announces that the mine has been practi-
cally self-supporting the last six months. Sent away ore
that netted upwards of £6OO. If that meant self-support
it doesn’t take much to run a copper-show at Nymagee.
A Is. call is made for the purpose of putting up a concen-
trating-plant. It will take two or three more, and then
it will be found that some more Is. calls will be needed
for water-conservation, which seems to have been over-
looked. The management is simply playing with the
copper business. Some weeks ago a good bunch of grey
ore was found in the N.N., and the day after the dis-
covery one of the workmen, when asked, “ What about
the grey ore ? ” replied, “ Well, we had some splendid ore
yesterday, but it’s all gone to-day.” When copper goes
out zinc has a playful habit of coming in. Shares, 2s. 9d. ;
worked and managed as the mine is and likely to be, they
would be dear at any price. Too much Overflow manage-
ment about North Nymagee.

The Jennings Sluicing Co., Adelong, N.S.W.,
is in financial trouble, and the question of raising addi-

* * * •
Boulder Main Beef: Important developments
reported in this mine last week, on the strength of which
shares rose 12s. in London, representing an increased
valuation of £70,000. Of course, the ore is “ bristling
with telluride,” but the market having talked no doubt a
good find has been made. This mine has about 300 ft. of
good ground on the Gt. Boulder southern boundary, has
paid a number of divs., and is well managed on the field
as well as in London.

SOCIETY
The presence of the Duke of York, heir
to the throne, at the first meeting of the Federal
Parliament, bearing the Imperial Commission for
opening, is playing it a little low down on Gov.-
.General Hopetoun. Though nominally the repre-
sentative of the CrOwn may be the first person in
the land, the Earl will naturally take second place
to the Duke in the notice of the public. So much-
difference will the visit of the Royal couple make
to the Governor-General, that it is only reasonable
to suppose he was consulted before the decision
to send them was arrived at. The temporary loss
of position in Australia will perhaps be com-
Eensated for in the fact that the Earl will always
e able to pose as the entertainer of royalty, on a
scale which falls to the lot of but few of the
peerage in England.
* * * *
'The Federal flag, to which the adjudi-
cators awarded Melb. Herald’s £25 prize from
several thousand designs, and which the Common-
wealth is to be invited to adopt, is not a marvel of
either beauty or originality. The left upper
quarter shows the Union Jack ; the left lower, the
red and white stripes of U.S.A.; and right half,
the Southern Cross on a red field. The Union
Jack and the Stars and Stripes are a dull, unim-
aginative steal, and the white races of South
A SABRE-GUT OF SAXON
SPEECH.
THE MISSUS : “ The boss
didn't come with you,
Harry. Was he intoxicated?"

America had a lien on the Southern Cross
for at least 200 years, and those of South
Africa for at least 100 years, before Aus-
tralia was settled by white men. Surely it is
possible to find some design that will be really
Australian —not cadged from any place. The
white elephant of Siam is reasonably distinctive;
it has at least the merit of not being easily mis-
taken for any other banner. What is the matter
with the kangaroo, or the red waratah on a blue
ground? Of course, the flag selected by the
judges and described above is pretty much what
might have been expected. The judges who gave
it the £25 prize were looking out for an “ Im-
perial ” design which would be emblematical of
Jingo newspaper opinion re the English-speaking
race and so forth. They found it.
* m + m
The unlucky episode of the Redwood ser-
mon at the dedication of the Sydney R.C. Cathe-
dral will not be dropped in a hurry by the more
aggressive Protestant section of the community.
The Evangelical Council states that, as the Queen
is prevented by her coronation oath from attend-
ing a celebration of the Mass, Lord Beauchamp
committed a grave error in attending the service
as the Queen’s representative, in the uniform
always worn as sucn, and attended by his official
staff. The matter is to be ventilated by the Evan-
gelical Council to the bitter end. The ultimate
result will not affect Lord Beauchamp, who will
soon be safe thousands of miles away, but will
only revive the old-world sectarian feeling which
seemed to be dying down in N.S. Wales.
Of course a Governor may attend Mass,
otherwise Lord Gormanston and other devout
Roman Catholics who have been Australian
Governors could not have gone to Mass. But the
Evangelical point is that Lord Beauchamp should
not have attended St. Mary’s “inState,” seeing
that neither he nor any other Governor has
attended any church “in State.”
The perky brother of a well-known Sydney
R.C. politician was the other day spoken to about
the Redwood affair and solemnly volunteered the
opinion that it was a “ most lamentable ” occur-
rence. “But,” added he with a twinkle,. “it will
show ’em that we are still alive.” _ Which quite
recalls the old story of the priest who was
preaching on the evils of drink. “Drink, my
dearly beloved brethren, is a curse. It is Drink
that makes you shoot at your landlords —and miss
them."
• * • *
The first practical outcome of Archbishop
Redwood’s foolish attack on Protestantism was
visible at the annual ball in aid of St. Vincent’s
Hospital, Sydney. Usually on this occasion the
Town Hall is crowded, and the complaint is
general that there is not room for dancing, on
account of the crush; but this year the reverse
was the case. The dailies let the thing down
lightly by simply stating that the attendance was
not so large as usual, and there was plenty of
room for dancing, but the real truth is that the
thing was a frost, and that Protestants were con-
spicuous by their absence.
* * • *
It would be interesting to learn the full
and exact influence which is being brought to
bear upon certain Australian dailies to induce
them to ‘ ‘ barrack ” so earnestly against any
reduction in the salaries of the State Governors
under the Commonwealth. In more than one
instance the Chief Justice of the province has his
eye on the vice-regal billet, while the leaders of
Society are extremely anxious that none of the
hospitalities which they consider peculiarly their
own privilege should be curtailed because of a
reduction of the Lieut.-Governor’s salary to
reasonable proportions.
* * * *
Lord Tennyson’s regime is so far remark-
able that it is the only one for years during which
no scandal of any kind has been talked concerning
the doings of any of the inhabitants of Adelaide
Govt. House. Frugality is the very worst
thing that can be urged against it, and certainly
there is no reason for expecting that a, Vice-
Regality should over-shoot his professional income
by profuse hospitality.
The “assistant paymasters” accompany-
ing (at England’s expense and in the interests of
the Sydney Hekald and Telegraph) the Aus-
tralian contingent to China, have already found
sufficient spare time from their exacting labors on
the troopship to write lengthy accounts of men
and matters on the “ Salamis ” to their respective
journals. The appearance of these letters will
come as a great shock to Premier Lyne, who ex-
plained to N.S.W. Assembly that the duties
attached to assistant - pay mastering were so
onerous, the hours so long and the work so
important that he could not see how those selected
could possibly have time to act as war-correspon-
dents.
* * * *
The paymaster-reporter of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph bursts forth, in a recent issue
of that enterprising paper, into an interview with
Old-Man-of-the-Sea Hixson, which took place
somewhere off .the coast of China. He also spreads
himself in an article which turns chiefly upon the
Great Necessity which exists for an Australian
Fleet (with suitable Paymasters, one for each
sailor probably), which will plough the Main
around various Foreign Shores, with a D.T. re-
porter at the helm of each ship and a leader-writer
in the conning-tower.
* * * *
Sydney Public Library enquiry has de-
generated into little more than a slanging-match
between bookseller Dymock and Librarian Ander-
son. Each claims to be a book-expert. No doubt
Mr. Dymock knows the trade by which he lives,
but as for Mr. Anderson—a few years ago when
he was N.S.W. Director of Agriculture, the
Sydney dailies boomed him as a scientific expert
in soils, crops, &c. Now he bobs up an expert
bibliographer. Agriculture and bibliography !
each needing the study of a lifetime.

The prize servant-girl story :
Dear Bulletin. —A certain Tasmanian seaport is just
now a servant-girl’s paradise. There the most incom-
petent slaveys can command the best wages and choose
their mistresses at that. Recently the cook at the local
THE CROSSING-SWEEPERS.
hotel, who was drawing the not-to-be-despised screw of
£2 a week, demanded a rise and, when refused, imme-
diately gave notice. At the end of the week, however,
instead of departing with her baggage, she merely moved
to one of the best apartments in the house, and as there
was nothing against her character she could not be
turned out. At meal times she sampled every dish and
audibly criticised the cooking—done by the unfortunate
landlady herself. This went on for a week. Then flesh
and blood could stand it no longer, and the cook moved
back to her old room with a screw of £i !—Amt M.

This isn’t published out of any high-stomached
scorn for the Australian system of education, but
rather with pride and satisfaction. The Bulle-
tin has met so many Australians who can’t write
at all that it almost admires the bush girl who is
able to state in paper and ink that she doesn’t
mind “ nitting ore croshing ” in addition to her
other engagements.
* * * *
Rev. John Walker, of Woollahra, Sydney,
energetically pursues his collecting tour in aid of
the N.S.W. Presbyterian Centenary Fund. His
diai’y is published bi-weekly in the Presbyterian.
Up to date, in pounds and promises, he shows
£27,000; £16,000 of this, however, is put down as
promises. The balance, nearly £lO,OOO, is cash in
hand. He has given himself three years to gather
the proposed £lOO,OOO. One year has already
gone. He says the bulk of the people are giving
cordially, but the rich, so far, have been somewhat
slow, although a few have forked out well.
According to the list, the wealthiest Presbyterians
are to be found in Armidale, Bur wood, German-
ton, Manly, Wahroonga, Waverley, Woollahra,
Walcha, and Inverell.

A VERSATILE WIFE.
(“ Variety’s the spice of wife.”— Bulletin proverb.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The most successful wife I know
Is little Mrs. Daniel Low,
The most contented married man
I’ve ever come across is Dan.
If Mrs. Low is dark or fair
I really cannot tell, I swear.
Ohameleon-like, to please the men,
She changes color now and then.
Though some to dye are much afraid,
In one brief year her hair will fade
From glossy black through sparkling brown
To gold as soft as chicken-down.
Not one complexion does she own,
But every hue and every tone.
At time with cheek of tender tan
An Eastern houri flatters Dan,
And then with blend of cream and rose
She thrills him with an English pose ;
Or, pale, with hair of auburn stain,
She wins her husband’s love again.
And Dan in state monogamous
Fares like a Sultan—bar the fuss.
“ A wife ?” he cries. “ Behold, in her
I run a perfect harem, sir ! ”■

Sydney attorney Mark Mitchell suggests
that there should be a specially-designed elector’s
right for use in the Federal elections, and which,
after stamping, should be returned to the holder
to preserve as a memento and a heirloom. The
idea- is a good one.
• * * *
Australian women are, unfortunately, by
no means averse to the endearments of the
Asiatic alien. Madras Times (,Tune 14) tells the
story of a Hindoo tailor, who married, in this
country, an Australian girl, Louisa G , and
induced her to return to Calcutta with him and
settle down at Howrah. Here the wife was closely
confined and subjected to ill-treatment. She
finally induced her husband to carry a note, con-
taining an alleged medical prescription, to a
European chemist. The chemist communicated
with-the police, who found the woman in a lament-
able .condition, and removed her to hospital. It
is as well that our women-folk should realise that
the Asiatic generally regards the gentle sex as
very.poor cattle. The Hindoo and the Chinaman
have.no respect for the woman’s body, and the
Mahometan won’t allow her any soul. She had
better stick to Us, after all.

“Cecil P.” : Re Mrs. Edmund Barton not
meeting any “ haw-haw ■’ Englishmen at ’Ome
(Woman’s Letter, 8.,.25/8/’00). This was probably
because Mrs. Barton did not encounter any pre-
tentious persons of the commercial-drummer order.
Like “ humph-hunoph ” in Australia, that sort of
thing is confined to,a quite inferior class. A short
“ er ” —due generally to shyness or other causes of
verbal unreadiness—may be endured, but to “haw-
haw,” as to “ liumph-humph,” is to betray a sense
of superiority—which is accounted hideous form
by decent people.

district (N.S.W.) marriage everybody immedi-
ately concerned was named Smith, and a prelimi-
nary invitation ran—“ Mr. and Mrs. Smith”
(bride’s parents) “invite the presence of Mr.
and Mrs. Smith ” (bridegroom’s parents) “at
the marriage of their daughter, Miss Smith, to
Mr. Smith.”
* * * *
In view of certain proposals of N.S.W.
National Council of Women to deprive the
thirstier sex of the power to get a drink after the
ordinary shop-closing hours, and thereby drive
them home to their wives and sweethearts for
comfort and solace : Why doesn’t the Council take
the situation by the scruff of the neck and simply
force Premier Lyne to pass a law making it
punishable (by imprisonment) for any man to be
caught out after G. 30 p.m. unless in the company
of a woman? Men would then be compelled for
their own safety to take a lady friend with them
everywhere, and the lady friend would have to be
well treated and looked after, or else the miserable
man-thing might find himself suddenly deserted
when the slavers appointed under the Act put in
an unexpected appearance.
* * * »
Commissioners threaten to become as
numerous in N.S.W. as colonels in the U.S.
Boards seem to be going out of fashion, and the
Imperial imagination of the period demands com-
missions. President of the Darling I r arbor Com-
mission, President of the Harbor Trust Commis-
sion, President of the Fire Brigade Commission,
and so on, carry resonance and wide suggestive-
ness of power and dignity. Compared with
“ Head of the Water and Sewerage Board ” they
are truly magnificent.
* * * *
Owing to the industrious W.C.T.U. it will
soon be quite impossible to build a new hotel in
any part of South Australia. Under a recent Act
a majority of citizens in any district can declare
that no licences for new houses shall be granted
for seven years. Barring the metropolis itself,
these restrictions already apply to every district
around Adelaide, and now the Cold Tea party is
attacking the country electorates. When a poll
is taken the already-established publicans, who
naturally seek a monopoly, work enthusiastically
with their cold-water enemies, and, as the average
citizen is too tired to worry about the matter, the
voting is always one way.

Rumored that N.S.W. Minister for Educa-
tion Perry will call together the two rival art
societies (which now have their miserable feud
subsidised by tlie State to the tune of £BOO a year)
and give them half-an-hour to settle their dispute
—penalty for not settling, abolition of both sub-
sidies. As aforesaid feud is founded on the differ-
ence between artistic tweedledum and tweedledee,
and whether eggs should be broken at the blunt
end or the thin, such a threat would be popular
with the public, who want to pay for the main-
tenance of art, and incidentally artists, and not
for art-wrangles.

Sydney City Council, as a meek way of
celebrating the birth of the Commonwealth, pro-
poses to spend £5OO in planting a few nice trees
as heirlooms for succeeding generations. Which
sets one thinking of “ Arbor Day” in Sydney. A
few years back the local School Boards assiduously
planted trees in the presence of the scholars of
the public schools. This ceremony was done at
Glovt. expense, and was called “Arbor Day,” and
was to be an annual function. But Arbor Day
seems to have died with the first batch of trees it
planted.
The members of the Melb.
Scottish regiment have a sub-
stantial grievance, to which they
are wont to give profane expres-
sion. Their abiding sorrow is
due to the kilt and the pertina-
cious familiarity of a large pro-
portion of the female population
with whom they come in contact
during street marches and func-
tional peregrinations. For these
women, it seems, the braw, bare
legs of the antipodean “ Hielan’-
men ” have an awesome and irre-
sistible fascination which mani-
fests itself in a strange way, the
poor weak creatures being im-
pelled to prod the tender knees
and unprotected calves of the
kilted military with anything
they may have in their hands,
an umbrella for preference. So
that in the course of an ordinary
night march a nice young
Scotchman will often have his
legs scandalously disfigured with
pinches and punctures. The
female inspired to jab at the
unclothed masculine calf usu-
ally emits an insane and malig-
nant giggle. A gallant Scot
marching to the Exhibition
Building the other Saturday
night made a slash with his clay-
more, and laid waste a fifteen-
and-sixpenny gamp wielded by a
florid matron in Swanston-st.
Another hero sadly displays
three hat-pins gathered out of
his left shank during the same
evening.

A peculiar malformation
was brought to light at Mel-
bourne, recently, when a half-
caste arrested by the police was
found to have six fingers on each
hand, the little fingers being
duplicated.
In South Australia children under 18
years of age are now never brought before the
ordinary police-court when charged with offences ;
there is a special “departmental court ” for their
benefit. During the last 12 months 273 children
have been charged before this court, 135 of them
for alleged misdemeanors ; but in only six cases
were the culprits sent to gaol. The “ State Chil-
dren’s Department” runs the tribunal, which, it
is locally claimed, is the first of the kind anywhere
and is doing excellent work.
* m * *
The Bishops of the Anglican Synod who
complain that English clergymen are accepted in
Australia on their credentials, but Australian
clergy are not so treated in England, voice only
one specimen of British arrogance. A member of
“ Lords ” who visits Australia has only to produce
his card and all Australian cricket-club meetings
are open to him. Members of Australian clubs
must back their cards with cash in England. And
so in several other fields. . „ .

“Easel”: Attending a picture-sale in
Sydney, the other day, it struck me as a rank
absurdity that paintings, &e., should be offered
for purchase in dingy and badly-lighted auction-
rooms. Would it not be better for sellers and
buyers alike if periodical picture-auctions were
held in one of the art-societies’ well-lighted gal-
leries —which, by the way, are now empty nearly
all the year round ?

•streets they are failures.” Mayor Harris here
tried to smile. “ Little Billee” also stated that

prided itself on its democracy, the measure of
local government, one of the first accompaniments
of modern democracy, should be so limited. Then

enjoyed'it.

“ Weka,” an old Bulletin correspondent,
writes from Maoriland
Have just heard from a friend of mine who went to
S’Africa some months ago. Here are a few extracts from
his letter :—“ lam firing now on the Natal railways at
Bs. per day ” (he left Bs. per day in M.L.). “ I was clean-
ing a few weeks at ss. a day, and some have been at it
for two years ; how they live here on ss. puzzles me. Lots
of people are coming here only to be sorry for it, as wages
will come down. Some of the gold-mining co.’s are bring-
ing men out from England on two years’ engagement at
10s. per day, not equal to ss. in M.L., as everything is
very dear.

“ Black and White ” to The Bulletin
The trouble over the exhibition of a nude male model
before a mixed class, lately referred to in your columns,'
recalls a historic deputation of girl art-students which
waited upon the director of Vic. National Gallery to ask
that their male model might no longer have any of his
manly beauty draped. The spokeswoman pointed out
that the male students were allowed to draw from a, lady
wearing not the “ teeny-weeniest bit of clothes” and it was
a manifest injustice to poor down-trodden woman that she
should not be allowed the same privileges in her artistic
career. The director, touched with the pathos of the
situation, said he had no objection to a reform, if
arrangements could be made with the model, and the
deputation withdrew. The spokeswoman then sought
out the model, a grey-bearded old chap of 55, and put the
case before him. The elderly Apollo nearly dropped and,
blushing like a beaten baby, blurted out a horrified
refusal. Later on he confided in the male students, and
having been assured that he could go back into the class
with safety, he ventured in with his scant clothing tightly
clasped to his bosom in case any attempt should be made
to supplement moral suasion by force. This ended the
Movement.

The most striking feature of the prize-list
of Adelaide annual show—agricultural, horticul-
tural, &c. —was that about every third prize-
winner in the comprehensive poultry-classes was
the “Magill Boys’ Reformatory.” And it is a
characteristic circumstance, too. While waterside
Sydney sends its young scapegraces to coil ropes
and climb rigging, bucolic S. Aus. sends hers to
study fowls and farming.

that it is a spotless place that smells like Araby
the blest), and a shuddering beak came out into
the yard, and, standing to windward afar off, gave
the curio two months. The policeman didn’t
capture him in the usual way, but drove his prize
