A WOMANS LETTER.
. Sydney, September 25, 1900.
Mr Dear Mtkr.—
After waiting all the season for a favorable
opportunity to make its hop, St. Vincent’s ball
finally landed itself in the middle of the most un-
lucky week of the whole year. With Protestants
up in arms against those printed utterances of
Archbishop Redwood, it was a positive act of
bravery for that ball to come off, as it did, last
Thursday night. It was postponed in May and
June on account of the war. Here, in September,
it finds itself in the heart of a fiercely-raging
battle, and—l am sorry that it was so—a poorly-
filled hall.
Representative Protestants tabooed the dance
with fine religious fervor. Among those present
I did not notice Lady Darley, the vice-president
(the Mayoress), or but I could go on like this
for a long time. The Protestants who stayed
away, and the business firms who also suddenly
manifested a new religious dignity that prevented
them from subscribing to this unlucky hospital,
must have been in a large majority, for I hear that
the ball didn’t cover expenses.
By the way, surely this points pretty plainly
to the assistance given to R.C. charities in the
past by people of other faiths. Take it away
and you see how much it was. Naturally
Catholics are conscious of this. In fact I have
knocked against some strong and knifey expres-
sions from men,and women who may be regarded
as very fixed Romans indeed. It was one of these
who loudly voiced his disgust at an “At Home ”
last week by saying, “ I’d like to boil him in one
of the North Island geysers ! ”
As to the dressing, Mrs. J. T. Toohey was
m black, with diamonds. Her sister, Miss
Egan, was charmingly frocked in a pink-
flowered silk, the fichu and elbow sleeves of
pink chiffon, and chiffon flounces frilling round
the skirt. _ Miss Toohey set off her powdered
hair against a geranium-pink satin deepened
with vivid geranium flowers. Pretty Mrs. Kenna
was also poudre, and looked fetching in turquoise-
blue brocade, with a fringed polonaise over
flounces of turquoise chiffon. Mrs. Brennan (lion,
treas.) wore black satin, with a touch of violet
velvet on the corsage, and her pretty daughter
(who debuted at the Australian Club ball last
week) wore an ingenue white satin and lilies of
the valley. Mrs. John Mullins was in grey
brocade; Mrs. Collins in black. Two charming
powdered maidens were Miss Brock and Miss
Nugent. By the way, Miss Nugent marries her
cousin, Mr. T. J. Dalton, on Tuesday, October 9,
at St. Mary’s Church, North Sydney.
An attempt was made to revive the advt. cos-
tumes of the Poster Ball, but the result was
streaky. However, the handful of fancy dresses
had the advantage of being seen, and one could
not help but notice Miss Mildred Aarons—chief
bridesmaid at Miss Harris’s wedding, next week—
in a poppy-decked hat and corn-wrei tiled frock, a
charming little kharki tea-girl; and a pen-woman
in a short white frock and parasol, as the Coupon
Co.
The same night heard Elsie Hall’s first “ Duo
Recital” at the Y.M.C.A. Hall. This talented,
modest, young musician has more enterprise than
one would give her credit for. Her energy has
helped half-a-dozen charity-concerts to hum this
year. But what becomes of the house when it is
her own concert that she engineers ? Wednesday’s
concert was so highly charming that I was sorry,
for other people’s sake, to see such a thin audi-
ence. But, mind you, though thin, it was loving
and as thin things can be.
Por instance, in one row there was a “he”
novelist hoping pensively that the quiet-faced
piamste was not too cer&brale; a black-moustached
young pedagogue who shut his big eyes firmly
and frowned fierce frowns when a silk skirt rustled
or a programme fluttered; and three thin girls in
big sailor-hats over big thin noses. If you don’t
appreciate the quality of that row you are no
musician. Of course, there were a few other
people in the house, and in one sparse region of
blank seats four placarded, classified, musical
people conversated with a noise that suggested
crowds.
-Dr- Mathew Ennis, a young London musical
M.D., with a fair moustache and a reputation,
helped with the duos. There were no solos
except Miss K. McKonnel, an uncompromis-
ing soprano, of power, but little sweetness. The
two pianists started off on two pianos with a-
Bach concerto, but to- my mind they were hap-
piest m a delightful little group of Chopin
things, delightfully played—Etudes 2, 10, and 11,
and the A-ffat waltz. Both players are delicate,
feminine and light-handed. Otherwise, to make
duets °f these Chopin fancies would have been
slaughter. They also gave a tender interpretation
of oamt Saens’ Variations on a Theme of Bee-
thoven. As both pianos were lacking in the
same quality —passion—neither overplayed the
other. In its way, that is without any great
breadth or fire, their concert turned out to be
about the least tedious any local concert-goer has>
encountered since—but my memory is getting
jaded. Let us keep to the things of to-day.
By the way, some people think four hands are
too many for Chopin. Perhaps they will agree
with a man who writes well about things musical,
and says that a woman wants three hands to play
Chopin— two for the piano, and one for the man
who is listening to hold.
Speaking of music, here’s our concert menu.
Next Thursday, September 27th, Jessie King, the
English contralto, gives a concert at the Centenary
Hall, in the presence of his Ex., and assisted by
Herr Volmar. Miss Adelina de Lara comes be-
fore Sydney as a pianiste at a piano recital on
Oct. 2nd, also at the Centenary Hall. She brings
a big reputation from England. I have not heard
her yet, but if her playing is as charming as her
manner she is bound to please. Also, I could tell
you of thirty-two other concerts getting ready to
burst, but the warning is enough for to-day.
“New Babylon” at the Royal is a whirl of
frocks and wickednesses. Frances Ross, as the
twin women, gets a double share of dress, and
rings some startling notes. One of her frocks is a
glowing pink, with angel sleeves of white chiffon,
and rosettes and streamers of turquoise blue on
the corsage and in her hair, a la Nance O’Camille ;
another is a black and white giddy-looking skirt
with dashing stripes, topped with a little black
velvet coat, a big hat decked with red and purple
roses, and long black streamers. Harrie Ireland
abandons the evil contralto of her other ways at
last, and comes out as a good lady in pale green
chiffon, with white chiffon sleeves and corselet, and
the glorious auburn hair dressed simply, tied away
at the back of the head with flowing black ribbon.
Pretty Miss Fitzmaurice Gill is no longer in this
co. to fascinate with her charming villainies, and
Frances Ross and Adela Kerr now divide the
wickednesses between them. A 3 usual, Mrs.
Bland Holt comes to the rescue, and this time in
a magnificent long white cloak lined with pale-
orange ; but I like her best in her natty navy-blue
riding-habit with a red ribbon on her whip.
Arthur Styan plays the fool—remarkably well
considering the difficulty it must be. And Walter
Baker’s fatal beauty—which, by the way, is in-
clining dangerously near the full-blown just now
—plays havoc with two women’s hearts, a fate the
gods always reserve for their Walter.
At Her Majesty’s, on Saturday, a stand-up house
watched the beginning of theendof the Comic Opera
co., which goes to make room for Nance O’Neil
in “ Fedora ” next Saturday. The “ Gondoliers ”
ran merrily. All the same, it is impossible to re-
sist a sigh over some of the leading female voices,
which vary between the thin, the painfully
cracked, and the absolutely broken, and, with a
weird but mistaken devotion to each other, nearly
all warble away half a tone sharp.
By the way—but not apropos of anyone in this
co., of course—do you notice what a spirit of
philanthropy is taking root in theatre audiences?
Time was when singing and elocuting we listen to
politely, nowadays, would have been kindly,
firmly, and unmistakably hooted into the wings.
I suppose it is increased civilisation, and a more
pitiful humanity, but, after all, the pain inflicted
on the hooted ones in those bad, old, remorseless
days was nothing compared to the anguish of the
many who sit and suffer toothache in the ear, in
these awfully polite new days of ours. It is not
so very long, either, since a well-known actress had
to ring down the rag because the house would call
out “Carlo, Carlo,” and whistle, whenever hubby’s
broken English (Carlo, in “Jealousy”) drove it to
desperation.
Broken English, indeed ! And now ? Well,
now we swallow murdered English out of broken
voices, and, falling down into the very depths of
an inconceivably ghastly weakness, give them an
encore l
Returning—yes, I mean returning —to “ The
Gondoliers. Miss Chetwynd, a little newcomer
with a piquant smile, played Tessa. Her small,
piping voice won’t bear discussion, but her danc-
ing is magnetising; she acts with abandon, and
how anyone so small can sweep such wide and
willowy curtsies is something to think of after-
wards. Carrie Moore, with a fisher-blue cap
on her dark hair, sparkles demurely as Gia-
netta. Pretty Cecil Englehart, who is work-
ing off some of her stiffness, goes tamely along as
Casilda. But Jenny Opie cuts a fine figure as
the Duchess of Plaza Toro, unlimited, in white hair
dressed high, and black lace flounces over yellow
silk, and a long chinee silk train. As for the men,
Mr. Beattie gets a nightly encore for Antonio’s
song and dance and shows himself to great
advantage on his toes. Wallace Brownlow is the
same large thing of beauty in the red plush jacket
and blameless white tights of the palace scene,
and warbles away in as rich a voice as his very
best days knew about the “Ministers of Steet.”
But Kenningham’s throat-troubles hamper him
evidently, and where he cain’t get into his high
notes even with a falsetto contrivance, his round
face and persuasive smile are turned on hard to
disarm his listeners.
On Friday the “ Banjo ” opened his lecture-
season at Centenary Hall to a splendid ultra-
society house. The problem of how to dress for a
lecture, or the strife between hats-in-a-hall and
bare-shoulders-without-an-orchestra, was brushed
aside, by a much-dressed crowd, which got into
evening clothes with one accord and set up a
ripple of color all over the front seats. “ Banjo ”
wore a dress-suit and kept the house waiting just
about 15 minutes—just long enough to create the
admirable impression that the man who can ride
out carelessly into full Mauser range isn’t neces-
sarily the hero who can face an army of toupees
with straight knees.
However, at a quarter past eight he was seen
mounting the steps. He received a magnificent
welcome. The palms and flowers on the dressed-
up platform quivered with the thunder of claps
and cheers that burst out over the lecturer,
nervously clasping and unclasping his hands.
A successful writer cannot be expected to turn
into a highly successful lecturer at a bound ; and
•seeing that “Banjo” Paterson takes high rank
among Australian verse-makers one mitigates
one’s first impressions, and looks rather to the
time when a few weeks at , the business
will develope his dramatic instinct, and enable
his work to “get over the footlights.” With
such a wealth of experience at his command he is
bound to win the interest of his audiences —which,
by the way, have been consistently large and
fashionable for his three lectures to date.
What a year this has been for mourning ! One
after the other, nearly everyone in Society has
gone into black for some relative departed. The
death of Premier Lyne’s father, who passed away
last week at his Tasmanian home after a long
illness, will add another to our many mourning
families. The Premier’s intense devotion to his
father was well known. Indeed, for several
months past, whenever an opportunity offered, be
used to rush off to Tasmania for a day or two,
fearing at each visit that he was seeing his father
for the last time. By the way, Mrs. Lyne has her
own way about her share of the title. Seeing that
she meant it, her friends no longer ring the ob-
noxious “Lady” on her consistently shrinking
ear.
I have just received a copy of a fascinating-
looking publication in olive-green covers, which
turns out to be the much-talked-of “Twentieth
Century Cookery and House Decoration Book,”
by Mrs. Fred. Aronson (“Thalia” of the Sydney
Mail). Next I shall be able to tell you what
manner of a book it is inside.
About the Brisbane Poster-ball:—
Lady Lamington “ might have been anybody.” She
wore a common or garden-nurse’s frock, except that it
was of blue-and-white check and represented the dress of
the nursing staff for her Ladyship’s hobby—the New
Hospital for Women. The apron wasn’t new and had a
big ironmould on it, but the cap was French in shape and
admittedly becoming. The vice-regal visitor, Miss Bris-
coe, appeared as Heidsieek’s Champagne. Her dress was
not too effective, consisting, as it did, of a black skirt,
with gold bodice and a miniature bottle worn in the hair,
but the wearer seemed to enjoy the show immensely.
The ball was largely favored by Aboriginals.” Jimmy
Governor was there—and keeps his incognito still. No-
body was seen dancing with him, for there was just a
chance that he might have been the real thing. The
Brisbane papers have, for some reason, refrained from pub-
lishing a description of Miss Sharpe’s dress, which repre-
sented Adams’ Sweep. There was a flutter in the dove-
cotes when it was known that Adams would give a hand-
some dress to the wearer and £5O to the Hospital if his
sweep were represented, but the flutter subsided when
women recollected that such an advt. was illegal. How-
ever, the lady mentioned compromised matters by
appearing as “ A Clean Sweep ” in a smart frock em-
broidered with the name of Adams and carrying a new
broom in her hand—a distinction with no difference, surely.
Beautiful Mrs. Gilbert Wilson had a good mate in Mrs. J.
A. Robertson, regal-looking women both, and gowned
handsomely—Mrs. Wilson for Paling and Mrs. Walker’s
dressmaking establishment, and the other for Carew,
Gardner and Billington. Apropos of Mrs. Wilson, she
was a lovely woman 20 years ago with a splendid voice ;
she is just as lovely to-day, and her voice has gained a
sympathetic note, which it lacked of yore. Think of the
flower-like girls who have bloomed, and wilted long ago,
and are nothing to look at ; and they were almost
babes-in-arms when Miss Carandini was a tall slip of a
girl just going into long frocks.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Marshall Hall’s sixth concert at the Town
Hall, on Saturday afternoon, was a thing of
beauty—in a musical sense, of course—and not too
persistently good to be enjoyable. The con-
ductor’s Prelude to Alkestis, always worth hear-
ing, prepared one’s feelings for Beethoven’s Violin
Concerto in D., which radiates from an artless
tune of the tum-te-tumpty order. The effect
would have been finer but for the uneasiness of
the chief fiddler, Franz. Dierich. In between the
‘ tumpties ’ he gazed su/.piciously at his fiddle and
inclined his ear to tl/e woodwork like a doctor
listening for lung complaints. The last half of
the concert was Schubert’s symphony in C
major, the ‘magnificent joy’ whereof got a
whole page of explanation on the printed pro-
gramme. Marshall-Hall gave this masterpiece a
great character. Its charm, it seems, is so unques-
tionable that the critics of yore ‘ declared it long
and wearisome, crude in form and wanting in
science,’ and the ‘tortured critic ’ of to-day com-
plains ‘ Good God ! Not a bar of decent counter-
point.’ On Saturday a few people who didn’t look
like critics made their escape before the finish of
Schubert, so probably they went to find a point of
bar counter in the vulgar world outside. Previous
to the four-act symphony, Madame Slapoffski
gave ‘ Elizabeth’s Prayer,’ from ‘ Tannhauser,’ in
a well-trained, well-worn voice, and with just
as much dramatic earnestness as the occasion re-
quired. A platform vocalist in a matinee hat
mustn’t pray very, very hard. Madame S. (who
has not come out with her husband as a member
of the Musgrove Opera Co.) got four recalls and
three large lots of floral tributes for her trouble.
She did her best to look surprised at the f.t.’s,
though they had been ranged conspicuously along
the side of the hall before she started to sing.
“Marshall Hall is not the only example of
poetry getting a man into trouble. Another
prominent citizen seriously compromised himself
by writing a book of verses, praising the roses and
raptures of a free unfettered single life, in com-
parison with the humdrum monotony and weari-
some bondage of wedded life. This volume was
brought up and used against him, when he
subsequently wanted to marry a young lady
whose pa strongly objected to the opinions of the
rhymer. By-and-bye, however, when the bard
had bought up and withdrawn from circulation
the offending volume, and fully apologised for his
atrocious sentiments, the stern parent relented
and gave him his daughter, conditionally on his
publishing poems in praise of marriage and its
advantages.
“The other week it was stated in this part of
The Bulletin that a certain angry parent had
run away from home because his daughter was
marrying into the wrong religion. The reason
given for the hasty departure was incorrect and
unfair, it must be admitted, to paterfamilias and
other members of his family. They are on
friendly terms with both religions, and have
indeed, always shown a disregard for creed bigotry
in matters matrimonial. J
“A. novelty that went off like wildfire at the
Children s Hospital Bazaar was a purse which
opens, at a spring, to reveal a powder-puff. One
carries the fraudulent purse up the sleeve, in the
crown of the hat, or in the pouched blouse.
‘‘ Tl >e re was less fun than expected at that Town
Hall Women’s Suffrage meeting. Slight, dark,
pretty Vida Goldstein, who has spent and is
spending the days of her youth in the service of
sister Woman, looked neat in brown suit and pink
vest, and said what she had to say (nothing-
startling) in a clear, quiet, distinct voice. When
Miss Vida gets a night off from the Cause she
goes to dances in pretty frocks and annexes part-
ners galore. The only other she-speaker at the
women’s meeting was a diminutive suburban
matron, a prominent temperance leader, whose
cutty skirts bared her boots to the public gaze
and whose voice didn’t carry any distance.
Though she’s been round the world, I believe, as
a speechifying temperance delegate, oratory isn’t
her forte. At drawing-room teetotal meetings,
where she queens it as a public character, Mrs. Z.
is fond of saying: 4 Dear sisters, I know you’re
saying to yourselves, “ I have no gifts, I could
never speak like Mrs. Z. ! ” Dear sisters,
we don’t expect you to come forward. Every-
one hasn’t public talents, but be sure there’s a quiet
little corner for you to do your humble task.’ Yet
poor Mrs. Z.’s small voice is no argument against
female suffrage, for some of the men-speakers at
the same meeting were quite inaudible. When
opponents were invited to argue, only two flies
accepted the bidding to the spider’s parlor. The
first was elderly, and had a big head. I think he
said * there was plenty stuffing inside it ’ and that
he owned ‘lO in family,’ but the suffragists were
cat-calling, so I can’t be sure. The other adver-
sary was youngish, so the audience chaffed him as
a presumptuous toddler, but his courage was full-
grown, anyhow, for when a speaker asked all
those who classed their wives, sweethearts, See., as
lunatics, criminals, and sich, to hold up their
hands, his lone, lorn paw ‘trod the winepress
alone.’ The only remark of this hero, not drowned
in the row, was: 4 1 don’t wish to degrade women
to the level of men.’

“Surgeon E. M. James, formerly of Melbourne,
who died in London the other day after years of
absence from Australia, made his money (he left
£70,000) out of silver-speculation superadded to ■
medical practice. In the early sixties, he was ■
4 Resident ’ at Melb. Hospital, and when he left
that institution he became partner of the massive •
and stately Garrard. When the delicate Mar-
chioness of Normanby, wife of a former Vic.
Governor, left for England, Dr. James accom-
panied her as physician, and never returned to 1
Australia. He said he wouldn’t come back to •
Melbourne during the lifetime of his brother, who •
lived in London, and to whom he was greatly at-
tached, but his brother has outlived him. Mrs.
James is a daughter of the late John Mathieson,.
for a generation manager of the Bank of Victoria.

pathy. _ Several engagements, temporary andJ

Various Queenslanders propose a monu-
ment to the late Thomas M‘llwraith. What’s
wrong with the Q.N. Bank ?
John Harper, principal traffic manager of
N.S.W. railways, began life as sub-editor under
Garnet Walch, then—more than 30 years ago—
conducting a Parramatta paper.
Salomons and Barton, Q.C.’s, have brought
back “ catch ” phrases from the English Courts.
The former now says, “Will you in the court
swear, &c.,” and the latter, “Now let me ask you
this question.”
Apropos of the N.S. Wales J.P. nuisance.
A member of the Leg. Council sends around a
letter from a fashionable tailor offering to supply
him with a dress-suit gratis if the M.L.C. will
get him appointed to the Commission of the Peace.
The inevitable Wallington, who will make
his next appearance in Sydney as private sec. to
the Gov.-General, is sailing from England by the
Britannia. It would have sounded better on
Wally s part to come by the Australia, but, no
matter.
Chief-Justice Way, of S.A., is alleged to
have got himself into an even more serious official
mess than that which resulted from his secret
dodgery in connection with the Commonwealth
Bill. Details will doubtless leak out in Parlia-
ment later on.
Sydney artist A. J. Fischer, who went re-
cently to London, writes amongst other things :
“Only one thing is cheap here —Beer, and that
concerns me not. One of the chief and strongest
exhibitors at the Royal Water-Colour Society is
Australian H. S. Hop wood.”
Sculptor Bertram Maekennal visits Aus-
tralia in a few weeks, arriving about Cup time.
Maekennal will be able to admire his own bust of
Melba in the National collection, where it is finely
placed in the Stawell Gallery. He has to spend a
couple of weeks, at least, in Sydney, to execute an
English commission.

everyone else’s cousin or mother-in-law, or some-
thing of the sort. Sometimes this is awkward.

The Treriwith-Maloney squabble, in Vic.
Assembly, reached, its acute stage when the big
man “desired to point out” that the little man
“had scarcely ever adopted a method which was
calculated to bring about the objects he had in
view.” Then he went on to refer to the little
’un’s foolish proceedings in India a few years ago,
and was countered with a stinging allusion to a
matter which caused the House to cry “ Shame 1 ”
on Maloney—who, however, declared that his
allegation was true and needed ventilation.
Whei Archbishop “Yes-No” Francis
Redwood returned to M.L. from Rome, after
receiving the pallium, some of the members of his
family who met him at the railway station went
down piously on their marrow-bones to kiss his
ring. “Old Harry” Redwood was keeping in
hand a frisky tandem team, intended for the con-
veyance of his illustrious brother, and regarded
the reception most disdainfully. At la t. irritated
by the curvetting of his horses, he yelled out to
the Archbishop, “ Now then, Frank, when you’re
done with that blanky nonsense, jump up. I’m
d d if I’ll wait another minute.”
N.S.W. Chief Justice Dowling, father of
ex-District Court J udge Dowling, still resid-
ing in a Sydney suburb was a Cockney
by birth, and became intimately asso-
ciated in law matters with the illus- «
trious Lord Brougham. Though
Brougham obtained for Dowling
the appointment to the N.S.W.
Bench, he was opposed to his emi-
grating, and assured him that a
front place at the English Bar was
a certainty. But Dowling was
impatient, and preferred a new field
away from the crowded Bar of the
England of that day.

onti of the wags of the

If “ Banjo ” Paterson wanted a testimonial
to his demeanour on the battlefield he could get it
from the Victorian Colonel Hoad. The Colonel
says that “Banjo” seemed to have no notion of
physical fear, and was constantly in the thick of
the fighting. He was also one of the most popular
correspondents on the field ; officers liked him,
a,ld tne correspondent who is popular with the
officers has a good time.

that he was a returned “hand” from Botany
Bay, and the Home Secretary immediately
ordered his return to Sydney,

Paris Nesbit, Q.C. (S.A.), visited Melb.
some years ago and delivered a “lecture on
Tennyson ”in the Town Hall. When he had got
to the middle of a very discursive address, he
exclaimed “Oh, the flowers that bloom in the
Spring, tra-la-la ! We will now all adjourn for a
drink.” On returning to the platform after the
lapse of 15 minutes, the moderate audience had
dwindled to the hall-keeper and a reporter on the
look-out for a comic par.
Paris Nesbit identifies himself with Jesus
Christ now The Adelaide Q.C. is a striking per-
sonality. He has a surplus of inches over Oft.,
and his big frame is crowned with a particularly
handsome face. Quite a number of the democratic
measures in 8. A. Statute-Book were conceived in
Nesbit’s brain. He is a fervid supporter of Coun-
cil reform, although latterly he has made blazing
attacks upon Kingston, present leader of the re-
form movement. Nesbit advocated law-reform, too,
including the appointment of a permanent Crown
Defender in criminal cases. And in the latest
issue of his paper, Morning, he admits love affairs
(among the M’s only !) with one Mary, two Mar-
garets, one Mabel (semi-platonic), one Martha,
and some three, or four, or five Mauds. So it’s
pretty mean of him to be denouncing Kingston’s
relations with women. Nesbit’s paper, Morning,
is a treasure to students of megalomania. For
example, watch his Ego parade in his answer to
the medical superintendent of Parkside Asylum:—
I do not remember saying that I was head and
shoulders above everyone else in Adelaide in intellect;
but if I did say so I submit that,, though it may have
shown that I was very conceited, it dal not show that I
am. insane. Many persons of much ability have said
much the same thing, or used expressions which mean
much the same thing, as, for instance, Mr. David Blair, a
friend and contemporary of Henry Pa. kes and of Daniel
Deniehy, who told my wife some years ago, as I was in-
formed by her and believe, that I had the finest intellect
in Australia, and told Mrs. Lane, of the Argus Hotel, in
Melbourne, as 1 was informed by her and believe,
that my conversation was divine; Dr. Boulger, who
told Mr. Wharton, now the editor* of the Cum-
berland Times, as I am informed by him and
believe, that I had the brightest and keenest
intellect of any man he had ever met; Mr. Sowden,
leader-writer for the Register, who, I believe, is the
author of a statement in the Kapunda Herald that I
am a giant physically and mentally ; Frederick Robert-
son, leader-writer for the Advertiser, who wrote to me :
“ Interested as an Australian native myself in watching
your brilliant career, I cannot but feed that you are one of
those men of whom these young States may well be proud.
With natural talent of a high order, and broad and liberal
culture, you combine a large and healthy patriotism,
qualities which give value to the public services you
render, and will, I hope, before long be exer-
cised in a wider sphere.” Henry Alierdale Grainger,
who, as I was informed by a friend (I believe it
was the Honorable John Alexander Oockburn, but am
not sure), and as I believe, told him there was only
one genius in South Australia, and that was Nesbit.
James Hague, senior member for Barossa, who, shortly
after I was discharged from the North-terrace Asylum,
against the wish and opinion of one Dr. Allan Campbell,
said publicly, in my presence at an election meeting, that
he knew no man so well qualified to do credit to South
Australia in the Federal Convention as Mr. Nesbit. The
Right Honorable Samuel James Way, who, as I am in-
formed bv the Honorable John Hannah Gordon and be-
lieve, said that I was a closer reasoner than Mr. Symon.
The Rev. Charles Marson, who told my cousin, Edith
Nesbit, the Poet, as I am informed by her and believe,
that I was the most distinguished man in South Australia.
The Honorable John Hannah Gordon, who has said in
my presence that I was one of the ablest men in the
Southern Hemisphere. My sister, Mrs. Benham, who has
said in my presence that I am like Gulliver among the
Liliputians ; and Mr. Harold Desbrowe Annear, of Mel-
bourne, Architect Associate of the Royal Institute of
Victorian Architects, who told Mr. John Longstaff, the
celebrated painter, in my presence, that I was in Adelaide
a giant among pigmies ; and other persons too numerous
to mention. And I humbly submit that, if I am to be
kept in confinement, the said David Blair, Dr. Boulger,
Mr. Sowden, Frederick Robertson, Henry Alierdale
Granger, James Hague, the Right Honorable Samuel
James Way, Rev. Charles Marson, John Hannah Gordon,
and Harold Desbrowe Annear, and the said Mrs. Benham,
and the said other persons too numerous to mention, should
for tlie sake of consistency be sent here to keep me com-
pany. I did not say that my said translations were superior
to the originals, but that the workmanship thereof was on
the whole equal to that of the originals, and that some
verses were better. I have a better instrument than
Goethe or Schiller had, in that English is a stronger and
better language than German. Mr. Addison, a German
scholar, who says that the translation of “The Descent
into Hell,” written by Goethe, is sublime, and transcends
the original ; by Mr. Philip Falk, who says it is sublime ;
by Mr. Siekmann, a German, who says it is magnificent;
by Mr. Rupert Ingleby, a German scholar, who says that
my translation of Schiller’s “ Gods of Greece ” is better
than the original; and by Mr. Alfred Loeser, a German,
who says that my translations sounded better than the
originals. Even if lam mistaken in thinking that my
translations are good, I humbly submit that the conceit of
an author is no reason for locking him up in a lunatic
asylum.
Great Nesbit! ! !

Mainly about S. A.'s
boss millionaire:
J. H. Angas, cattle and sneep
Dreeuer, philanthropist and millionaire, is one of
Australia’s richest men. When George An gas
practically “ founded” S.A. in ’36 he sent out the
future millionaire to manage his estate, now one
of the largest and most valuable in S.A. The Angas
family also founded the S.A. Co., which has been reaping
fat dividends from the land-toilers for 65 years. When
old George Angas died he provided in his will that
£lO,OOO a year should be spent in charity, and much of
this has gone in founding scholarships at Adelaide Uni-
versity. J. H. Angas is now 77 years old.

ifi the time-honored fashion, when the Bible his
mother gave him turned a Boer bullet from his
heart, recalls a couple of similar instances. Melb.

lation notebook, when fired at by burglars and
badly wounded ; while (listen to this !) a youthful
burglar brought down by another Melb. detective
was protected from an otherwise fatal pistol-shot
by a packet of indecent French cards !

S.A. This ambition is also held to explain Gor-
don’s definite announcement that he will not have
anything to do with the Federal elections.
The Social Purity Party in Wellington
(M.L.), are bitter over George Fisher’s re-election
in the teeth of the combined opposition of them-
selves and the Tories. Fisher, who has ever been
the “black beast” of Wellington journals, is
always to the fore in opposing any weakening of
the libel laws. So A. R. Atkinson—an “ Oxfawd ”
grad.—the rising hope of the S.P. party, has
actually circulated a draft libel bill, intitled “An
Act to prevent the truth being spoken about
George Fisher.”
When Henry Kendall was a young man
and articled clerk to a solicitor, at Grafton,
N.S.W., he undertook to deliver a lecture at tlie
local School of Arts. He went to much trouble in
preparing his address, and the preliminaries over,
faced an expectant audience. Through “stage-
fright ” he was utterly unable to speak a single
word, and stood on the platform dumb-founded.
After a painful pause, attorney Michael, who was
in the chair, walked up to him, told the dazed
young fellow that he had better postpone the ad-
dress, and sympathetically filled the breach with
a short lecture himself. Kendall’s speech was
never delivered.
A brother-medico obituarises the lafcd Dr.
Gray, of Vic. Health Department:
B.: Died September 18, aged 47, Dr. Tom Gray, of Vic.
Health Department—best of fellows and most tactful of
officers —Oft. 2in., 18st., and apparently good for 100 years.
But acute pneumonia cum overwork proved too much for
even his robust constitution. Well-known and liked
all over the State, Gray’s consummate tact and know-
ledge of the world smoothed over innumerable difficulties
with municipalities, and he had an irresistible way of
“blarneying” stubborn councillors into reason. In fea-
tures and build he was extremely like John L. Sullivan,
and when that truculent celebrity arrived in Melbourne
some years ago an admiring crowd followed Gray about
for hours, with occasional outbursts of cheering. Prog-
nathous persons slapped him on the back, and
told him that he’d lick a room-full of champions,
and, after many vain disclaimers, the doctor was fain to
take refuge in a shop whence he escaped over the back
fence, while the self-appointed retinue waited patiently
in front and commented upon the champion’s unnecessary
modesty. The resemblance did not stop at externals, by
the way, for, as a student, Gray was such a noted boxer
that Tom Ball, the Edinburgh" fistic mentor of his day,
implored him to go into the ring professionally. Indeed,
despite his normally gentle disposition, Gray had the true
fighting spirit backed by an abnormally powerful and
active physique. Moreover, he was a Liverpool man—
and there they make ’em tough. Gray had a distinguished
professional career. Soon after his qualification he had
charge of a big small-pox outbreak in the Isle of Man,
and made a name for himself over it. Then after a
period of sea-service with the Orient Co., he joined the
Immigration service and soon became Super, of Tas. Im-
migration Department, holding in addition many other
important appointments in connection with lunacy and
public health matters. In 1892 he became Assistant
Medical Inspector of Vic., and had he lived would almost
certainly have become Govt. Medical Officer on the
approaching retirement of Dr. Shields. A proposal is on
foot to erect a memorial over his grave.

Aboriginalities.
“ Uloola ” on women workers :
Writer’s mother is now (at 50) a confirmed invalid,
through “ working like a horse ” in early life. Cutting
bundle-wood, loading it, and carting it to Sydney (10
miles in this case) was commonly women’s work in those
days. Sometimes they would get bogged, have to partly
unload, take the rest on to firm ground, and go back for
the remainder; and this would sometimes happen several
times on the trip in wet weather. Think of the present-
day female weed of the Australian cities essaying that
sort of thing I Then in starting cultivation in timbered
country, grubbing, “breaking up,” and hauling the logs
together for “ burning off ” was all women’s work. And,
in between times, they reared families of up to 16
children t
A Far Back picture :
Struck a boundary-rider’s hut out West lately and,
hearing a kiddie’s voice shouting lustily somewhere
about, I went round to the back and found a 5-year-old
nipper chained by the waist to a brigalow sapling, and
amusing himself with an old Epsom-salts box and eight
bottles yoked-up to it for bullocks. The youngster was
flogging away at the bottles and his language was steep
—even for out-back. He stopped for a moment, cursed
at me vigorously, and resumed his “ driving.” I cantered
along a bit and met the nipper’s mother, axe on shoulder,
coming out of the scrub. Told me th’ old man
was away on th’ fence and she’d been cuttin’
scrub for th’ milkers. At the hut, while she was cutting
down a sheep in a manly sort of way, I asked her why
she kept the kid chained up. “ Blime ! ” said the lady of
the mulga, gripping the butcher’s knife between her
teeth, “ ’cause I ain’t got no time ter shepherd ’im. But
he’s all right, you bet. There ain’t no flies on him, my
oath I Well, g’da.v, mate, I’ve got couple o’ panels o’
fencin’ to fix up fer lie old man, an’ I’m in a hellofa-
msh ! ” Rural simplicity with a vengeance I ”

“TRADE FOLLOWS THE FLAG.”
It all made him feel quite at home. The last hours of the Rev. J. Smith,
of Birmingham, missionary, deceased.
is a kid anecdote with a difference. We were in
an open drag serving as a coach between a new
alluvial field and a small mining town, and the
driver, being late, was sending his horses at a
smart pace up a long rise. It was night, the nar-
row track was cut through thick bush, on either
side rose clear black walls of clustering white-
gum, and before us, on the apex of the rise, hung
an enormous yellow moon seeming so close that 1
fancied one might almost have cracked its lumin-
ous face with a stone. Opposite me sat a miner’s
wife with a delicate-looking child of about six in
her arms. I noticed the youngster staring
amazedly at the moon, and then she hid her face
in her mother’s breast with a vague expression of
terror. A few seconds later smash went the drag
into a stump, and over went the show. The first
words I heard were the whimpering of the child :
“ Oh, mummer. I knew we’d run into the moon ! ”

Concerning Bundaburra, a well-known
sexagenarian Forbes (N.S.W.) aboriginal, there are
numerous yarns. The Forbes police were anxious
to catch the publicans who sold drink to the abo-
rigines, and a constable, meeting Jacky with a
cargo in, offered him half-a-crown to tell where he
had got the liquor. “Half-a-crown,” said Jacky,
as he took the coin, “ Why I wouldn’t put poor
old Mrs. Brown away for a quid 1 ” Another time
Jacky was “ up” for drunkenness and “righteous,”
and the P.M. gave him a week. “A week?”
echoed the astonished Jacky. “Can’t do it,
boss—haven’t got time. I’ve got a job to go to
down the Lachlan to-morrow. He had to find
the time, however.

“Peter K.” : To “ H. J. Mac” (8.,
15/9/’00). The increase in number of gulls at
Lakes George and Bathurst is not due to breeding,
for gulls will never “ sit ” far from the sea-shore.
What attracts the birds is the presence of a
peculiar sand-fly, for the gull of the Australian
region lives almost wholly upon insects, and this
sand-fly is a rare dainty. If “H.J.M.” will
watch he will find that the birds arrive and depart
in flocks ; that the flock will stay only a few days,
and that as soon as a fresh crowd comes the first
ot clears out.
“Barney Bul-
lock”: The station-
hands who are
unable to doctor
themselves success-
fully look to the
squatter for advice
and medicine. The
average bushman
owns a collection
of alleged medical
works which make
a speciality of ner-
vous debility, and
which he lias re-
ceived gratis from
the “World-Re-
nowned Specialists,
Brown and Smith.”
He studies these,
books, and is al-
ways ready to diag-
nose and treat any
disease which
comes his wav.
When the result is
unsatisfactory the
patient goes up to
the boss and details
his symptoms, and
the boss acts ac-
cording to the mul-
titude of his
wisdom or the
teachings of his
own debility-book.
A boundary-rider
Suffering from
things in general
went up to the
homestead a few
evenings ago for a
cure. Mr. Stay the
was not at home,
but Mrs. Smythe
suggested that per-
haps she could be
of service. She prescribed certain pills and
a drink of hot tea. He obeyed, and got
much worse. His mates decided that a wrong
medicine had been given, and as the pills were
injurious, they would administer an emetic.
Salt and water were tried and proved ineffective ;
next a large dose of soap and water was given ;
then a rope was tied round the patient’s shins anil
he was suspended from a rafter on the verandah
with results highly satisfactory. You may not
find the highest of medical skill in the bush, but
by the aid of |oap and water, a rope, and his
debility book Clancy of the Overflow-of-Beer
pulls through many difficulties.

“H.G.” to “Critic” (8., 28/7/’00) : I
have seen hundreds of bullocks, teamsters, &c.,
&c., on the roads, and have noted that during the
greater part of the journey they drive from which-
ever side the team happens to cast its paltry
shadow on. Dust and a blinding out-back sun
will send the poor wretches anywhere. Once saw
a bullocky drive his team from neither side, but
from the bar of a bush pub. He gurgled out (un-
consciously) the nasal and necessary curses
at “Sheet-o’-bark,” “Viper,” “Bloogum,”
“Thistle,” &c., &c., during intervals of “putting
down” his two long beers —then went out and
found the waggon half-a-mile down the road.
The bullocks heard him.

Dear Bulletin. —Can any of your readers
explain the decay of the myall and yarran ? Hun-
dreds of square miles of the Western black-soil
country are strewn with the dead trunks and
branches -of these valuable trees. They have
neither been cut nor ring-barked, but, with roots
exposed, lie on the surface as though blown down
by a hurricane.—D.B.

“ Matabele ” : Re fire-
making by blacks. Have
never seen Australian
blacks at it, but in Rho-
desia I have at times been
entirely dependent on my
Malaka boys’ ability in
that line. A pointed stick
of dry hardwood pressed
firmly into a hole in a
piece of dry softwood by
one boy is rapidly twirled
by a second, who pulls
alternate ends of a piece
of string, passed once
round it. A little pow-
dered grass or dust out of
a pod found on anthills
worked into the junction
of the two woods smokes,
and, with a little blowing,
burns in from 3min. to
smin. The use of the
string (quickly worked out
of mimosa bark) gives
about five times the pace
possible when using the
hands only, and pace is
everything. In cases of
no fire after about smin,
work, have seen boys
throw away the sticks and
start again with fresh
ones.

“Via”: Re climbing. If “ Derford ”
(B. 28/2/’00) wants a hair-raiser, let him visit
Hawkesbury River at the telegraph-line crossing
just after a big break. Big mast there stands
85ft., (two poles stepped) and no nig. up to date
can be persuaded to ascend with the climbing
ropes. Journey up takes 40 mins, for good line-
repairer. On the Burdekin, (N.Q.) crossing, the
pole is 73ft., and I saw a nig. refuse £1 to take a
wire up (after the line-man had slipped and
strained himself.) King Billy earnestly remarked :
“ B’pose slip lika tha pfeller and breakim cobra
(head) me no catch’em tha pfeller cabawn (good)
twenty chillen by-in-bye no fear.”
* * * *
After this, from “ F.A.R.,” the climbing
nigger will be given a rest: —
Re Australian aboriginals climbing trees by cutting
notches and using' a vine as support. I have often seen
a black in New England mount a stringy-bark and strip
six or eight 10ft. sheets of bark off it, having his gin to
steady them as they slipped off, to prevent splitting
through falling backward. Again, the Clarence, Rich-
mond and Tweed blacks are splendid climbers, their
children being taught to climb from infancy. Mustering
cattle once, on an Upper Richmond station, we had been
out from daylight, with nothing to eat, when the collie
dogs hunted a big iguana up a tree which, having had its
bark peeled by lightning, was as smooth as glass. The
tree was about GOft. high, yet one of the black boys, hav-
ing stripped himself naked and using the whip-thong as a
“ vine,” followed the goanna right up to the top, caught
him by the tail, and jerked him off, when the others soon
had him on a fire, and all hands had a good feed. This
was accomplished without the aid of notches.

“ H.S.” to The Bulletin
A much-needed move was Mr. Kethel’s recent deliver-
ance fn N.S.W. Leg. Council about the urgent necessity
for the “ better protection and conservation of the State
Forests and the re-afforesting of the bare and depleted
areas with indigenous and exotic timber-trees.” It’s not
hard to forecast the result of the reckless timber-destruc-
tion that has so long been going on. Valuable Crown-
lands clothed with fully-developed and highly-marketable
timber have been sold and leased for a mere trifle,
enriching the few at the expense of the million, fattening
tile private speculator and impoverishing the State
resources. A law is needed compelling every holder of
purchased land to plant at least one acre of timber trees
for every 100 acres lie owns; the trees to be chosen by the
Forestry Department and supplied by Govt. ; temporary
exceptions to be made where the land is naturally
timbered.

“ A.P.R.” : City friend Jimmy on sodden
dampers : —“The fust damper I ever cooked wus
a reg’lar thunderstriker. I rolled it outside —it
wus too heavy to lift. Some birds come along
soon an’ started pickin’ it, but they got their
beaks stuck, an’ couldn’t get ’em out. I caught
those birds. I caught 25 birds the same way that
day. Then a tribe uv ants come along an’ started
carryin’ away crumbs. After goin’ about three
inches all those ants stopped an’ wiped the sweat
off their brows with bits uv grass. One uv them
took a fit and then they all cleared out. I used
that damper fer a doorstep when it got dry.”
In the early ’Bo’s a C.Q. squat-
ter, through scarcity of labor, decided
to team his own wool to the nearest
railway. On the first day he passed
a number of teamsters, spelling under
a tree, who invited him to “Come
and have some tea, mate.” “ I’m no
mate of yours,” said the squatter —
“ I’m a gentleman driving my own
team ! ” —and he passed disdainfully
on. A mile or two further the gen-
tleman-teamster got bogged, and pre-
sently the teamsters he had passed
earlier caught him up. “My good
men,” he begged, “kindly help me
out of this mess.” “We ain’t no
good men! ” said one of them.
“We’re gentlemen a-driving our
own blanky teams! ” And they
drove.
Beyond Drummond Range, C.Q., the
goats that swarm about railway-fettlers’ camps take
fits of unrest at intervals and clear out, one and
all, travelling always to the East. So well is this
understood that owners in search of their strayed
“ kerosene-tin push ” never dream of looking west
of their camps.

“Pelican ” : Not generally known that a
curlew can be mesmerised, paralysed—what you
will—by riding fast around it in diminishing
circles. As you get close, the bird will squat
down so that you can pop off your horse and
grab it.
THE BOOZER’S TIP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Say, mister,” said the boozer, as he shook his empty
glass,
“ I guess you ought to do a roarin’ trade ;
I notice all the fellers have a drink before they pass,
No doubt a power of money should be made.”
The pubby looked important as he stroked his waistcoat
down,
He said : “ Of biz. I doesn’t want to speak,
But we’ve got near all the custom of this sleepy little
town,
And we use a dozen ’ogs’eads in the week ! ”
“ But, mister,” said the boozer, “you’ve to learn a bit, I
see,”
And lie watched the empty pewter like a cat ;
“ By filling up my measure you can get a tip from me
How to sell, I think, a dozen more than that ! ”
The pubby filled the pewter, and the boozer drained the
cup,
Then unsteadily he ambled to the door,
*• When yer servin’ liquor, mister, fill the blanky glasses
up !
And that’s the way to sell a dozen more ! ”
ROBERT ALLAN.

Kuppers Elberfeld Lager.
Joseph Bosisto, formerly M.L.A. for
Richmond (Vic.), used to tell some queer stories
of “Flash Jack’s’’-party of bushrangers in Rich-
mond and Hawthorn in ’52 and ’53. Bosisto, in
’52, went from Adelaide to Richmond to open a
chemist s shop. But he couldn’t get a house, and
so engaged the stable of the Star and Garter
Hotel at the corner of Waltham-street. One
night a man, with a pistol in each hand, ran into
“ Bos.’s ” “shop,” from which Bos., without asking
any nasty questions, ran out and away towards
the police-station. Presently he heard horse-
hoofs after him pell-mell, and he was soon run
down by a trooper who stopped him. Bos. said
he hadn’t been doing anything, and asked what
was up, anyhow ? The trooper wanted to know
why he was running ? Bos. said he was putting
space between himself and an armed bushranger.
“ That’s the cove I want,” says the trooper, and
back the pair went to Bos.’s drug-stable. But the
bushranger was gone, and hadn’t taken either pills
or money. He had just run into
cover among Bos.’s bottles from
the pursuing bobby.
NAUTICAL ITEM.
SMALL BOY: “Hey,
Bill, what company
does this craft belong to with
the black funnel? ”
“Fortuna”: Re “bush surgery.” I
knew of a small pig that had been so badly gashed
by a big one that its liver protruded from the
wound. The owner pushed the liver in, and
stitched up the wound, but a week later he found
the wound opened up, with the liver again sticking
out, and, in the exposed part, mortified. A bush
“doctor” then cut off the protruding part,
stitched up the wound, and the animal recovered.
* * * *
“Well, Pat,” said the priest, “and how
did the crushing go ? ” “ Well, yer revenue, some
av the shtone wint purty well, an’ some av it wint
damn bad ; but we’ve saved the sand, an’ we’re
goin’ to send it down to the Footscray metalogical
works to get it consecrated.”
* * * •
“Imray”: Does “TheM‘Cooey” know
anything about some Murray cod having a
greenish-colored circle round the eye pupil, and
others having a golden yellow one ? There are
other distinctions in the shape of the head and
markings on the back, and fishermen always speak
of them as two different sorts.
* * * *
“H.Gr.”: Recently witnessed a curious
poultry-yard incident. Two cocks were having
a fight, when No. 1 managed to put his spur
through neck of No. 2, and, being unable to extract
it, hung head-downwards till No. 3 came up and
settled No. 1 by spur through head.

'Scotty ” : “ Yril ” (8., 12/7/’00), Belgian
and French-made chokebores are marked “ Aon
pour balle.” A wire cartridge might burst the gun
near the muzzle just as a 12 bullet will burst a
weak barrel “choked ” down to 14.
* * . V *
“ C.L.M.” : Should like “ W.D.V.” (8.,
8/9/’00) to let me know the bore of the gun with
which he made the shot recorded. Also maker’s
name and the loading of cartridges. I have used
many guns, but one that will drive a single No. 1
pellet to break a turkey’s wing and kill at 130
yards is a bit of a miracle.
* * * *
‘Yril”: Re wire-cartridges and choke-
bore guns:—
I used to be very successful at trap-pigeon-shooting
with a heavenly c.b. ; but, having on one occasion lent il
to a friend, was astonished at how the gun performed—
with him—kicking like a mule and missing every bird.
Every time he fired I heard the unmistakable “ zung ” of
a heavy body in motion, and, if the bird was low, there
was a spurt-up of dust beyond the traps. He re-
turned the gun to me (blood flowing freely from a kick-
cut on cheek-bone) with the verdict—“ I don’t think
much of her ; and, Lord, how she does kick !” “ What
cartridges ?” I asked. “My own. Loaded ’em myself;
3J drachms powder and ljoz. No. 4 shot—wire-cartridges.
I reckon I ought to have smashed every bird.” And so
he would—if he’d hit them ! ”
* * * »
“Pelican ’ : Re the fatal curiosity of
birds. Water-hens in undisturbed country can
always be draivn from a reed-bed if you sit down
and make a strange noise, by, for instance, knock-
ing two. bones together. Have had them walk up
to within a few yards.
* * * •
“P-”: Asked a man the other day what
the country up towards and in Orange Free State
is like ; for he is a good back-country Australian
who knows country when he sees it. “We were
tired out,” he said, “and, going by train, slept
till Bin the morning, and so got well into the
Karroo. Then a fellow-traveller put his head up
over the truck-side, looked round the country,
rubbed his eyes, looked again, and said ‘ Oh,
Hell! ’ And there you have the average Aus-
tralian’s idea of it.” If it weren’t for the police-
work, he says, we shouldn’t need to trouble much
about losing our bushmen.
* • • •
A certain pressman once started a com-
mission - agency in a Northern town. His
“ colonial experience ” was limited, and the local
wags soon found this out, and began placarding
absurd notices on his window. One day he would
have for sale “ 2000 wethers, heavy in lamb,” or
“1500 " ooth ewes.” The next day, it would be
“200 speyed bulls,” and so on. At last, he
packed his trunk in disgust.
“\P.H.C.” : “ Coolawine ” (8., 21/7/'00)
is undoubtedly the aboriginal name for the native
bear, always so-called by the blacks of Wollumbi
district. In fact, there is in said district a place
called “Coolawin”—meaning, I am told by the
blacks, “big bear,” on account of the former local
abundanoe of these animals.
* # * »
All the talk about what mules can do in
the rough parts of S’Africa has set the wealthy
owners of Momba (far western N.S. W.)
experimenting with the same animals.
Momba (N.S. Wales’s biggest sheep
run) is out-back from droughty
Wilcannia; and as Wilcannia
is on a river which is nowadays
mostly dry, the only way be-
tween Momba and the world is
via Broken Hill. So far com-
munication hasbeenmaintained
even in the worst of weather by
camels, which are bred on the
station ; but it is inconvenient
to shift greasy wool with them.
Hence the mule experiment.
Barr Smith, one of Adelaide’s
reputed millionaires, is about
the biggest holder in the Momba
co. ; but its real ruler is Peter Waite,
the late Thomas Elder’s pastoral
adviser.
Before Minister O’Sullivan had N.S.W.
State Treasury to draw upon he once proposed—
it was in a bad drought—to win fame by cannon-
ading rain from passing clouds. In Broken Hill
the thing was, in fact, actually tried under the
supervision of an artilllery-officer now serving in
S’ Africa. It failed. Interesting, therefore, to
learn that numerous associations are being formed
among land-owners in Italy to carry out the idea
more systematically ; and not only to precipitate
rain, but also to prevent threatened hailstorm

The London and Foreign Bible Society
was not long ago jubilant over the great demand
for bibles in an in j anc i district in China. But later-
on came the gruesome discovery that, as said
district is a centre of the shoe-making industry,
the Word was being freely used as sole (not soul)
“leather”.

Europe The prodigy, Frit,

no doubt, but he will always rank as a German,
notan Australian, in his native hemisphere. Una
Bourne, the South Melbourne girl, has never been

anyway “ little ” when she appeared at the first of

J° Uth f Be T'° wf r elc whotook upthetui-
tion of the damsel in ’9(5 (previous to then she had a
careful training from her sister), lays stress upon
the fact that his pupil’s success has been legiti-

artist of admitted talent and exceptional promise ;
sbe }, as done fine hand and brain work at
Scherek’s orchestral concerts—work that showed a
steady improvement as the months went on ; and
she is now fully qualified to go forth and develope
her talent—genius—in the musical circles of the
Old World. The date of her departure is not
fixed, nor has there been any newspaper talk of
farewell concerts for brainy, unpretentious Miss
Una, hut she is certainly going, and she will go to
the front of her profession in due course.

No notice ivill be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu -
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case ivill MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

They love a Lord of high degree :

In the Spring “ that tir&d feeling ”
Comes to one and comes to all,
As we see fresh terrors threaten
From each hoarding, fence, and wall.
A SONG of PRING.
In the Spring our Weather Prophets
Turn their optics toward the sky ;
'Twixt the two they’re sure to hit it,
One says “ wet ” and t’other “ dry.”
In the Spring the bilious party
For his liver-something takes.
In the Spring when rusticating
Keep a sharp look-out for^snake?.
In the Spring the party leader
Iterates in utt'ranee vague—
“ Splendid action, —Saved tho Empire,
Slew the rats acid squelghed the plague ! *
Jfl the Spring the boating season
Opens up off Sydney Cove.
Then the rash, unskilful lubber
Hires a boat and—see above.
. In the Spring a younglhan’s fancy
Turns to thoughts of love, of course.
Later on, perhaps in Autumn,
“Fancy ’’ would prefer divorce.
In the Spring the minor poet
Thinks he’s hit on something wwr.
“ Chestnut! ” ories the man of suiwuus
Has this e’er occurred to you•? ” -

MELBOURNIANA.


The latest Yankee invention is a method
of almost instantaneously removing a dead fowl’s
feathers by means of cross air-currents generated
by electric-fans moving at nearly 100 revolutions
per second.
George Hutchinson, who has just left
Maoriland again for S’Africa, where he has been
“spying out the land,” recently says in Christ-
church Press article: —
Behind the British lines, roosting on every fence, are
huge flocks of unclean birds, waiting till our soldiers
enable them to again sweep down on Johannesburg the
Golden.
And we are slaughtering Dutch peasants because
they objected to indiscriminately enfranchising the
sweepings of civilisation—the creatures whom
Hutchinson aptly styles “unclean birds.” But
the Rhodes gang must have cheap niggers.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they, will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
According to a letter recently received by
a Sydney resident from his father, a prominent
Italian Socialist, the sensational cablegrams pub-
lished by Sydney dailies about the 1800 arrests
over Humbert’s assassination and the terrorism
reigning in Italy, were all pure bunkum. The
only persons arrested were two members of the
staff of the Socialist journal L’A v anti, and they
were soon released. It is further stated that
Humbert’s death is deeply deplored by Monarch-
ists, Democrats, Republicans, and Socialists
alike, and that the senseless crime is looked upon
as a national calamity.

GOOD HEALTH NECESSARY TO HAPPINESS.
HOW TO ATTAIN IT.
Without exception health is the most essential
condition to perfect happiness, the chief bene-
diction of life. Without health labor is _ a
drudgery, and all the light of life is shaded and its
sweetness embittered. So strange is the law of
sympathy that if any member suffers, all the
members suffer. An injury to one is the concern
of all. Nature is harmony. Disease discord.
One cannot be happy when tortured by the aches
and pains of rheumatism. One cannot work
when the back is so lame and the kidneys so weak
from kidney disorders that any movement causes
agony or requires extra exertion. Thousands
have been cured by the use of Warner’s Safe Cure
for these troubles. Don’t hesitate, delay may
mean death. Read the following testimony as to
the efficacy of this medicine :

of medicines, but could find no relief until I used
Warner’s Safe Cure. I obtained such good results

The Case Against the Tote.
Opponents, reverend and otherwise, of the
Totalisator, are just now looking to Maoriland
for comfort. They have wearied of setting
up their old South Australian arguments to be
knocked over by the hard skittle-ball of Truth.
Rather more than two years ago the alleged
shocking example of S.A. was put before Vic.
Assembly by Frank Madden, the bookmakers’
friend. Burly Frank made a number of state-
ments concerning the influence of the Tote down
South, and these passed for facts until Chaffey
Baker, the parliamentary father of the machine
and chairman of the S.A. Jockey Club, was asked
to disprove them. Some extracts from Baker’s
letter to S’Rupert Clarke in contradiction of
Madden’s audacious statements will be found to
put the extinguisher upon the anti-Tote argu-
ments which have lately sprung from Maoriland.
He wrote from Adelaide (July, 1899): —
* * * •
That the totalisator has been a magnificent and un-
qualified success in S.A. from nearly every point of view
is incontestable and has never been doubted locally. It
has purified the turf from its worst enemies, enabled the
clubs to expend large sums of money in grandstands,
training and racing tracks, and conveniences for members
and the public, to gradually but surely increase the
stakes given, to diminish the percentage on the stakes
paid by the owners for entrance and acceptance fees, to
foster and encourage in every way legitimate and honest
sport, and to eradicate welching, and to suppress an undue
ind ulgence in betting and wagering. It has also diminished
crimes and offences. The mere perusal of the schedules
attached hereto referring to the transactions of the three
leading racing clubs prove most of these statements ; the
annual reports of the S.A. Commissioner of Police as issued
in the S.A. Blue Books in your Parliamentary Library (in
which special reference is made to the influence of the
totalisator) substantiate others. See also report from
Commissioner of Police, dated 6th July, 1898, sent to the
Premier of Victoria, and published in the Age, and letter
from Commissioner of Police to Mr. F. Madden, dated 28th
July, 1898, if Mr. Madden will produce same.
The practical impossibility of holding any race meeting
in S.A. without a license from the Govt, to use a total-
isator has entirely eradicated those objectionable
suburban everyday meetings which are not unknown in
Melb., and helped the premier club to control the number
and improve the character of country meetings, thus
placing racing in the position it ought to occupy, a manly
and wholesome sport, a means of occupation and living to
a few certainly—but not an institution to enable an army
of parasites to fatten on the public.
Mr. Madden states that at one time I was for the totali-
sator, at others against. He has been misinformed. I in-
troduced and passed the first Bill, entitled “An Act to
remedy some of the evils arising from betting at races,”
in 1879. Owing to the fact that the’ use of the machine
was not restricted by the Act, what to my mind was a
great evil arose. In consequence of the large profits
accruing from the use of the totalisator, the number of
objectionable race-meetings was largely increased—-pri-
vate persons established courses in all directions, and put
the profits into their own pockets. Had the Act not been
repealed we should have had a race-meeting every day of
the week. I advocated the repeal of this defective Act,
and shortly afterwards had charge of and helped to pass
the present Act, under which the use of the machine is
properly regulated and restricted.
Mr. Madden quoted from a speech made by me in which
it appears that in one year the amount invested in the
totalisator was less than in the year before, but he does
not mention that the speech was made in answer to a
clamor from cwners to raise stakes ; nor does he mention
the fact that the bad times we have had for the last few
years have of necessity had an effect on the spending
power of the people. In fact, the success of the totalisa-
tor and the prosperity of the racing clubs are perfectly
marvellous under the circumstances.
I admit that the owner who desires to back his horse
for large amounts at long odds and forestall the public
tt&.'iTiAffSiSi SgSSfcZt
tralia, even where the totalisator is not legalised—but- the
stakes will be so largely increased by its aid that the
Poetically obtain a large amount to nothing.
The v.R.C. would probably be able to add £20,000 to the
M e ib. Cup. What more can a man want than £21,000 to
his entrance fee ?
* * * *
The Maoriland case against the Tote is presented
for all it is worth by parson Joseph Nicholson,
of Geelong, who proudly claims to have been a
s^ u ent °f tjje betting-question ever since the dark
shadow or the machine first fell across his apos-
to]ic life . Such drivel as appeared in a ree P 6nt
Sydney Referee, over the signature of Alf.
Burton, is not worth considering in the light of
Re v - Nicholson’s recent letter to Melb. Age.
fo , r + evid f ce to
support his dogma, and what he can t say to pre-
Ber ve the ignoble army of hook-nosed parasites
from further legislation is not of any importance
i n conflict. His astounding industry simpli-
Acs matters. To dispose of Rev. Jos. Nicholson’s
crafty fallacies is to convince the Court of Common
Sense that Ikey Mo has no claim to toleration.
Here are some extracts from the good man’s latest
epistle to the Age :
For years past the conviction has deepened in Maori-
land that-as Mr. Hutchison put it in Parliament-" the
totalisator was simplv proving itself to be a well meant
but terribly mistaken experiment-something like another
Frankenstein monster-a terror to its masters.” Con-
sequently, in July, 1899, Mr. T. S. Taylor, member for
Christchurch, brought in a Totalisator Abolition Bill,
Mr. Flatman brought in another entitled The Totalisator
Gradual Extinction Bill. The Premier, Mr. Seddon,
speaking to the latter, said “He hoped they would see
this bill made one of urgency, and that the totalisator
wou i d be a thing of the past in the colony.” As there
was not fcime to carr v either bill the Leg. Council agreed
t o the following resolution That the Govt, be re-
quested to introduce a bill for the suppression of
gambling.” This subject has been duly announced as in-
cluded in the Govt, policy for this session. So while
Victoria is being asked to legalise the totalisator Maori-
ian(j j s prepared to abolish it
The discussions in the Maoriland Parliament last session
were characterised by singular unanimity as to the ac-
knowledged evils of the totalisator and the wisdom of
repealing it. Out of the scores of speeches I will quote a
few testimonies from Hansard’s official report, which
illustrates strong reasons for abolishing it. Other quo-
tations are also from authentic sources,
Mr. T. Dick (a member of the Cabinet which introduced
the tote, writing Rev. A. H. Wallace, Vic.) said : “ I
hope the totalisator will not be introduced in Victoria. We
have it here, as you know. Mr. Whittaker, when he in-
troduced our Gambling Bill into Parliament, inserted a
clause legalising the totalisator, under certain conditions,
in the hope of crushing out bookmaking. But I fear
the remedy is a failure. Bookmaking continues, and I
think numbers go to the totalisator who never would
have thought of gambling but for it. I wish we could
get the law repealed ”
Premier Seddon:* “The effect of introducing the
machine had been to spread the vice of gambling. When
they had bookies people used to go to the races to put a
pound or two on a horse, but the evil was confined to a
certain class. Now the totalisator had caused gambling
to permeate the homes of the people.”
Mr. Taylor, M.P., writing Rev. A. Wallace: “We
have a large number of spieling bookmakers here now,
who live by laying tote odds. They are known as tote
bettors, and their clients are unfortunately in hundreds
of cases boys and girls. It is no uncommon thing to see
girls and women going into tote-bettors’offices. There is
a feeling in favor of abolishing the totalisator, and I
think in another year or two it will be abolished by law.
You had better suffer the ills vou have and know than fly
into the arms of the totalisator with its attractions,
The bookmaker is infinitely less injurious than the
machine.”
• • • '
Most Maorilanders are satisfied that bettors increase
when they find well-attended race-meetings becoming so
frequent as to occupy 313 week days of the year, a great
advance on pre-tota’isator times. Alas, that the receipts
through the totalisator increase from £350,000 in 1881 to
nearly £900,000 in 1899 ; and with all that bookmakers
to-day are as numerous as ever ; or, as the Yic. Sportsman
put it, “That the bookmakers have prospered in M.L.
against the use of the totalisator is very obvious.”
The experience of the totalisator in S.A. runs parallel
with that of M.L. Sir Richard Bai«r may claim it is a
■“magnificent success” from a racing point of view, and
no one will dispute with him. When a deputation of 1000
persons waits upon the Premier asking him to repeal the
totalisator, and urging exactly similar reasons to those
urged in M.L., this coincident dissatisfaction with the
machine should “ give pause ” to Victoria.
In view, therefore, of regrets expressed in M.L. and
elsewhere, the “ terribly mistaken experiment ” of
legalising gambling and the attempts to repeal ; the per-
nicious depredations of bookmakers, which are not abol-
ished ; the creation of new evils, which defy the moral
conscience of the community, it is surely not wise for
Victoria to attempt to nurse this viper of legalised gam-
bling in her bosom, under some spasmodic attempt to
abolish bookmakers by licensing the totalisator. The
wiser course is attack the evil in straightforward sim-
plicity, and declare, as in S.A., “ that betting in a public
place, or any place to which the public has access,” is a
misdemeanor, to be enforced with drastic penalties.
* * * *
Mr. Nicholson, of The Parsonage, Geelong, does
not openly pose as the champion of Isaac Moses,
by the way. He professes to be working for the
abolition of all betting, which is much the same
thing as advooating the Millenium, the more
especially as he speaks in a province where a
fashionable Poster Ball procession was headed the
other day by sassiety advertisers of Tatt.’s Sweep.
But he carefully quotes the “argument” of men
like Taylor, M.P., who say “the bookmaker is
infinitely less injurious than the machine,” because
Maoriland runs the Tote 313 days of the year, and
permits bookmakers (otherwise commission-agents)
to roam at large inviting men, women, and infants .
to gamble with them on Tote terms. The Bulle-
tin would like to know what Messrs. Jos. Nichol-
son, Taylor, M.P., Premier Skddon and Co.
take the public for when they quote the sins of
the Govt, as proof of iniquity in the Tote ! N.S.W.
and Victoria ask the right to employ the much-
maligned machine in place o/parasitical bookmakers
for reasons which this journal is tired of repeat-
ing. The sporting and economical arguments in
favor of the peaceful democratic Tote on race-
courses are unanswerable. The “ moral” objections
that Premier Seddon and Co. now raise against it
are ridiculously contemptible. We don’t want
313 days racing in the year. We want the use of
the machine restricted by a respect able Act, as in
S. Aus., and we herewith call upon the Maoriland
Govt., and every Australian Govt., to compel
their corrupt police departments to suppress
street-betting, shop-betting, tout-betting, and all
Tote-betting save what is conducted on racecourses,
for the benefit of sport and in the interests of fair-
play for punters. The Tote may be a bad thing,
or a comparatively good thing, just according to
how the Act is framed by Parliament and ad-
ministered by the police. The bookmaker is a bad
thing, anyway. And, by the way, when Taylor,
M.P., abuses the Tote because “ we have a large
number of spieling bookmakers,” he is a very
illogical little man. When he is so angry at the
bookmakers, why doesn’t he wade in to abolish
them? His idea is, because there are swindling
bookies, to abolish the honest machine.

POLITICAL
POINTS
In most Australian Parliaments at present
the smaller men are filled with anxiety that the
bigger men shall go for Federal seats.
S.A. Labor Party has chosen Education
Minister Batchelor, Kirkpatrick and McGregor,
M.’s.L.C., and Price, M.P.. to contest Federal
districts. This is the strongest quartette available
in the Labor ranks.
The Bulletin trusts that Cardinal Moran
and Co. are pleased with their work, one of the
results of which is that a “ Protestant bunch ” is
to be organised for the Senate elections by the
Evangelical churches.
If Australia doesn’t watch it, the name of
the Federal capital will be “Georgetown,” or
“ Yorktown,” or “ Mayvillo,” or “Guelph.”
Thank Heaven it won’t be “Victoiia”—N.S.
Wales would be too jealous.
South Australia sticks pretty closely to her
policy of “no more alienation of Crown lands.”
In the past 10 years she has got rid of only 53,917
acres, for which she lias received £52,352, and
most of this has gone in very small lots.
Beautiful extract from Queensland Han-
sard, showing the advantages attaching to the
plentiful importation of Asiatic cheap labor
Mr. Givens : In my ignorance, I walked through a street
in Maokay, and I saw a Japanese woman walk from one
side of the street to the other without as much costume
on as Mother Eve had.
Chunk of argument from a speech of
Premier Seddon’s during the recent debate on
Australian Federation.
“Shall we sell our independence for a few bags of
maize ? ”
Yet Sedddfi does not call his idea to tie Maoriland
on to the Fijis, a proposal to “sell our complete
independence for a few bananas.”
THE RIGHT HAND OF FELLOWSHIP.
The empire of Russia will desire to sub-
jugate Europe, and will itself be subjugated. The
Tartars, its subjects or neighbors, will become its
masters and ours; this revolution seems to me
inevitable. All the kings of Europe are working
together to accelerate it.” — Rousseau : Social
Contract.
Thus “Kiwi,” from Maoriland :
Apropos The Bulletin’s expulsion from M.L. Parliamen-
tary library. The cause alleged is the irreverent attitude
of the paper towards the great god, Jingo, but though
this may be the pretence, it is not the real reason. Some
months ago, when Seddon audaciously endeavored to get
Chamberlain to tinker the Commonwealth Bill, notwith-
standing that it had been emphatically endorsed by the
Democracy of Australia, The Bulletin dared to call him
“ a high-handed boor.” The paper that could thusattack
the head of the Maoriland Liberal party need expect no
quarter It will certainly get none from Big Dick.
The glad tidings come from Adelaide that
the invalided S.A. soldiers from Africa are to get
the first chance of a job at the Labor Bureau! It
is a great drop from the promise that their names
would be treasured for ever in the remembrance
of their admiring country, and would be writ large
on the Scroll of Fame, to the reality of the first
chance of a pick-and-shovei job in the burning sun
at verv little per diem—this for an invalid, too.
Still it might have been worse. They might have
been promised first vacancy in the Benevolent
Asylum.
Quaintsampleof Foreign
trade logic. Pulsford’s new
Foreign-trade paper recently
announced that big N.S.W.
in 1800 had more persons em-
ployed in factories than little
Victoria had in 1898, and
asked if this was not a trium-
phant proof, &c., &c. George
Reid proudly quoted the same
figures in Melbourne. Where-
upon the Aoe pointed out
that, if he had given the
figures of each State for 1898,
or the figures of each State
for 1899, Victoria would have
been ahead, and that taking
different years in this fashion
was pure misrepresentation.
And Pulsford’s new Foreign-
trade paper replies gravely
that the 1899 figures for Vic-
toria were not ready (the idea
that he might have compared
the figures for 1898, and
thereby have arrived at a fair
result, doesn’t occur to his
limited intelligence), and
complains that the Age’s
charge of unfairness is there-
fore “ without a shadow of
justification, as anybody can
see for himself.” And Puls-
ford is supposed to be one of
thq. intelligent men of the
Foreign-trade party ! What
can the ruck be like ?
Victoria is excelling it-
self lately. Not satisfied with
filling its gaols with the des-
titute and sick, it now incar-
cerates acknowledged luna-
tics. At Horsham lately, a
man suffering from religious
mania and obviously men-
tally deranged, was actually
sent to gaol for 15 months by a P.M. —not a
common honorary. He had committed a small
assault on a policeman, for which a sane and
responsible man would have got 14 days.
J. P. Abbott, before surrendering the powers
and privileges of N. S. W. Speakership, issaid to have
appointed the gardener who used to look after his
little private plot at North Shore to be gardener of
the other little plot at Parliament House, Mac-
quarie-street, at a salary (from the taxpayers, of
course) of £l4O a year, and quarters equal to
another £5O. When the estimates come up there
is to be a row.
Zeal, President of Victoria’s 48
Rich Landlords, made a speech re-
cently, in which he assured the public
that the alleged Toryism of that re-
markable Upper House was all a mis-
take. It wouldn’t in the least object,
said Zeal, to widen the Council suffrage.
Perhaps not, but Zeal knows as well as
anybody else that the Council suffrage
isn’t the trouble. If the 130,000 Coun-
cil electors were allowed to choose any
of their own body to represent them
in the Council, Victoria would be
reasonably satisfied; the grief is that
there are so many qualifications at-
tached to membership that only about
2000 of. the 130,000 are eligible to be
M.sL.C. ; that these are the old, fat,
bigoted fag-end of the electors ; and
that when you deduct those who are
too aged and infirm, too busy, those
who don’t care for politics, those who
are too ambitious to bury themselves
in the Upper Tomb, and those who
are absent, there are only about 100
dreary bigots left. It is the same as if
130,000 teetotallers were allowed to
choose their representatives among 100
confirmed drunkards. And Zeal kindly
assures the public that the 100-con-
firmed drunkards don’t mind being
elected by a rather larger number of
teetotallers.
From things that are happening
in a big N.S.W. mining town it seems
that there are ways of dodging the very
good law which provides that works
estimated to cost over £20,000 shall go
to Parliamentary Works Committee.
The plan, it seems, is to spend £20,000
on a section that isn’t any good unless
other sections costing other 20 tlious.
are added to it; and so the work
is done by instalments. The pre-
sent proposal is to build an entire
street-tramway system on this dubious plan,
the fear being that it would not pass the Works
Committee; and, worse still, a Labor Mayor is
engineering the thing that looks and smells so
fishy.

Ed. Bulletin.— lsn’t it time somebody called attention
to the effrontery with which the N.S.W. pastoralists

ilk, are fencing in and otherwise coolly annexing what

the abuse. The M.P.’s game is to go for the roads-and-
bridges .vote—not for the interests of the voteless nomads

following idiotic p srforiuance, known as “Flag
Drill,” is now indulged in. Three boys, known
as the “ color party,” carry the Union Jack and
halt in front of each class. The Teacher : “ At-
tention ; stand. Position 1. All put right hand
on heart and say, ‘ L lovo my country.’ 2. 1 1
honor her flag,’ saluting witli right hand to fore-
head. 3. Raising the right hand as if taking an
