The pietists of Maoriland have again
fallen in. Premier Seddon, it was confidently
anticipated, was going to re-establish the dilapi-
dated old principle of State aid to sectarian
schools. He hesitated long between two opinions,
but at last he seems to have decided that the non-
sectarian crowd was the one he could least afford
to offend, and the heels and coat-tail of the
Church have just been seen disappearing over-
board once more.
In 1898 Maoriland resolved to try the Old
Age Pension system for three years, the Upper
House regarding it with a certain amount of
shudder, as a dangerous temporary experiment.
But it has proved a success, and has come to stay.
A Bill now before the House of Representatives
changes the temporary expedient into a permanent
law and a fixed item of public policy, and even
the most Upper of Upper Houses wouldn’t dare
to kick seriously now.
The cabled story about Michael Davitt
having renounced the Boer cause and justified
England because of what he had personally seen of
the Boers’ cruelty, cowardice, greed and corruption
proves, on Davitt’s own authority, to be the usual
cable-lie. Had Davitt recanted as described, his
recantation would have carried enormous weight;
for than the crippled Irishman, whose kindly
nature has not been soured nor his keen judgment
impaired by all the horrors of his ten years’ incar-
ceration in a convict prison, there is no more
devoted lover of Truth.
The proposal to greatly reduce the duty
on patent medicines was one of the most
foolish of all Premier Seddon’s
recent proposals for throwing
away revenue which Maori-
land can’t spare. Seddon
proposed to sacrifice so much
revenue by the abolition of
duties that his province
would be compelled to con-
struct practically everything
in the way of public works,
productive and unproduc-
tive, out of loan money. A
well-timed revolt in the
Govt, party seems likely to
knock this suicidal policy on
the head. Think of loading
posterity with debt to make (among
other things) bogus liver-pills a shade
cheaper !
Political quidnuncs state that
Attorney-General Gordon is using the
fact that he is the only lawyer on the
Liberal side in the S. A. parliament, to bluff
Premier Holder and his other Ministerial
colleagues by threats of resignation whenever
he does not quite get his way in Cabinet.
Gordon has long been famed for the easy
adjustability of his political conscience.
He has the manners of an angel, the
plausibility of a Chinee and the backbone
of a jellyfish.
Of 243,244,800 acres in South Australia
proper only 7,836,553 acres is so far sold, though
another six millions is held under right of pur-
chase, and as much as 5,231,000 acres is now held
under perpetual lease. As a result of bad seasons,
dog and other pests, and, no doubt, also the pro.
vince’s policy of paying for improvements on
thrown-up country, the unoccupied lands have
now reached the enormous total of 154,558,692
acres —five-eighths of the entire area of the pro-
vince !
Victorian Assembly when passing the
Electric Light and Power Amending Bill, inserted
amendments specially stipulating that a week’s
work should consist of six days of eight hours each,
that Sunday work should be paid for at double
rates, and that no Municipal Council should grant
a concession or in any way transfer its powers
without first taking a poll and getting the consent
of the ratepayers. But the obstreperous House of
48 Rich Landlords will doubtless eject these demo-
cratic clauses with great fury.
It is a go id many years since the general
issue of electoral-rights in N.S.W., and it is evi-
dently high time there was another. The old
rights have been lost wholesale ; those that were
not lost have become dirty, infected, and spotted
all over with the stamps and brands of many elec-
tions, and voters have been dying and departing
till the N.S.W. electoral lists dunno where they
are. A recent overhaul of seven Sydney elector-
ates revealed the astounding fact that out of
18,959 alleged voters on the roll only 7307 are
alive and still resident and entitled to
vote in these electorates. Against this fact there
are 9938 who should be-on the lists but are not.
Under these conditions it doesn’t seem much use
to have lists.
It is interesting to remember that George
Reid, who recently went over to Melbourne to
hold forth about the beauty and' advantages of
absolute Freetrade, is not only the man who re-
tained many Protective duties while he was
N.S.W. Premier (either because he didn’t believe
his own assertions about the injuriousness of Pro-
tection, or else because he wanted to injure
N.S.W.), but is also the man who assured a Tuiriut
audience that he no longer had the same faith in
Freetrade as of old. That was when there seemed
a possibility that Reid, by turning Protectionist,
might get the Federal Premiership. His chance
of being the Protectionist Federal Premier
vanished when he fell from office, but there is just
a shadowy chance that a Freetrade Federal Pre-
mier may be wanted, and Reid is in active train-
ing for that billet now.
Th e Two-headed Government of Victoria,
according to the indignant Auk, is about to
introduce a Bill to prevent “confiscation” of
private lands for railway purposes without com-
pensation. That is to say, it having been
specifically agreed between the State (which is the
people) and the persons to whom it gave or sold
lands prior to 1861, and the heirs, executors, and
assigns of these persons, that the State should have
the right to resume land for railway purposes
without compensation, and the grantshaving beep
parted with more cheaply on account of this
drawback, the M’Shiels Govt, cold-bloodedly pro-
poses to rob the people of a valuable right, and
make a present of it to a small body of land-
holders. And it does this largely at the instigation
of certain papers whose proprietors hold land
bought on conditions mentioned. Said proprietors
howl about “confiscation” when they are merely
requested to keep to the terms of their bargain,
and will profit by having their titles improved at
the public expense.
At a recent meeting of aldeTmen from the
many municipalities of Sydney, a tremendous
scheme was evolved for establishing a Greater
Sydney Council, to supervise the more important
concerns now looked after- by 43 mayor’s, and 43
councils, and 43 sets of aldermen and dog-catchers,
without abolishing these existing functionaries.
All the present pomp and riot were to be main-
tained, but there was to be a great extra council
piled on top, so that there would be 44 mayors and
about 800 aldermen and town-clerks, all to muni-
cipally govern less than 500,000 people. And, so
long as the drawing up of schemes of municipal
union is left to aldermen, instead of being taken
up by intelligent citizens, they will go on like
this. What would rouse them to enthusiasm,
however, would be a scheme for establishing a
Greater Sydney Council, and also having 88
mayors and 1600 aldermen. If that plan could be
devised, the air would be black with aldermen
rushing to express their approval and enthuse for
union.

The little Bill of Griffith, M.L.A., provid-
ing for the sacking of Cornstalk J.’sP. who become
bankrupt, are guilty of indictable offences, or
otherwise become subject to well - founded
suspicion, was rejected in N.S.W. Assembly
by 44 votes to 18. Evidently each of the 44
knew that the J.’sP. in his electorate couldn’t
stand inspection, and wouldn’t thank him for
giving them an opportunity to prove their
innocence. The S.M. Herald got a good old
expression off its chest in this matter ; it said the
Bill was “inquisitorial.” And that sounded just
as terrible as if it had been hypodermic or equi-
lateral. For that matter, when the Herald
engages a new clerk aren’t the inquiries it makes
into his previous character inquisitorial ? And
shouldn’t the character of a man who is entrusted
with power to send his fellows to gaol be inspected
as carefully as that of the man who is entrusted
with the petty cash ?
A measure to prevent unqualified persons
passing themselves off as qualified doctors, and
thereby getting medical fees under false pretences,
and killing or torturing the unfortunates who
ignorantly trust them, is making better progress
than usual through N.S.W. Parliament. It pro-
Eoses to let the quack and medical fraud pursue
is nefarious way just as before, except m one
particular—he must not assume titles not rightly
his. He isn’t to be completely abolished, because
“ the liberty of the subject” to entrust not only
his own life but that of his wife and children to
some ignoramus who may kill them, or cripple
them for life, must not be interfered with. By
the same logic a tailor should be allowed to com-
mand a ship provided he doesn’t profess to be a
qualified captain. The liberty of the shipowner
to entrust his vessel to a tailor, and the sacred
right of the passengers and crew to sail in a vessel
navigated by a tailor if they choose to do so, should
not be interfered with. The system under which
tailors are not allowed to command ships is
tyranny.
The Fat Man is particularly angry about
■ the idea of Victoria voting as one constituency for
the election of the six Federal Senators ; the
chances are that six democrats will be elected
whereas if the State was cut into six electorates
: the Stout Person could almost certainly carry one
•of them. And just here ex-M.L.C. Sargeant
comes forward with an idea. He proposes to cut
the State into six divisions, and let each division
• choose one Federal Senator at a mook election,
just to see which man can command the most
support, then nominate the six at the real Federal
■ election. He reckons they would then go in with
a devastating whoop, and it would really, though
; not nominally, be an election by constituencies.
Yet it is quite possible that out of the six men
•elected by the divisions, not one would get a show
'when he went for election by the State voting as
■ a whole, and that would badly upset Sergeant’s
'banana-barrow. Barton’s case showed that the
'man who could command an overwhelming
'majority in the State as a whole might easily be
‘the one that no separate constituency wanted.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
Tor week ended Saturday, 22nd, that affairs in the world
of wheat have been somewhat less blue. On Monday
week cabled news was received that London market was
improving and a better demand was in evidence in conse-
quence, and a considerable quantity of the “old grain”
was disposed of at 2s. 9d. to 2s. lOd. per bushel for prime
samples ; 2s. Bd. to 2s. BJd. for F.A.Q., and 2s. 3d. to
2s. sd. for chickwheat. The number of sacks that have
reached this market during 1899-1900 is a record, but
owing to area under crop and climatic conditions the
coming season promises to beat it clean off the field. ♦
It is estimated that the N.S.W. public
servants have contributed 88 per cent, of the
entire capital of the Superannuation Fund, while
the Government, which has managed the whole
concern, has contributed less than 12 per cent.
In other countries it will be found that the most
solid pension schemes are those in which the
company, or government, as the case may be,
subsidise the employes’ contributions pound for
pound, and it is noteworthy that in such schemes,
employes are fully represented on their boards of
management. It is indeed monstrous that public
servants of N.S.W. have not had representation
in the control of a fund which they themselves
almost entirely provided.

Perth, June 5, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —I have been invalided for about 2 years and
3 months. The doctors treated me for Inflammation of
the Lungs, Bronchitis, and Pneumonia, but failed to cure
me. I used to suffer with very severe pains through my
back and chest, and had a very nasty cough. I was in
the Perth Hospital three weeks, but the doctor told me
he could do no more for me, and that I was as well as he
could make me. I was laid aside for 12 weeks at Fremantle.
The landlady of the Club Hotel advised me to try VITA-
DATIO, which I have done, and after taking four bottles
lam now in splendid health. I will be pleased to answer I
any enquiries that any sufferer may require in the hope
that my humble testimony may be the means of leading
others to health.
Yours respectfully,
(Signed) FREDERICK DAVIS BAYSWATER.
Witness: W. J. Fist, Perth.

“ Westralian ” to The Bulletin :—
In Review oe Reviews, last issue, appears an article by
a clerk in the office of A. P. Matheson, M.L.C., on the
Federal Campaign in W.A. The writer is described as
secretary of the Fed. League, a position he never held.
Zollner was the first secretary, and Fowler succeeded
him. Both did good work. Frank Wilson, M.L.A.,
Kingsmill, M.L.A., Briggs, M.L.C., Gregory, M.L.A.,
Editor Forster, of Albany Advertiser, Editor O’Connor,
of North Coolgardie Herald, James Gardiner, Farmer
Warren, of Katanning, good old Port, of Bunbur3’, Mayor
Fimister, of Kalgoorlie, and Sommers, of Coolgardie, and
Pearce, of Trades and Labor Council (to mention a few),
each did more for the federal cause than Matheson, but
they are not mentioned at all, while Matheson’s name
appears in every second paragraph. George Leake and
Walter James are named casually as sub-assistants to
Matheson. Leake’s great services in breaking down the
Forrest opposition to Federation are not spoken of,
Matheson’s clerk being in a hurry to catch the mail.
Some time back, Gov. Ranfurly (M.L.)
sent his carriage down to Wellington wharf to
meet a friend. As the regulations re carriage-
traffic on the wharf are stringent, the officials
did not allow the vice-regal robbo to pass, and the
visitor —haw ! —had to walk from his steamer to
the wharf-entrance. The Harbor Board backed
up its officials, but informed H.E. that, it notified
beforehand, it would see that no inconvenience
was caused him for the future. Yet Premier
Seddon actually put this forward as a reason for
refusing to re-nominate ex-chairman J. Hutche-
son to the board !
The latest result of the struggle to go one
better in the coupon business is a system devised
by the Melbourne firm of George and George.
The old idea was that the collector had to save up
a certain number of coupons before getting any
value for them, and the value of unpresented
coupons was so much profit to the man who ran
the scheme. The Melbourne firm now gives its
own coupons to the extent of Is. in the £ on cash
purchases and redeems the coupons immediately
in money or goods. Also the value of the coupons
not presented for payment is to be formed into a
separate fund, and persons making £25 worth of
purchases, receive a certificate entitling them to
share in that fund, which is to be divided half-
yearly. Of course the man who gives coupons to
the amount of Is. in the £ redeemable immedi-
ately, and divides • the unclaimed-coupon fund
among his customers, will naturally be followed
by the man who gives Is. 3d. in the £,
or who redeems his own shilling coupon at
fifteenpence-farthing, or who is even more
immediate than the other man ; and the man
who undertakes to double the unclaimed-coupon
fund out of his own pocket will jjrobably follow.
And finally there will arise the Ultimate Crusher-
—the man who gives a 5s. “ Tattersall’ssweep
ticket and a drink with each £1 purchase, so that,
by buying a cheap suit or three shirts, you may get
a £5000 prize and live in affluence ever after.
There was a time when two rival steamship co.’s
running between Liverpool and Dublin fought
each other so bitterly that at last one co. carried
steerage passengers gratis, while the other carried
them gratis and also gave every one a loaf. The
coupon business is likely to come to something
like that in the end. The last development before
the burst should be the man who doubles his
prices and gives 10s. worth of Coupons in the £.

The great electrician Preece, it is cabled,
has discovered a method of wireless telephony
across the sea a distance of 8 miles.
A Kimberley (S’Africa) correspondent
gossips to The Bulletin
It does not appear to have heen hitherto mentioned
that in Kimberley was discovered a house equipped with
telegraphic apparatus, the wires leading to the Boer camp
outside. It was through this means that the funeral of
the engineer Labram was shelled during its progress to
the cemetery. Labram, the man who designed “Long
Cecil,” was preparing for dinner when a Boer shell blew
him to bits. The Boer custom was to cease bombarding
daily at 5 p.m., but the hour fixed (8 p.m.) for the funeral
was duly communicated and the procession shelled.
Kimberley is De Beers, pure and simple, for the huge
co. dominates everything. There is here little or no fluc-
tuation in businesses, trade being the same year in and
year out. This is due to the fixity of the output and con-
sequent limitation of the wages total. The full staffs are
there or thereabouts and likely to the best tiling
outsiders can do is to keep away. '
People are war-curio mad in S’Africa. Anything will
do—from bits of exploded shells to fragments of the
“ Queen’s chocolate” boxes. At a recent auction sale in
Kimberley I saw a dozen siege postage-stamps passed in
at £3, and a Kruger sov. at £3 15s. A Mafeking brick-
colored sixpenny-stamp is now worth about £l5 !
Hundreds of Britishers are flocking to S’Africa from all
quarters of the Empire every week, and for what? Is it to
see a black man work ? For the present and for some time
to come, yes 1 Said blaekfellow is everywhere equal to the
occasion.' He has been taught the trades by the Britisher
and he holds fast. You will find him coach-builder, ca,b-
driver, policeman, shop-keeper, barber, farmer, con-
tractor, miner, etc., etc., right through the whole gamut,
and the Britisher is pondering what is left, after all the
fighting.
Gazing upon the graves of the Scots who fell at Modder
River and taking in at a glance the nature of the veldt
and the kopje rising therefrom, one is inclined to curse
the man who ordered the unfortunates to take such
positions by storm. Most of the kopjes are mere big
heaps of large, loose rocks packed anyhow (by Nature) one
upon the other, so that a man would have to seek a resting
place for his foot before he could advance, and his eye
would be off the enemy half the time. The lack. of
artillery may be advanced as a reason for storming, but
why should the wealthiest nation on earth lack artillery ?

Contributors to the N.S.W. Public Service
Superannuation Fund have, since its inauguration,
paid in no less than £825,482 ; the interest on that
sum added year by year increasing it to £1,035,368.
The Crown, during same period, has only con-
tributed £lOO,OOO. On the other hand, according
to the reports of the Public Service Board, the
Crown has been assisted in saving a sum of not
less than £1,616,000 in salaries, by placing officers
on the Fund before they reached the retiring age
to the extent of £214,107. And yet the State had
no more right to shove these retrenched officers
on to the Fund than it had to shunt them on to
the A.M.P. Society.

SPORTING NOTION’S.
The heavy-weight bruising struggle ’twixt Tim
Nolan (Sydney) and Joe Cullen (Melb.) on Saturday night
was an eye-opener. Both scaled about 12st.— Cullen the
lighter, if anything. They bore into each other, banged,
bashed, roughed and clinched. Joe peppered with his
left. Tim smothered mostly till Joe gave a chance, then
rushed and swung punches that staggered every time
they made impact. Cullen went prone often, but
wouldn’t stay longer than he could help. Once referee
M’Donald sprawled over him. Again all three were down
together. Crowd stood up often and roared with excite-
ment. ’Twas a rough-and-tumble, hit-anywhere scramble
from end to end. Science was off the bill completely, but
writer has rarely seen so much real down-right fight in
many big boomed 20-round battles as during the ten
minutes Nolan and Cullen were at work. Both got rough
usage and bled freely, without the faintest semblance of
flinching. Cullen would try and catch ropes or posts
sooner than fall, and then couldn’t get up quick enough
to be fighting again. Ultimately the police gave the
“ office,” the battle was stopped, and Nolan goo the
money. Cullen could hardly have seen another round
through.
• • * *
On Monday night at Gaiety A.C. (Sydney),
the so-far unbeaten Otto Cribb will be up against the
toughest thing he has yet tackled, for all previous victims
weren’t near the same class as Mick Dunn, who owns the
Australian middleweight supremacy. The fact that Mick
says “ Well, if I don’t beat Cribb he won’t beat me,"
makes it appear as if he is impressed with the seriousness
of his situation. Sturgeon kept off several rounds, and if
he could do so Dunn certainly can. Money up totals £lOO,
and winner is to get 75 per cent, of what should be a big
gate. There’ll be heaps of cash behind Otto, but it
doesn’t look as if t’other side will respond to any very
great extent. Mick and Cribb had a willing go for a
round or two when the Maorilander first came here ; it was
a trial arranged by Foley, but Otto has come on a heap
since, so that no line can be taken on that. Writer
thinks they’ll both stay the agreed-up n 20 rounds,
though it’s quite possible one may get the other out be-
fore then, for the thing’s genuine enough.
-** * •
“Micko” Walsh supplied a big surprise-party
for Mick M'Carthy at Victoria Hall, Melb., Saturday
night. Latter undertook to stop former inside 20 rounds
but ran against a snag, for he never held his own a
moment, was down 9sec. nine different times in second t
round, and got such a torrid 3min. of it in the third that
the rag was skied for him. Mick was fairly good once but
has been out of the game for some time ; he had a couple
of fights in America .years ago. His brother Billy got a
decision over Jem Hall and put up some good battles
with Dooley, Slavin, Fitzsimmons and co., and is the only
man who ever knocked out old “ ironbark” Burge. Last
time writer heard o.f Billy lie was in the cotton business
at Galveston (Texas) and may have been among the
12,000 who perished there for aught that is known to the
contrary.
* * * *
Vancouver mail brought details of Fitzsimmons’
win from Akron giant Ruhlin, in New York, Aug. 10. 15,000
people looked on, and “gate” returned 70,00,id01., of which
winner banked 26,250 and loser 8,750. They literally fought
each other into helplessness, but the Australian always
saved enough to make the knock-out punch convincing
once it got home. The usual hand-shake had barely
taken place when they were at it. Give-and-take was the
game from end to end. Fitz was fiercely aggressive all
the time. Giant Ruhlin (15st.) towered over Fitz. (12st.)
like a dray-horse alongside a thoroughbred. Result
was in doubt a dozen times till the sixth round, when
Fitz. dropped Ruhlin cold as an iceberg. Bob looked, as
always, the “ lean and slippered pantaloon ”, of pugdom,
but in reality was a crouching bundle of seasoned sinews.
He kept close to the Yankee Brobdignag, flogging away
and at times fiddling. His knees were bent on
occasions, and his gait wobbly; his bald and bony
head often rocked again from the force of the giant’s
punches, but there was no sign of dizziness about the
freckle-fisted fighter. He went into Ruhlin immediately
“time’’was heard and began to slug. Ruhlin bashed
straight from the shoulder and beat Fitz back again and
again. The first was the giant’s round, but the next went
t’other way a bit. Both “ chivvies” were badly bruised
by the end of round three, and Ruhlin bled like the stuck
pig of tradition.' There was a ragged gash alongside
Bob’s left eye, and shining lumps stood out on forehead
and temple. Fitz took Ruhlin’s left full in the face fre-
quently and still kept after the “ Buckeye.” The pon-
derous one evidently didn’t have that boasted smashing
punch with him that night. Twice the M.L. freak ham-
mered his man clean across the square and brought him
down the second time. Fifth was a remarkable round ;
neither troubled about science. Swings, hooks and
straight jabs landed in faces without meeting opposition ;
Fitz’s were most telling. Later Fitz. fell back on tricki-
ness. From inception of sixth 3 mins, he was atop of
Gus hammering him to his knees early. The big fellow
stood erect again. Fitz feinted with his right—Ruhlin
came, then the lathy one’s left crashed against the bulky
one’s jaw with a collision that could be heard every-
where, and the fight was over. Gus lay inert; it took
him all night to get round.
* * * *
“ Old Timer” : The sporting world looks upon
Bob Fitzsimmons as a wonder —can’t understand why a
man of 39 fights is so full of ginger. Now, Jem Mace was
verging on 40 when he beat Tom Allan for U.S. champion-
ship. Black Richmond had seen 42 when he tackled Tom
Cribb, and was within three years of 60 when Carter went
under to him. Worth mentioning that Richmond didn’t
begin fighting till he had passed 30. Though born in
1751, Joe Ward didn’t have his first “ dust up ” till 1788,
when he beat the Irish top-notcher, Reynolds. Old
“ Ironbark” Jim Burge, of Sydney, was takingtrouncings
(each of which would have killed half-a-dozen ordinary
pugs) and winning battles long after 40. Fitz. has an
advantage which most of those named did not possess—
he is of most determined character, and looks after him-
self well.
* * * *
The fashionable ’Frisco Olympic Club wants
champions from this side to compete against the world’s
amateurs in Feb. or March next. Trouble is where are
they to come from. Writer mixes a lot in the game,
but hasn’t for a long time seen a really good amateur pug.
in Svdney. Since the day of George Seale, Jack Vallen-
tirie, dead Dick Sandall.'and burly heavy-weight Harry
Beckett, we’ve had nobody standing out as remarkable,
nor do we hear of any in adjoining States. Ever since L.
Foley (first) and old York-street (Sydney) club put up
their shutters the high-class brand of non-professional
has been rare. If Peter Jackson’s projected ben. pans
out decently he’ll probably get to work on whatever raw
material is available. The sable warrior purposes opening
an exclusive gentlemen’s club with up-to-date appliances
for exercising, and will arrange big fights once monthly,
admitting only members and friends in same manner as
the swell London National Sporting Club. This should
catch on, and if conducted as outlined will fill the bill
nicely.

* * * *
Paul Pry must be a tough ’un. He has been
racing six years, and giving all the States a turn, running
in 82 races, and winning 26. Since Randwick trainer
Tom Payten sold him he has got away with following :
Summer Cup, £lBO ; Kalgoorlie Cup (twice), £250 and
£500; Autumn Handicap, £9O; Birthday Cup, £l2O
Toorak Handicap, £5OO ; Elder Stakes, £l2O ; Adelaide
Cup, £800; Onkaparinga Cup, £350; Autumn Stakes,
£407 ; Birthday Cup, £575; Spring Stakes, £430 ; Craven
Plate, £400; Wycombe Stakes, £320; and Randwick
Plate, £340.
» ■ • * *
Paul Pry, ’tis said, used at one time to run in
blinkers, not from roguery, but through an injury to his
eye by an oat, which, interfering with his sight, caused
him to swerve when other horses raced up to him.

Hard to understand why the A. J.C. wants an

the A.J.C. meeting than she had done some three weeks
earlier at Newcastle. Three weeks is long enough for a
horse to come on or go “ right off.”

* * * *
Dear Bulletin. —The anti-tote argument of
“E. and 0.E.” is absurdly thin. He says “it is the-
bookmaker who makes betting—and therefore racing-
possible for men who do bet. It is the ‘ scores ’ and fifties;
of the punters who think they know something that fill
Ike.v’s bag ; the half-sovs. and sovs. of the mug wouldn’t •
pay Ikey for his risk.” As a matter of fact, it is the pub-
lic who make racing (on a big scale) possible by providing
clubs with the means to give away big stakes. And we
have the best of evidence that big stakes can make racing
possible to the best of horse-owners without any assist-
ance from Ikey Mo, the middle-man. Lord Falmouth,
the successful English horse-owner of the ’7o’s, and the
first employer of jockey Fred Archer, never had but one
bet in his life (he bet his trainer’s wife sixpence against
one of his own victorious horses, and gave her
the “tanner” set in diamonds), yet he made a profit
on his racing and breeding studs. Indeed, he couldn't-
have afforded to carry on the game at a loss. Mr. Stead,
in Maoriland, stated a few years ago that he, as a non-
bettor, was racing at a profit, thanks to the increase of
club stakes through the agency of the tote. As to the
allegation that Ikey Mo fattens on the “ knowing ones”
who get the “cream of the market,” it merely goes to-
show that he must profit enormously by the gold contri-
butions of the mugs who “follow the stable”—at a dis-
tance —and get short odds. Anyway, what is the good of
“ E. and O. E.” pretending that Ikey alone makes racing
possible when the tote is already established in South
Australia, Maoriland, Queensland, and Tasmania? We,
who want the tote in other places, don’t care a button
whether I. Moses is a necessity or otherwise. If he be a
necessity, he will continue to exist for his customers,
whilst the machine exists for us. — Mug.

« * » *
Bulletin yarn of the Sydney sport who backed
Ampier t’other week, because he was looking at the gas-
jet of “ Dampier” in front of the Criterion Theatre when
the lights on letter “D ” suddenly' disappeared, is a
reminder of some old English Derby' tales. It is recorded
as a fact that on the eve of Ellington’s Derby, a turfman
was passing a certain Wellington Restaurant in Picca-
dilly, or somewhere. A flag that had been hanging from
a window above the gas-lit “Wellington” transparency
suddenly slipped, and the bunting obscured the letter W
from the punter’s view. Whereupon he went and backed
Ellington to win him a small fortune. Another well-
authenticated case was that of a horse-owner driving
down to Epsom in a mail phaeton when a butcher’s cart
nearly brought his vehicle to grief. The butcher went
tearing along the road, with the phaeton in pursuit, and
the butcher’s fast trotter didn’t allow the horse-owner to
get near enough to distinguish more than the word
“ Teddington ” on the back of the cart. So he backed
Teddington and won.

expenses. Fined £2O, Bench accepting £4 deposit and

Sunday-school’ story.

* * *
The Eales-Grant contest for the pigeon-shooting'
championship of N.S.W., came off at the N.S.W. Gun-
Club’s Ground, at Botany,' on Wednesday September 18.
Nothing to approach Eales’shooting has ever before been
seen on the ground. He killed every bird of his 100 ; the
first 59 with, absolute perfection ; the 60th, the only' one
he lost, fell dead out of bounds. He shot 79 out of the
100 with his first barrel; and not more than 10 of his
birds required the second barrel, although he did fire his
second 21 times, often as not to make dead sure of birds
'that were fluttering. He shot most of his birds within a
half yard or yard of the trap ; some before they flew
-eight inches. * The man was wound up to mechanical
perfection, nearly every bird being centred, and fall-
ing, cut to pieces, amidst a storm of feathers. Eales’
very quickness was the reason for his losing one. For
that one, a middle-trap bird, sat for a second or two,
.giving the shooter the baulk. When the match began
the betting was 10 to 9 on Eales ; half way through it was
10 to lon him. But there was very little betting. The
bookmakers sat quiet, holding their noses reflectively in
their fists, and admiringly watching Eales mow his
birds down. Before the exhibition several matches
with Eales were mooted ; but when it was over nobody
had a word to say about them. Everybody seemed to
agree that Mr. Eales at present was a good thing to be
avoided. There was perfect order through the day and
absolute stillness whenever a man toed the mark. Only
the men, their seconds, and the club officials were
allowed inside the enclosure ; brother Walter, with an
umbrella, affectionately sheltered Arthur at times from
the sun, while Drury did the same for Grant. Out of the
200 birds only six sat, and not a bird was barred. The
traps fell very evenly all day', averaging well for both
men. And, oddly enough, each man excelled himself on
the most difficult traps, and failed comparatively on the
•easier ones. Eales used 47grs. powderand ljoz. shot; No.
6* in the right barrel and No. 6 in the left; while Grant
used 49grs. powderand IJoz. No. 6 shot in each barrel.
Grant is a deliberate shooter. He seldom downs his
birds inside 40yds., while some of them were over 50yds.
'away when he caught them with the second barrel.
Thus he never gave his birds the penetration that
Eales’ birds received. And so birds that Grant feathered
with both barrels sailed strongly out of bounds. Grant,
however, didn’t drop his bundle, and when at half way he
appeared hopelessly beaten, he went on and lost only one
bird out of his remaining 44. Eales should do well in
England, for although the light is worse there than here
he has proved himself a remarkable shot in a dim light,
being classed by the club as one of its “ ’possum-shooters.”
He’ll be minus one advantage in England, though. He
won’t have Duckenfield station to practise on any day at
any hour he chooses.
‘ * * * *
The 12th annual N.S.W. Public Schools
; Sports meeting was a great success, and was witnessed by
about 33,000 persons. There was an all-round improve-
ment upon last year's display, though the walking-contests
were unsatisfactory exhibitions of trotting, jogging and
go-as-you-please. What is wanted is to remove at once
unfair walkers, so as to give a chance to those who do
walk fairly however slowly. An old lady, after the Junior
•Champion Walk, declared vigorously to the judges that
her grandson (who had comejin first) was the only boy r
who had walked fair from end to end. “ He’s the favorite
• of Mr. Turner(Fort-street head-master) and he never wags
it, and it’s a downright shame it is if he don’t get the
medal—and nothing else but favoritism —so there ! ”

The 100yds. championship of N.S.W. Great
Public Schools’ Association at Sydney Cricket Ground,
Friday, was won by A. D. Fisher (C.E.G.S.) 3in. in front
•of N. Barker (Newington College), with E. Cochrane
(S.G.S.) a yard further away, third. Time, 10 7-10.
Same day and occasion H. Henderson (S. Sydney A.A.C.)
secured 100yds. supremacy of N.S.W. by a yard from W.
Lindsay (E. Sydney A.A.C.), who was a like distance
; ahead of S. S, Middleton (Goulburn). Time, 10 4-5.
* * * *
After the fastest and most exciting football of
the season, Melbourne beat Fitzroy (last year’s winners)
•on Saturday, and won Vic. Premiership. Essendon—up
till then well in the running—had been downed by Satur-
day’s winners, the previous week, and for the final
tussle ’Roy started favorites. 20,000 spectators rolled up
and roared themselves hoarse. The Northerners led by 2
points only at half-time ; during third quarter Melb. put
on 2 goals 3 behinds, equal to 15 points, while ’Roy re-
mained stationary; last quarter latter slowly pulled up,
but the Red Legs'lasted well and won by 34 to 30. Both
sides “ played the game,” remembering, no doubt, the
League’s recent punishment of sundry rough players,
also, the fact that on the previous evening a Williams-
town player had been disqualified for life for repeated
breaches of the rules. Though high up the list several
times, Melbourne has not flown the pennant of Premier-
ship since ’75.
Woolabra, between Narrabri and Moree (N.S.
Wales), possesses a sporting curio in the shape of a well-
formed, laid-out football ground, with but one goal-post.
Said that the ground was cleared by navvies, who used
to play spells of half-an-hour, one side only being allowed
to score while their opponents defended the solitary goal
till half-time. In the second half the other team had
its try at scoring. * * »
Dr. Turner, the English cycle authority, on
Hale, the man who cycled 100 miles a day for 12 months :
“ He came to me two days before completing his task,
and immediately on riding 77 miles of that day s 100. His
nervous system was in perfect order, and there was
nothing iri any way to show that he had passed through
any exceptional stress. His pulse was quiet and steady.
His arteries were soft, silky and elastic, and the heart-
sounds perfect. The heart itself was enlarged, the apex
being displaced about an inch and a half downwards and
an inch to the left. There were no signs of circulatory
disturbances. This condition of the heart is what must
be expected in anybody who has led an active athletic
existence, the heart-muscle in such a pefson becoming
enlarged.”
» * * *
Veteran Spofforth still plays cricket in Eng-
land. In a match at Arlingham last month he took seven
wickets for 31 runs, and did the hat-trick. Spoil, was
45 this month.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rowing Ass. Championship Eights, on
Parramatta River, Saturday, resulted in the closest
struggle seen for years—between Glebe and Sydney R.C.’s.
Race was with the tide over 2J miles. Glebe’s form did
not reach the standard of Sydney, but it made up in
power and stamina. It was an exciting neck-andmeclf
battle for fully two miles, then condition asserted itself,
and Glebe shot out at the finish, winning by a length and
a half. Vanquished crew protested that it was “de-
liberately fouled,” but umpire wouldn’t have it ; both
coxswains were to blame. J. J. Daley was unopposed for
the Champion Sculls, and paddled over the course. Mr.
Daley is so superior to all other local amateur scullers
that his entry frightens the lesser lights away, and, as it
is a barren honor at best paddling over the course, it
would be sportsmanlike on his part to in future stand out
and allow the others to make a race of it.

Cabled that Australian top-noteber Freddie
Lane dead-heated with ’Ome champion, Derbyshire, in
British furlong supremacy at Birmingham, 17th inst.
Lane was 2yds. best at 154, but the Englishman caught
him on the post. Time given (2.34 4-5) is very fast, being
3 2-5 better than previous world’s best, set up by Lane
when he won same race last year. Derbyshire hasn’t an
equal over 100yds.—his figures are the fastest ever done.
Said that Lane will leave for home next month.

Yankee papers print a lot re ’Murkan negro
“ Majah ” Tavlor’s challenge to the world’s cyclists—best
2in 3 one-mile events for £lOOO. Failing biz. at home
Taylor talks of coming out here. Sprinting is his game,
and there isn’t a man in the land of twang who can come
near him. The world’s best for the mile (1.19) is his.
Said that Taylor was offered £2OOO to go to Paris and race
through the season, but because they wanted him on
Sunday's he declined. A rare bird among athletes truly.
The “ Majah ” has the reputation of being even smarter
than Zimmerman ever was.

Hannan’s Marvellous Mile is the name of the
area including the big mines, and some bad ones, at Han-
nan’s. But it is not a mile in length nor is it a mile
square. If Brownhill Mine is included it is considerably
more than a mile long, and if the poor mines on the
eastern side of the belt are excluded, it is considerably
short of a mile wide, but admitting both those mines the
area may be thus set out The Big Mines : Great Boulder,
Boulder Perseverance, Golden Horseshoe, Lake View
Consols, Ivanhoe. Good, but not great mines: Brown-
hill ; whose greatnessjias mostly departed on account of
its oxodised bodies being rapidly exhausted'and its sul-
phide bodies very limited. South Kalgurli, a mine with
350 ft. of good ground on the right side of the break.
Kalgurli, a mine with one ore-pipe, big at the top,
small at the bottom. Associated Northern, a coming
mine, has all its ore untouched. A good mine established,
with possibility of greatness with more development.
Associated Gold Mines had greatness thrust upon it
by a gouging-out policy oh one narrow rich shute of ore
and by a lying policy concerning the assay-value of the
ore outside it. May yet become a big mine by develop-
ing virgin country, of which it has a good deal; but the
chances are against it. Oroya, in an excellent situation,
one block being surrounded by the Associated, Kalgurli,
and Associated Northern Iron Duke Lease ; the other
block by the Associated Lake View Consols and Central
West Boulder. Has paid one div. and may pay some
more, Dut it will be in the sweet by and bye.
Prospect mines within the area. — Golden Link
has the best position, being between one Lake View lease
and one of the Great Boulder. Has got the Lake View
lode at depth, but so far very poor. Market valuation
absolutely unwarranted. Chaffers, next to the Golden
Horseshoe, has spent a heap of money on prospecting
and found nothing, but must still be regarded as a
good prospect because of its situation. Central
and West Boulder, surrounded by the Associated and
Oroya, a splendid position on the map, has had one lenti-
cular block of oxidised ore, which was good a few feet
below the surface and poor Within 100 ft. of it. Has
crushed tens of thousands of tons without a div., and has
been working at a loss this year. Hainault, the will-o’-
the-wisp of the camp, is just outside the much-cursed
quartzite break, which runs along the N. boundary of the
Ivanhoe and takes a N.E. course through the northern
lease of the Great Boulder, the Boulder No. 1 lease,
Hainault, and the northern portion of the Kalgurli lease.
Probably its course will be more defined in other
leases to the N.E. as mining development proceeds.
Hannan’s Star has had many a crushing, but never a div.
Having used up most of its pay-ore and leased its sulphide
plant to the Brown-hill, this mine must now be regarded
only as a prospect. Lake View South still a prospect,
although it has done so much prospecting that in any
other field in Australia it would have been given up as a
duffer long ago.
Proved duffers within the area. — Lake View
Extended, Trafalgar, Golden Pike and Lake View East,
Kalgoorlie Mint and Iron King. This mine has had some
crushings, but has no pay ore in sight. Ditto the Bank
of England, also the N. Boulder, which deserves its fate
for its lying name ; the Oroya East, belonging to Hannan’s
Proprietary Development Co., which has 24 blocks all
over the field and every one of them a duffer ; the North
Kalgoorlie, Paringa Consolidated. Brookman’s Boulder’s
only remaining hope is the reef it is cutting for at the
900 ft, level ; a bore 5 months ago showed about an oz. for
a good width, but it is impossible to put too little faith in
the assay of a diamond-drill core in formations such as
these, which may be amazingly rich in one spot and disgust-
ingly poor a few feet away. Brownhill Extended has made
some splendid shipments from a run of ore that crossed
at a sharp angle its N. W. boundary. If it only had a little
more ground to the west it might well be placed in
another category, but situated as it is a little more work,
and it is all up with the B.H.E. Shares have been down
to 55., up to £7, and are now under £2. The Adelaide
block of the Associateds : not much work done, it is
true, but its situation and the result of work on other
leases around it gives no encouragement in that direction.
* * * *
There are five distinct ore-belts in the area.
Probably the best is the extreme Western, certainly the
very worst is that on the extreme east. The Western is
represented by the Golden Horseshoe and the Ivanhoe,
taking in the entire length in these two 24-acre blocks.
It is probable, however, that as the break on the northern
boundary of the Ivanhoe dips rapidly north and strikes
rapidly N.W., the Northern block of the Gt. Boulder will
find a continuation of rich ore in the deep sulphide zone.
The next belt or ore-bearing channel is taken in by the
Gt. Boulder two 24-acre leases, with the exception of that
left in the Boulder Main Reef, from 250 to 300 ft. of fairly
good ground. Between the Boulder and the next belt
there is a considerable blank represented by the Golden
Link and most of the Northern block of the Lake View
Consols. This belt runs along near the western boundary
of one L.V. Consols lease and the eastern boundary of
the other, through almost the centre of the Perseverance
and a part of the S. Kalgurli. These three belts are well-
defined, the other two are not; the fourth is repre-
sented by a block or two of very poor ore in
the L.V.C. southern lease, a lenticular mass of
poor oxidised ore in the Central and West Boulder, the
rich but limited and now practically exhausted ore-body
in the Associated worked from Tetley’s shaft, the other
bunches, less rich but quite as erratic, worked from
Waddington and Judd’s shaft, and the other big but ill-
defined ore-body in the Kalgurli. The fifth ore-channel
has been equally erratic, ill-defined, and disappointing,
and if it becomes established that the Brownhill and the
Associated Northern ore-channel is distinct from it, then
it is a duffer from one end to the other. It is represented
by the Kalgoorlie Mint, the Bank of England, and North
Boulder, none of which have any pay-ore in sight and
never had any to brag about. The Oroya has to shut
down because it could not find 7dwt. rock in the oxidised
zone and has not established enough ore in the sulphide
to call itself a mine.

£BOOO is said to have changed hands consequent
on the Glenfine No. 1 salting.
* * * m
The Glenfine salting affair recalls another Bal-
larat story of a somewhat similar kind, but in the earlier
instance the brothers concerned did not give the show
away till long after they had dissipated their profits. The
Blue Moon No. 1 Co. was doing splendid business with
three-ounce stuff, and the Blue Moon Extended had just
cut the reef, and was knocking down a trial crushing.
The crushing retorted at the rate of about sozs. to the
ton, and Blue Moon Extendeds went up hand over fist,
only to fall with a dull sickening thud after the hig
crush, which didn’t return even sdwts. to the load. The
scoop on this occasion was engineered by two men. one of
whom worked at the Blue Moon and cabbaged a shirt-
ful of specimens each shift, which were carefully smug-
gled below by his brother who was trucking at the Ex-
tended, and who mixed the stone from the rich mine
with the hungry rubbish at the new one. The faceman
who is honest misses many fine opportunities of dying
rich and respected at 80 to die miserably at 56 of miner’s-
disease.

4 S. Proprietary, 200 tons, 6370 z. ; Rogers’ G. Gate, 510
tons, sSBoz. ; Moonstone No. 1 S., 90 tons, 3040 z. Aug.
calls, £867 ; divs. for same period, £22,990. G. Gate 3
and 4 Prop, cleaned up again Sept. 4, from a crushing of
895 tons, for 29140 z. Bdwt. ; average per ton, 3oz. sdwt.
3gr. ; average value of gold, £2 13s. sd. per oz. ; average
value of ore, £8 14s. per ton ; average value of crushing,
£7786. Out of this the syndicate "divided £6BOO, at the
rate of £425 per one-sixteenth. Prospecting shafts,

North Chillagoe Co.: Q. Department of Mines
has called attention to certain omissions in this co.’s
prospectus from the report of Assistant Govt. Geologist
Dunstan. The Sydney crowd running this concern seems
to have made a mess of things, more the result of its own
folly than from any bad motive. Writer suggests that
they now publish the original report verbatim. The pro-
perty is very highly spoken of by experienced and reason-
able people ; in fact, there are grounds for believing that
the block known as the Titana Hill is a fine, fat copper
“ P'g ” that needs no greasing.
“ Trotter ” writes: “ A recent look at the big
mine at Ohillagoe recalls to me the Lake George at Cap-
tain’s Flat, N.S.W. I knew a man who had 1000 shares
therein at 755., which he said he would keep until they
reached £s—and then for a trip round the world. I was
very anxious to get the strength of this mina, and sat up
till 3 one morning in Mrs. Groggin’s hotel listening to
present Agent- General Copeland recounting his adven-
tures in Borneo. He had talked the others to sleep,
including manager Trewenack, when I got his opinion—
‘Sell.’ Next day I advised my friend to unload, but he
wouldn’t, and six weeks afterwards his shares were worth
about ss. He still owns that scrip.”
* * * *
Apropos the proposed extension of raining edu-
cation in Victoria. The directors of schools of mines
and technical colleges are finding that the supply of mere
assayers has more than over-taken the demand ; but the
demand for managers who know something of the practi-
cal side of mining and are also assayers is still greater
than the honest supply. The Adelaide School, which,
having Broken Hill to work on, has been unusually suc-
cessful, is now sending its young men to do hard graft
underground wherever that course is possible.

Reported to the police that one of the
Breelong blacks has outraged a 15-year-old girl at
Cobark Creek. This is just the sort of news that
any observant and reflective person might have
expected to hear. But it is not the kind of news
he would necessarily believe.
The Scandinavians of Sydney seek to have
Mr. Oscar Hellstrom, secretary to the Consulate,
and now Acting-Consul, appointed Consul in suc-
cession to his late uncle, Mr. Falstedt. Mr.
Hellstrom is most popular with the general pub-
lic, and is an earnest and able official possessed of
charming manners and a fund of good stories.
The position in China is simply this :
Germany, backed by sundry other Powers, says
to the ChineseTmperial Govt., “ Hand over to us
the persons responsible for the recent massacres.”
Li Hung Chang (who is willing enough to deliver
up dummy-nobodies, on whom vengeance can be
vicariously wreaked), replies, “Impossible! Your
demands force upon China the necessity of fight-
ing the world.” And who can foretell the result?
"Vic. Contingent Private M'Cance—danger-
ously wounded and captured in S’Africa—re-
turned, the other day, to his native place near
Castlemaine; and, as a preliminary flutter, had to
kiss all the young women thereof. Then there
was a drag, and a band, and some of the inevitable
Queen and Empire; after which the returned
warrior observed that, though the Boer bullet sat
heavy on his chest —being, in fact, still lodged in
one of his lungs—the kind treatment he received
from his captors was a pleasing memory and a flat
confutation, as far as he was concerned, of the
yams about Boer brutality.
Editor Bulletin.—G. H. Reid said re-
cently :
“ As to the despatch of troops to South Africa, if you
want a real ‘yes-no’ specimen, compare N.S.W. Works
Minister O’Sullivan as the British patriot with the same
gentleman in his capacity of editor of the Sydney Free-
man’s Journal."
Now, as O’Sullivan left the Freeman editorship
long before the Boer war was thought of, and as
the Freeman’s Journal has been throughout, dis-
tinctly Pro-Jingo, much to the disgust of many
of its constituents, Reid is either talking
through his neck, guessing, or endeavoring to
touch the pup gently while the aftermath of the
Redwood blunder remains.— Church-Hill.
More backwash. Letter printed in several
Sydney dailies:
Ten months ago on 3rd Nov., 1899, Sydney witnessed
the departure per s.s. Aberdeen of 125 volunteer infantry
for service in S’Africa. I have just seen the widowed
mother of one of those soldiers. She has lately received
a letter from her son, wherein he states that of the 125
who so sailed in Nov. but 35 can now answer the roll-call,
the other 90 are either dead, in hospital, invalided,
or prisoners. Her son also states that the 35 are
absolutely penniless, without proper clothes, in great dis-
tress, and but scantily supplied with food. Here is a
picture of the desolation and misery caused by war, both
at home and abroad. Who can realise the depth of anguish
the mothers, wives and sisters of these 125 men must now
be enduring. Is it not pitiful in the extreme ? Cannot
the survivors of these gallant volunteers, this for-
lorn hope and remnant of patriotic valor, be now
recalled with honor, seeing they have lost 75 per cent, of
their original number ? I appeal, on behalf of the sorrow-
ing relatives bf these men, through your columns, to the
Govt, of N.S.W., and ask if an arrangement cannot be
made with the Imperial Government for their return.
At Poverty Point.
“E.F.”: “The Commonwealth” is hardly likely to be
adopted by J. O. Williamson, or anybody else, as a suit-
able name for a big theatre. It is too suggestive of a
busted management and a co. chasing the treasurer
for a fair division of the profits. But the “ Federal,” or
the “ Dominion,” or the “ Unity,” the “ Century,” or
the “ National,” would fit the house that was lately re-
christened (for the second time) Her Majesty’s. The name
that permits of a short catchy abbreviation is good.
“ Fed,” “ Nat,” “ Dom.”
Poor old St. George’s Hall, Melbourne, has, after many
vicissitudes, been wiped out from the show business and
become a rag-shop. A Bourke-street draper has now
turned it into a store and “ show ’’-room—the latter as in
mockery. St. George’s would bear writing up theatrically.
Amongst those who have “ read,” sung, played, or
made jokes therein were Walter Montgomery, the Caran-
dinis, the Fakir of Oolu, the whole Stewart family in a
lump, Weston and Hussey, Anna Bishop, Madame Cora,
Baldwin, the Hellers, and hundreds of others. St.
George’s saw no more prosperous time than ’69-70, when
it was called “ Weston’s Opera House.” Frank Weston
and Frank Hussey had there the finest negro-minstrel co.
Australia has ever seen. It touched the other “ houses ”
palpably. Nellie Stewart may be said to have made her
first appearance in St. George’s—in 76, when she played
Ralph Rackstraw, in “ H.M.S. Pinafore.” On t’other side
of the street, the Lingards were running the same thing
the Josephine (Alice Dunning Lingard) being the most
beautiful ever seen there.
Tom Fitzgerald has an enthusiastic admiration for Carl
Hagenbeck, the animal-trainer, with whom he lately did
business in Hamburg. “Stands 6ft. 2in. high, slim, and
strong as whipcord, always on the alert, a non-drinker
and non-smoker, and as bright, at 56 years of age, as a
boy of 20.” It was Hagenbeck who sold elephant Lizzie
and the lion, likewise the horse and tiger, to Dan Fitz-
gerald. He is prepared to buy those two “acts’’back
from his Australian friends whenever they care to come
to terms, but meanwhile he has arranged with brother
Tom to ship some more lots of educated animals during
next year. A photo of one of these Fitzgeraldian invest-
ments, with a Polar bear as chief performer, appears in
present issue. The work of inducing lions, tigers, bears,
&c., to perpetrate strange deeds is a matter of years in
some cases, says the patient Hagenbeck. He has experi-
mented with as many as 16 beasts for one trick before he
could strike the necessary combination of docility and
common-sense. And it’s “all done by kindness,” seem-
ingly. The animals greet the tall German with roars of
affection, and rub against the bars of their cages as he
goes around the menagerie and talks to them. All the
same, it is difficult to understand why a Polar bear should
have tender feelings towards the taskmaster who teaches
it to push a tricycle.
“ Count B.”: The Rickardian reply re English lady
ballad-singers (8., 25/8/’00) evidently does not apply to
the alleged “ serios,” many of whom have evidently
secured Australian engagements merely by reason of
“ hubbv ” (who is generally a comedian) being under con-
tract to Rickards. If we can breed ballad-singers, I think
we can also breed serios. If ’Arry would apply his rule,
the next time he goes ’Ome, so that it would work both
ways, he would earn the gratitude of a long-suffering
Australian public.
London Topical Times reports that Amy Castles, who
lives at the Convent du Roule what time she is not attend-
ing Marchesi’s Academy, “ wants to get home very badly,
even if she had to live in the back blocks.” Which will be
something of a shock for Mrs. Doublt day, the Melbourne
“discoverer” of Amy. The little girl from Bendigo is
utterly ambitionless and lacking in artistic temperament.
Even when she was at the height of her Australian
notoriety,'receiving diamond rings, illuminated addresses,
and all sorts of adulation, she told Mrs. 1). that if she
had her own way she would rather be a nursemaid.

Olive O’Hara, son and heir of Melb.’s doughty surgeon,
has been the bright particular star of the Cafb Ohantant
at the Children’s Hospital Bazaar with his marvellously
clever sleight-of hand tricks. Young O’Hara is a medical
student in private life, and his conjuring is understood to
be undertaken in direct defiance of “ the old man’s ” dirty
left. Nathless, it is likely that the lad—he is just 20-
will appear as a professional before long. For tolerably
good surgeons are many whereas gifted conjurors are few.
“ Yarra” : The American invasion is growing rapidly.
Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, is now running two Yankee
stars; the previous season was bossed by two more from
the land of the spangled banner, and the co. was chiefly
American. Mrs. Potter and sundry other Yanks are
coming, and at the Bijou the three star turns are supplied
by ’Murkan teams—lrene Franklin, the Patterson gym-
nasts, and Emmonds, Einmerson and Emmonds. By the
way, Sheridan, at the Royal, is also an American, but we
seem to have taken him over.
A temporary eclipse has happened to the gaiety of
Oollins-street, Melb. Festive F. B. Norton, as his com-
mercial friends know, and his many theatrical friends will
be sorry to learn, has been compelled by the demon In-
somnia to sell his advertisement agency business and take
a rest. Just now “ Fred B.” is engaged in cultivating the
art of slumber and when he has slept himself back into
“ form ” the man with the waxed moustache and flower
garden in his buttonhole will probably seek an opening
for his talents somewhere in the theatrical market. Any-
how he is under medical orders not to carry any more
ads. by storm for the next year or two. Which is a sad
relief to his Melbourne clients. They had grown so
accustomed to being rushed into strange advt. contracts
by the anecdotal Norton that the loss of the infliction
leaves a blank in their lives.
Marshall Hall has arranged for a series of lectures in
Adelaide, beginning at the end of the month. After
which the combative Prof, will probably undertake a con-
cert and lecturing season in Sydney.

“ Anti-Gammon ” . In reply to “ S.S.” I may as well say

Luscombe Searelle, known in Australia as a comic-
opera composer before he made his pile as a S’African
showman, is just now anxious to go on active service in
China. The Chows have massacred his young sister—she
was only 26 who was preaching the Word in their midst
when the trouble broke out.
Amusing to read in a Sydney print claiming special
theatrical knowledge that Billie Barlow “ has been sing-
ing one of Alma Gray’s songs.” The song in question
was, as a matter of fact, specially written for the sprightly
Billie, is published with her portrait on the front page,
and was probably sung by her ere Alma was out of the
nursery.
Whispered that both Billie Barlow and Lottie Collins
are to be in the cast of Rickards’ Melbourne Xmas panto-
mime this year. If Harry throws in the Haytors, and
sends Harry Diver to stage-manage, it ought to be a
pantomime of pantomimes. J. A. Philp, of Sydney, is
writing “ the book.”
Our old friend Carrie Swain is said to be taking instruc-
tion in singing along with Amy Castles. Carrie (Mrs.
Frank Gardiner) is quite old enough to be the mother of
her fellow pupil, and during the 15 years that have rolled
along since she made her first Australian appearance she
has tried sundry courses of study for her rough, flexible
voice. Way back in the 80’s we had it that Carrie was
being trained for the operatic stage in Sydney. Her voice
was of the kind that almost invariably drops its melody
whilst gaining culture. People smiled at the thought of
Carrie Swain as a prima donna, but as the rorty heroine
of a melodrama, taltingaheader into a tank and swi inning
back to “ shore,” with a rescued kiddie seated on her
back, Carrie was generally approved of. Her most
original stage effect was the squirting forth of a mouthful
of water from between her teeth, as though she had taken
it in by accident. Frank Gardiner, her husband, is now
one of the very biggest and richest and most daring
mining men on earth. He has splendid establishments in
both London and Paris, and, according to an Australian
lately back from Europe, he drives absolutely the smartest
turn-out in the Bois de Boulogne. He began life as a
stage-dancer, but you only had to see him with his deadly-
pale, impassive face and jet-black hair sitting stonily
behind a poker-hand to know that he was born to make
a big spoon or spoil a big horn.
The original Tyrone Power, a rather famous Irish
comedian, went down with the good ship President some
60 years ago. The present T.P. is a grandson of the
drowned man.

Alterations in names and addresses in City
and Suburbs for insertion in Sands’ Sydney
Directory 1901, may be sent to the compiler
not later than the first week in October.
The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas Co. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success and are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc., from
the Head Office of the Company, 36 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
A recent letter from S’Africa relates that
the bodies of 17 strangled Boers were found in the
field after a recent encounter. They had been
choked by the lassoes of certain Canadian Scouts.
And a Maoriland swaddy, writing home, some-
what exultantly relates how several hundred Cana-
dians got 23 Boers into a tight place. The Boers
offered to surrender ; but the Canadians “ weren’t
taking any, and shot and bayoneted them ■ to the
very last man.” The Glory !
Sydney bookseller Dymock, in his evi-
dence before the Sydney Public Library Inquiry
Committee, told a pathetic tale about how he was
taken in. A well-dressed man was wandering
about his shop after the manner of the desultory
book-buyer, and at last he gathered up a valuable
work, put it under his arm, and went upstairs.
If he had made for the door he would, of course,
have been stopped, but it was inferred that he
was going to select some more books in the upper
premises and pay for them in one lot. Later he
came down without the book and departed, where-
fore it was assumed that he had changed his mind
about the proposed purchase and left it on a table
somewhere. But he hadn’t. He had gone up-
stairs and sold the boss his own book at a con-
siderable reduction, and there was great cursing
that afternoon in the Dymock emporium.

A number of Victorian Tories deputation-
ised Premier M‘Lean last week and desired that
the Government should use its influence to sup-
press Trenwith’s Industrial Arbitration Bill.
They assured M‘Lean that it was all a mistake to
suppose that Maoriland prospered because of this
kind of legislation; it prospered in spite of it.
Possibly ! Still the writer can remember Maori-
land in the middle eighties, when it was the
poorest, deadest, and most wretched of the seven
provinces. It had all Tory legislation then. Pre-
sumably the theory is that it wasn’t the Tory
legislation that made it poor; it was poor in spite
of Tory legislation. Still if a country is poor
despite the blessing of Tory law-making, and then
is rich despite the curse of Democratic law-making,
this paper would rather take the curse any day.
Elsewhere is an appeal for £25,000 to
build a new Hospital for Sick Children in Sydney.
Up to now about £9000 have materalised, of which
£5000 were contributed in sums of £1000 each ; so
that it is necessary to raise another £16,000 to do
the work as it ought to be done. If the Mother
State has really anything maternal about it it will
rise unanimously and put up the shekels, for if
ever there was a noble purpose, it is this. The
present hospital has done splendid work, but it is
in a crowded suburb and is altogether too small
for requirements, and it is time Sydney got out of
its hideous habit of pottering about things in a
small mean way. So the public is requested to
send along its sovereigns, and let there be a
children’s hospital fit to cope with its work and
be a credit to the humanity and enterprise of the
community.
Patents for Inventions —Arthur Griffith,
International Patent Agent and Consulting
Engineer, University Chambers, Phillip-street,
Sydney. Patents, Trade Marks, Copyrights,
obtained throughout the world.
South Australia gets a good idea of the
close relationship of ex-Premier Kingston and the
Labor-party in a little correspondence that took
place just before the former declared, himself a
candidate for the Leg. Council, to which he was
returned by a majority of some 300 in a heavy poll
over his opponent, Parsons. Though Kingston
wanted to get back to politics more than he wanted
anything else, he wrote to Robinson, ex-M.L.C.,
the man entitled to be Labor candidate for the
first vacancy, offering to support him for the seat.
“You have the first right to contest it,” he said,
“ and you can rely on my support in every con-
ceivable way ” (Robinson had lost a seat for that
district last general election). Of course, Robin-
son was wise enough to reply that Kingston ought
to be the candidate.
One of the Lyne Government’s sins of
half-heartedness came home to roost the other
night. N.S.W. Leg. Council badly mangled its
poor little Bill for the reformation of Sydney
Municipal Council, and refused to budge from its
manglings. If the Bill had provided for amalga-
mating the 41 municipalities of Sydney, and their
debts and assets, establishing one-adult-one-vote
at municipal elections, making the Council. of
Greater Sydney all elective on one day, having
salaried aldermen—the individuals who give a
little of their time to municipal matters for nothing
being worth just nothing, establishing a sinking-
fund and State audit of accounts, making it compul-
sory to levy enough revenue to cover outgoings,
giving the council the widest powers to resume
lands and buildings for city improvement pur-
poses, and to abolish slums—if it had been, in
short, the comprehensive reform it might have
been, the Lyne Govt, could have kicked the Coun-
cil into the next geological period. The matter at
issue would have been big enough to swamp the
Upper House over. But it was such a poor little
measure that to make a political crisis over it
would only have excited ridicule, and the Council
knew it, and smiled.

An Engineer in*Difficulties!
TRIES, LIKE MANY MORE, HEAPS OF
SO CALLED REMEDIES
But Meets with Final Success.
The Case of
MR. JAMES BURNS.
(By Our Reporter,)
No invalid is so difficult to deal with as
the one who has “ never known a day’s
sickness.” For the most part he resolutely
puts away the suggestion—“ That his time
has come at last, that his smooth working
machinery has broken dewn, and that active
repair is at once required.” No 1 the
chances are the sufferer battles on, getting
daily worse and worse, till final collapse lays
him on his back.
“My husband never would have that
anything was seriously the matter with him,
that he was bilious, or that his liver was not
as sound as it ought to be,” said Mrs. Burns
as our reporter interviewed that lady and
Mr. Burns. “ Not till he was really bad
and desperately ill would he give in, and
admit that something was radically wrong
with him.” Mr. Burns, it may be men-
tioned, is an engineer by profession, living
at 107 Stanley-street, North Melbourne,
and well known in that locality. Some time
ago he was engaged in a very arduous
undertaking, where he was exposed to all
kinds of weather, continually knocked up
by excessive travelling, and left for long
periods with poor and irregular food. In
the pride of good health and an iron con-
stitution, Mr. Burns thought nothing of
these hardships at the time; but later on,
like many more before him, he had good
cause to recollect. “ I fell,” said Mr. Burns,
“ into a low lethargic condition of body
and mind. I had pains all over me. Ido
not think there was a part of my frame free
from an ache of some kind. I was bilious
with an excess of bile that seemed to numb
all my faculties. I did not care to work, to
move about, to do anything. Yet when I
braced myself to active exercise I felt better
—that is, while the work lasted ; but when
the excitement and stimulant of exercise
was over I was done, prostrate and desti-
tute of all hope and energy. I suffered
from a pain below my neck and between
my shoulders, that cramped up my body.
At the least excitement my heart was
deranged, and went thump, thump, thump
against my ribs like the beat of a hammer.
My stomach and digestive organs behaved
very strangely, as though they had gone on
strike and quite given up work. No sooner
had I eaten a meal than I had to run to the
back yard and vomit it all up just as I had
swallowed it; so that my assimilative pro-
cess was absolutely paralysed. At times I
would go giddy in the head, like a child
that turns round and round; I could not
stand on my feet, and felt the sick deadly
sensation of one about to faint. If I had
been resting any time in one position, and
went to get up, I was held fast by a stiff
gripping pain in the small of the back,
Gradually I lost all taste or inclination for
food ; I would go for two or three days con-
tinuously without eating. This starvation
greatly added to my weakness, and at the
same time I rapidly lost flesh, and became
reduced to a shadow of my former self. My
nervous system suffered with the rest of
my body, and I became morbidly sensitive
to the least sound. A loud knock at the
door would make me start and jump as
though a cannon had suddenly been tired
close by. My temper became very irritable,
I was vexed and upset at the' least thing.
I slept well, in fact I was always drowsy ;
but the sleep appeared to do me no good. I
was never rested. Bleeping for one hour
was ai good as eight for all the benefit 1
derived, and in the morning I would be
more tired than at night. A terror that
haunted my night’s rest was nightmare.
Even when I awoke, or, what would often
happen, my wife woke me, I still for a time
experienced the cruel pains of suffocation
and strangulation. This general upsetting
of my whole system affected my eyes ; there
were times when they were quite dull, my
sight becoming blurred, and ic was with
difficulty that 1 could see to read. When
stooped down there followed pains across m„
loins like the piercing of sharp instruments.
My palate always had a nasty flavour, my
tongue was parched and dry. I became
wonderfully changed in outward appearance
and was depressing to look at, thin, worn
and emaciated, a skeleton of what I had
been.”
“ What remedies did you try, Mr. Burns?’"
enquired the pressman.
“Heaps of things, various makes of bitters
and blood purifiers, all warranted to curs
cases like mine and all useless, also r
number of patent and much lauded
with a like result. None did me the slight*
est good, in fact I became day by day
steadily worse. It was about this time,
when I concluded that there was no medi-
cine in the world that would relieve my
sufferings, that a book was left at the dooi,
descriptive of the virtues of Clements Tonic.
“I wonder,” said'my wife, “ if that would
do you any good, let us have one more try. 1 ”
She went over to our chemist and asked
about Clements Tonic, and that gentleman
told her she could not go wrong in buying
it, that it was the best remedy he knew 0 2-.
that his sales of Clements Tonic were larg«
and increasing, and he had received the very
best reports from his customers who haai
purchased it, and that there was not the
shadow of a doubt that Clements Tonic dit.
all that it claimed to do. After that, you
may be sure I purchased a bottle of dementi
Tonic, for I know that chemists are not
naturally inclined to give credit to proprie-
tary medicines without good reason. My
health began to mend from the very first
bottle J took. I gained a good appetite, an<£
my stomach retained the food. My sleep
refreshed me, the pains all over my body, i
my back, head, and heart were all subdued.
I regained energy and flesh and lost my
sallow appearance, my nerves were strength-
ened, and at night I slept soundly, undis-
turbed by dreams ; and vary soon my oure
was complete.”
“ I report this for the papers, Mr. Burns,”
said our representative.
" I am glad to hear it, and I hope it wil
be read and do good ; it’s all true, and yoC
can publish it how you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, James S. Burns, of 107 Stanlay-street, North
Melbourne, in the Colony of Victoria, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of six folios and
consecutively numbered from one to six, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any
manner my statements—which I give voluntarily,
and without receiving any payment; and I make th
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of an
Act of the Parliament of Victoria rendering persons
making a False Declaration punishable for wilful
and corrupt perjury.
Declared at Melbourne, in the Colony of Victoria,
this 27th day of December, one thousand eight
hundred and ninety-nine, before me,
CHARLES E. HIRST. J.P.
A Justice of the Peace in and lor the Central Baili-
wick of the Colony of Victoria.

A loaded truck recently got loose at
Riddell’s Creek (35 miles from Melb.), and started
“on its own” along the down-hill traok to the
city. An engine was promptly started in pursuit
and just managed to oaten it at Sunbury (24
miles out). Had it got past that station, nothing
but a streak of greased lightning could have
caught it till it broke through Spencer-street
station into Bourke-street and hit Cole’s Book
Arcade, as the down grade becomes much steeper,
at Sunbury. Some years ago a truck similarly got
away from Daylesford, and, there being no engine
speedy enough to overtake it, the station-master
wired to a station 20 miles down to shunt it off
the track to perdition, and spent the next few
minutes praying that his message would get there
first. Luckily it did, and the truck was sent into
a dead-end and shattered into dust.
In a recent London divorce-case, the
petitioner, Capt. Vivian James Forbes-Smith,
produced some entertaining letters from his wife,
who used to come home at 5. a.m. with always
the same old story of having been engaged in
teaching an aged French lady “ the art of conver-
sation.” Her last letter was comparatively candid,
It said : “I am fearfully in debt, and altogether
wicked. Except for one sin, lam altogether bad ;
and perhaps it is a pity I have not that one, for
it prevents you getting a divorce.” However,
the jury found the matron guilty of the one sin
needed to complete her badness, and a British co-
respondent (otherwise the aged French lady) who
had been taking lessons in conversation from Mrs.
Forbes-Smith was sentenced to £1250 damages.
An insurance-agent named Slaughter is
touring N.Q., accompanied by a doctor named
Butcher.
Be the non-naming of Bishops at Synod,
S. M. Herald, reporting Parliamentary debates,
omits the names of Ministers, and merely notes
that the “Minister for Justice” —or so on—said
so and so. A year or two hence, or less, the
reader will need a work of reference to see who
happened to be Minister for —say, Dust Bins—in
the year 1900. London Times, the Herald’s pat-
tern, has more sense.

SPRING TIME CLEANING.
Spring has arrived, and now the careful housewife goes through the annual Spring-
house cleaning. The Summer will be upon us, and at this time of the year we should
take care to cleanse the whole human system, and fortify it to withstand the enervat-
ing effects of the Australian Summer. The increasing hot weather of this period has
an extremely debilitating effect on the system if it is not in perfect working order all
through. It will be thus seen that a medicine which will regulate and cleanse the .in-
terior mechanism of man is especially valuable at this time of year. Such a medicine
is Bile Beans, and no better preparation can be found for toning up the digestive
organs, cleansing the blood, and invigorating the liver. Spring generally brings with
it an assortment of ailments, such as Spring Fever, Tired Feeling, Loss of Appetite,
&c. For these take Bile Beans, which are also a specific for Biliousness, Indigestion,
Constipation, and Debility, which are also prevalent at this season. As a purgative
Bile Beans are acknowledged without an equal in Australia. Their price places them
within the reach of everyone, and no one should run the risk of being m ill-health
through the Summer when a short course of Bile Beans would effectually purify the
body and keep it in thorough working order.
BILE BEANS.
This great remedy possesses the peculiar properties of acting on the bowels with-
out purging or otherwise weakening the patient. This is because the substance that
relaxes the bowels has about the same action as the natural bile. Bile Beans are
purely vegetable, and a course of them will probably successfully oust the most chronic
cases of piles, indigestion, chronic headache, kidney trouble, rheumatism, constipation,
and a host of other ailments that owe their origin to defective bile flow, assimilation
and digestion. These Beans are placed on the market in a form that anyone can take
them without medical supervision, and, as the price is so very low, there are few homes
that cannot afford to always have a box on the shelf for emergency.
