The Woes of Hochblatter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HoOßßLaTter Was a foreigner with a large, fleshy
frame* and a watery-blue eye. He leased a row of
houses, which he let off as “Furnished Apart-
ments.” The terrace was situated in a good
position and handy to the city, and if Hochblatter
had been a smart business-man he might have
made money. Not being a smart man, he didn’t.
Still, there was a certain amount of method in his
stupidity. He invariably collected his rents in
advance every Monday morning before twelve,
and ejected before three in the afternoon all those
who could not or would not pay.
Unfortunately his method ended right there.
He mismanaged the internal arrangements so
badly that no one who had the cash to pay regu-
larly in advance dreamed of stopping there any
longer than it took to find new quarters, always
barring a few wily old residents who had ways of
their own of turning Hochblatter’s arrangements
to profit. When the new lodger went into the
kitchen, after having taken possession of his room,
he found there one range or small American stove
for the use of all the tenants, and each tenant sup-
plied his or her own coal or wood. Also, while
each tenant’s provisions were cooking each tenant
strongly objected to anyone else so much as looking
at the fire. But when the new arrival appeared
and made up the fire, and sent it roaring up the
chimney, the old residents used to arrive and
instanter cover the whole top-plate with their pots
and pans, and gradually drive the new lodger’s
utensils over the edge. Thereby they saved coal,
but unless the new-comer was large enough to
fight the old residents in a body he usually left
Hochblatter’s premises within a week.
Hochblatter’s tenants never loved him —no
man who collects his rent regularly before twelve
on Mondays could expect to be loved; but at
last he became positively hated, with disastrous
results to himself. It arose over the man in the
back ground-floor room of No. 4, who was des-
perately ill—almost dead of consumption. His
pale, worried wife had toiled when she could toil,
and pawned everything on which the three-ball
man would advance the price of a loaf. She had
even got out of paying her rent in advance, a feat
for which Hochblatter never forgave her. Her
husband ought to have been in hospital, but he
had already been discharged as a hopeless case,
and they were simple people who knew nothing
about how to get him into a home for incurables,
so his brave little wife struggled on and kept
him somehow. But she struck a streak of bad
luck at last, and one Monday came when Hoch-
blatter’s rent was not forthcoming. It was only
75., but Hochblatter, with half a terrace empty,
put them out into the street—“on principle.”
The consumptive husband broke a blood-vessel
and died before they were much more than out of
sight of Hochblatter’s tenement.
Hochblatter saw the beginning of unpopularity
in this incident. He spent 10s. on legal advice,
and found that he was within his legal rights, as
he had used no force to expel his tenants. But
next time the lady with the strawberry nose in
the front room saw Hochblatter she cursed him.
Even people who never bought their own fuel, but
stole that of the other lodgers, told him he would
never have any more luck, while Mulligan, his
least respectable tenant, spoke to him in terms of
disparagement and threatened to throw chairs at
him.
* * * *
Hochblatter was fast asleep three nights later
when some one clanged loudly on the bell and
Eounded the knocker furiously. Thinking one of
is tenements was on fire, the landlord rose
hastily. With chattering teeth, he opened the
door and said, “Vhat’soop?” The man outside
explained that the Mulligans were kicking up a
dreadful row, using language that was demoralis-
ing all the other tena.nts, and hitting each other
something awful. Hochblatter smiled as well as
his chattering teeth would allow, and told the
tenant to go back and threaten them with the
police. Just then the cold, wintry air was filled
with the noise of crashing furniture. The tenant,
who was retiring disgustedly, came back in high
glee and shouted, “Hoo-ray ! they’ve started on
the furniture now—your furniture. Hoo-blanky-
ray ! ” A white figure rushed past him, and he
looked round and saw Hochblatter tearing towards
the police-station in his night-gear.
Hochblatter turned the comer and ran into a
couple of constables, who promptly ran him in as
a lunatic who had got loose in his night-gown, for
what with anxiety for his furniture, excitement,
unwonted exertion and a foreign tongue, the
flying apparition was incoherent, and the watch-
house-keeper entered him on the books as ‘ ‘ bark-
ing-mad.” Then the two officers went out to
further pursue their avocation, when the cold
night-wind brought to their ears the sound of awful
smashing and cursing. They hurried along, and
found that the Mulligans were still breaking
things, and Mrs. Hochblatter was calling for help.
They went upstairs and recommended the Mulli-
gans to cultivate a calmer frame of mind, but told
Mrs. Hochblatter they had no power to prevent a
man breaking his own furniture in his own house,
so long as he broke it peaceably.
Hocnblatter appeared at the Court in the
morning, and despite many entreaties and pro-
tests was fined for keeping a disorderly house,
for being wildly and dangerously drunk, and being
found in the streets in a state of undress.
He went back, determined to make the Mulli-
fans sit-up. He was sorry he had collected
lulligan’s rent in advance. As it was there was
nothing to go upon, and he doubted if he could
even demand to see the state of Mulligan’s room,
and insist on inspecting the furniture.
Mulligan met him at his door with a black patch
over one eye, and flatly refused to let him inspect
the furniture or enter the room on any pretext.
The landlord complained bitterly of Mulligan’s
conduct, and asked him when he would be pre-
pared to pay the amount his (Mulligan’s) unruly
conduct had cost him (Hochblatter) at the police-
court that morning ?
Mulligan’s remarks upon and replies to the last
question were unpublishable.
That night the Mulligan battle was resumed,
and the fragments of the windows littered the
pavement, and the roar brought the landlord out
m a thick topcoat. He opened the street-door
and marched up the stairs and knocked at Mulli-
gan’s door with the loud bash of authority. In-
side, the awful din continued, so Hochblatter
opened the door and entered. A scene of desola-
tion met his gaze. The room was wrecked,
the furniture reduced to splinters, and the whole
Mulligan family was doing battle. Just as Hoch-
blatter entered the door Mrs. Mulligan had seized
the kettle, and the action produced a panic in the
opposing ranks. In about 2sec. the panic had
become a rout, and the whole force of Mr. Mulli-
gan stampeded for the door, where it bumped up
against the landlord. But there was a kettle of
hot water behind and only a landlord in front, so
the flying Mulligan army trod landlord Hoch-
blatter in the dust and ran over him. He arose
just in time to stop the kettle of hot water that
Mrs. Mulligan had heaved after the defeated
enemy, and to get tangled up in the table-cloth
and other properties. He fled for his life with the
table-cloth still attached to him—he didn’t know
how.
Next morning, landlord Hochblatter was burn-
ing to wipe the Mulligans off the face of the earth,
and he sent for a lawyer to consult about what
steps he should take in the matter. While he
waited for the arrival of the lawyer, sitting in his
easy-chair before the fire, a knock came to the
door. Mrs. H. opened it, thinking it was the
solicitor, when two large policemen entered and
effected the arrest of one Herman Hochblatter for
' entering the apartments of Michael Mulligan arid
stealing therefrom one table-cloth to the value
of ss.
There was nothing for it but to go quietly, and
get bail, and have the Mulligans flayed alive if the
law could do it at any cost whatever,
Hochblatter went “ peaceful-like in a keb ” and
wept tears of rage before the party who granted
bail. Mrs. H. went with him. When they re-
turned they expected to find that the Mulligans
had fled, but they were mistaken. Hochblatter
had to go off to the Coroner’s Court to give evi-
dence at the inquest on the late consumptive, but
when he returned he and the solicitor proceeded
to interview Michael. Michael told them with
dignity that they must knock at the door and wait
till somebody beyant answered and “axed ’em
what they were after requiring,” and explained
that even a landlord could not walk into a house
like that, for although he was the landlord lie was
not the occupier, and was likely to get himself
into trouble, especially since he had developed
kleptomania and marched off with people’s table-
cloths. Hochblatter appealed to the lawyer to flat-
ten out this presumptuous Irishman, but the shark
said he was afraid Michael Mulligan was right,
and counselled retirement. Theyretired according-
ly, and entered an action against Mulligan for the
value of the broken furniture. But the lodgers
looked coldly on Hochblatter for his treatment of
the consumptive, and so many of them were
suborned by Mulligan to swear that they had seen
Mulligan buy the furniture off Hochblatter, and
pay for it and get an unstamped receipt, that the
case fell through with costs.
Hochblatter came up before the magistrate on
the day after the Mafeking holiday, to answer the
charge of breaking into Mulligan’s rooms and
stealing Mulligan’s table-cloth. The table-cloth
was fully identified. Mulligan got into the box
with a Union Jack pinned on his coat, and con-
trived to state incidentally that he was an ex-
soldier of the British army, which created a favor-
able impression at once. Hochblatter’s confused
statement about how he found the table-cloth
hanging on to his coat when he got home was un-
convincing. Then he unguardedly admitted hav-
ing been born at Amsterdam, and the court gave
him three months without any more ado.
HERMES.
The Wail of the Waiter.
(A Tavern Catch.)
All day long, at Scott’s or Menzies’, I await the gorging
crowd,
Panting, penned within a pantry, with the blowflies
humming loud.
There at seven in the morning do I count my daily cash,
While the home-returning reveller calls for “ soda and a
dash.”
And the weary hansom-cabbies set the blinking squatters
down,
Who, all night, in savage freedom, have been “ knocking
round the town.”
Soon the breakfast gong resounding bids the festive meal
begin,
And, with appetites like demons, come the gentle public
in.
“Toast and butter!” “Eggs and coffee 1" “Waiter
Mutton chops for four ! ”
“ Flatheads ! ” “ Ham ! ” “Beef! ” “ Where’s the
mustard ? ” “ Steak and onions ! ” “ Shut the door 1 ”
Here sits Bandicoot, the broker, eating in a desp’rate
hurry,
Scowling at his left-hand neighbor—Cornstalk from the
Upper Murray,
Who with brandy-nose empurpled, and with blue lips

The Ides of Morn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A solitary horseman swung at a canter along the
white road. Rather than face a four hours’ jour-
ney in the crawling train which crept laboriously
up-country some 30 miles every afternoon and
dawdled back the following morning, he had
chosen to ride to his destination. Ten years of
absence had dulled his recollection of the misery
of a typical nor’wester, or he might have hesitated
before deliberately facing the hot blast which tore
down the valley. Overhead the sky ached clear
and blue, and the sun shone brightly. The yellow
stubble-fields swept away on either hand to the
yellow-clad tussocked' foot-hills, which in turn
shaded to the yellow-parched mountains. The
gorse and broom glowed in late patches, and their
black seeds crackled in the heat. A flaw of snow
on the distant hill-tops, and an occasional white-
washed farm-house, nestling in a clump of olive-
gums, alone broke the tawny monotony.
The wind had swept the road clear of dust and
had lodged it like driven snow at the foot of tele-
graph-poles and fencing-posts, dusting the hedges
into a mute hopeless grey. It sifted it in at every
chink and crevice, and lodged it in the ears and
eyes and nostrils of horse and rider with insistent
grittiness. But the discomfort was lessened, so far
as the rider was concerned, by his utter oblivious-
ness to his surroundings. Ten years before,
he had left his home, in a passion of boyish
misery, under the regulation circumstances —a
woman’s inability to appreciate his worth. During
the years of monotonous money-making which
followed, he learnt that his impossible She had
followed the proper precedent of the hopelessly
prosaic, and had married his rival. With the tense
earnestness of youth he had believed that he would
never get over his early disappointment, and that
the empty hunger of his heart would never be
appeased. But the flying years had taught him
many things, the fleeting nature of the emotions
among them, and it was without a trace of con-
sciousness and more in a spirit of experimental
curiosity that he set his face homeward. His case
was unique in one respect only : he had never for-
gotten the girl. He had cherished a judiciously
faded recollection of her, and had spent many a
happy spare hour in rebreaking his broken heart.
By some sin of omission “the other girl” had
never eventuated, and he had been left to harrow
his soul with the luxury of recollection. It was a
coincidence, he knew, that the news of the death
of her husband should have reached him shortly
before he decided to revisit the home of his boy-
hood. He was almost annoyed, indeed, at the
infinite possibilities which the situation at once
suggested to him, possibilities which disturbed the
even tenor of his self-consolatory cherishings, and
which necessitated the entire revision of his atti-
tude towards her. She had been left, he learned,
practically unprovided for, and he felt that it was
his obvious duty, by all the tenets of Bret Harte,
to go to her assistance, to regard her as one of the
“fatherless children and widows” of the Litany,
and to act the truly Scriptural character of—■
but he could not recall the Biblical counterpart
which he felt ought to be available. Jacob pro-
vided in a sense the years of waiting, David the
affection for another man’s wife, and the Prodigal
Son the return of the wanderer ; but all had reser-
vations.
It did not take him long to settle down, with
the adaptability to circumstances of the true
philosopher, into his new attitude. He was
speedily persuaded—by himself—that his idol, as
a young' and charming widow, was much more to
be desired than even the winsome girl who had
been the ideal of his early manhood. Although
he knew that ten years of married life must have
changed her, with the faithful inconsistency of
even a convalescent passion he failed to realise it,
and the picture which swam before his mental
vision was always that of the girl, not that of the
woman. On the lazy voyage out he had derived a
good deal of satisfaction from the position, which
he conceived to possess certain dramatic features.
He lived over the coming meeting, in anticipation,
many times. She would be surprised to see him,
of course, and a little reserved at first, for she
would not quite understand their new attitude
until he gave the cue. But when she learned that
it was for her sake that he had returned, the
realisation of his long years of fidelity would
touch her, and she would surrender to him with a
half-tearful reservation which his tenderness and
love would soon dispel. Thereafter, by all the ac-
cepted precedents of fiction, their future would be
one long heritage of colorless content. His brief
renewal of his acquaintance with his native land
had sharpened his retrospective faculty, and the
blurred image which he had so often recalled took
a more definite shape. The night before he had
left home, in a sudden access of pity she had
kissed him at the garden gate, and he now re-
called her—slight, willowy, with a glory of golden
hair and a depth of violet eyes.
The clatter of passing hoofs roused him to a
realisation that eight dusty miles lay between him
and his destination. As he spurred his horse he
noticed that a stream which he was crossing was
turgid and yellow. The phenomenon struck him
as peculiar until he remembered that it was in the
hot-wind season that the rivers of the district
flooded through the melting of the snows upon
the hills. He crossed the Koroiti at a swollen
ford, and left the ford for a dimly-remembered
bridle-track which brought him out upon the
banks of the Waikaio a mile below the township.
The river, which at ordinary times stole treacher-
ously along, a stone’s-throw wide, now surged by
in a torrent of seething discolored water, half-a-
mile across. The sullen stream tugged and
snarled at the shingle banks and gulped hungrily
at the clods which fell away. Sheaves of yellow
grain swept past. A dead sheep circled irreso-
lutely into a backwater at his feet, hung unde-
cidedly there for a few seconds, and then
was seized and shot seaward once again by
the angry current. Logs, fences, saplings—all
the flotsam and jetsam of a sudden rising of the
waters —whirled past with dizzy reiteration. He
had seen it all before, long ago, and he felt that it
was but another bizarre variation of the theme of
reminiscence which he had been practising for
some days. Nevertheless, it irritated him. He
had a conservative dislike for anomaly. The
proper accompaniment for the flooded water he
conceived to be rain and storm and sullen skies.
The pitiless sunshine and the brilliant, cloudless
sky seemed in ill-accord with the wicked waters and
the parching wind. It struck a vage formless note
of admonition, which jarred. As he moved up the
river bank, one by one familiar objects, long for-
gotten, stole upon him. There was much that
was new—the railway had come during his ab-
sence—but there was still sufficient of the old days
to stir a vague ache. The township stood upon a
river flat, near to the fertile foot-hills responsible
for its existence, but bare itself of vegetation. It
looked bleak and unattractive enough in the glare
of the afternoon as he approached. His head
ached with the heat, the flying grit stung his
face, and the dust parched his throat and lay in
streaks upon his perspiring'cheeks. His eyes and
temples pulsed painfully. The thought sprang
almost to utterance on his lips: “Good God,
fancy living here ! ” The full misery of it smote
him with a physical pain.
He had intended to seek lodgment at a cousin’s
house, but in his present frame of mind he re-
volted at the restrictions of social domesticity,
and reined up his tired horse in front of the
Waikaio Hotel, an unpretentious wooden strue-
ture of one storey, which abutted on the stony
station-yard, and which was unfamiliar to him.
The township seemed deserted. A couple of
swaggers seated upon a bench under the hotel
verandah eyed him with almost insulting indiffer-
ence. He hitched his horse to a post and engaged
a room. It was cooler inside, and after a lavish
wash he hastened to claim a meal. As he entered
the bar-parlor a slatternly-dressed woman, with
down-at-heel shoes and a shawl thrown over her
head for covering, left the bar-door carrying a
billy of beer. A small boy in an almost disreput-
able state of tatters clung to her skirts. He had
but a momentary glimpse of her, but something
even in the unfamiliar carriage struck him with a
feeling akin almost to dismay. A brief interro-
gation of the landlord .confirmed his half-con-
jecture, and his dinner turned to “ dead sea fruit ”
upon his lips.
It was only after the solace of a pipe and a good
“ think ” that his loyalty laid to his charge the
continuance of his mission. He remembered that
the little meannesses of poverty can wilt the
beauty of any woman, and that it is hard to be
neat and tidy and self-respecting in the sordid
surroundings of indigent widowhood. He had
intended to surprise her, but after his newest ex-
perience he thought it better to warn her of his
coming.
The wind bad fallen when he knocked at her
door, and the night was cool and calm. She came
out to greet him. She was tidily dressed now,
and he could trace in the somewhat worn features
of the woman before him some recollection of the
girl he had loved. He did not enter the house,
but took her hands unresistedly in his and told
her plainly and straightforwardly what his errand
was. The meeting was not what he had so often
pictured, and it smote home to him irresistibly
that perhaps he had, after all, only been in love
with being in love, and that the cherishing of a

hand-clasp left her standing in the doorway. As
he looked back she was almost beautiful in the
radiance of a rising moon, and something of the
old affection yearned in him and tugged at him to
return and claim her at once. He should have
gone back and kissed her then. He did not. For
an hour he paced the roadway near at hand,
The eucalyptus, tremulously weary, rustled above
him. Bubbles of lurid lightning burst on the
grey horizon, and the vivid stars hung over-
head in a velvet sky. It was so quiet, so
restful, so peaceful, that it seemed to him that he
could do nothing better and nothing nobler than
win this woman and settle down once more in his
old home.
The morning dawned bright and sunny. He
, was bi waking and had to hurry through his
breakfast. Outside in the station-yard all was
bustle. There was a clanging of bells, a shriek of
en « lae whistles and much bumping and josthng
aad fussing as the busy little locomotive snuffled
about on the interminable shunting operations of
country sidings. There was a frowsy and un-
kempt look about the waitress who hung over the
ha>ole, and the hideous stolidity and mere physi-
calness of the men who moved about the station
and loitered near the hotel appalled him. Ihe
gum-trees, beneath which he had paced the night
before, were lean and patchy in the daylight, and
the dry, parched, sterile flat, and stubbled foot-
bills, uncompromisingly uninteresting. He ex-
Perienced a quick revulsion of feelmg. Things
looked so different in the day-time, and he could
not nd hl ? mind . of the of a slatternly
Wol l nan with a billy of beer, and an unkempt
urchin at her heels.
As the tram shuffled noisily out of the station
the sunshine was creeping down the hillsides. The
level beams struck upon an untidy lean-to with
pitiless severity. A small boy with a dirty face
) vas vigorously banging an empty kerosene-tin
in the section at the back, in the intervals
of . biting huge semi-circles out of a substantial
sllce of bread-and-dripping and chasing a trio of
disreputably lean fowls which had escaped from a
shelter improvised from rusty sheet-iron and wire-
netting. At the doorway a woman was stationed
w i fc h a wa sh-tub. Her arms were bare to the
elbows. She wore no collar, and the two upper-
most buttons of her bodice were missing. One or
two w i s P s of hair had worked down across her hot,
damp forehead. She did not look up as the*train
passed, but as it rounded the curve a hundred
y ar ds away she shaded her eyes with her hand and
l?°ked out across the bare plain, the glittering
nver » an d the shadowed hills to where the sun
was shining, as she had looked daily, it seemed to
ber ’ f° r almost a lifetime. And a man upon the
Platform saw and noted it.
HUBERT MITCHELL.
The usual crop of fool-wagers on U.S.
Presidential election is already turning up. One
crank supporting Bryan is to engage in a butting-
match with a full-grown ram if his man loses, the
opponent being pledged to twist the tail of a mule
every day for three weeks, or till death if the mule
kicks sooner. One Cartwright will support the
mother-m-law of neighbor Jones if M'Kinley
wins, Jones’s risk being to tend Cartwright’s
heating-apparatus all through the winter. Other
forfeits are to wheel a barrow from N’York to
’Frisco; to shave one side (only) of the face for
four years ; to wear all clothing wrong-side-before
during Bryan’s term of office ; and to eat crow-pie
every day for breakfast for the same period.

cream, consequently the butter has a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process ; but it is satisfactory
to know that these are getting less daily. The difference in price between Pasteurised, beautifully-flavored butter and the
ordinary make is only per lb. It would pay consumers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send your Orders to US, and we will deliver it at your doors.
Good butter such as we have described is appreciated always, whereas at afternoon teas or for ordinary use bad butter is dear
at any price. The famous “ Berry ” brand Pasteurised butter can be,obtained only from this Company.
H. PATESON, Manager,
Harbor-street, Sydney.
